# TIMBERWOOD HOLLOW

*A Novel*

## PART 1: REKINDLED

---

# Chapter 1: The Spark That Hides

Cody Guiles wakes up feeling like crap. The sun sneaks through his dollar-store curtain like an unwelcome guest. He stays flat on his back for an extra second, just long enough to hate it, before he forces himself to sit up. His mattress makes that sad squeaky sound old beds make. The sheets are twisted from how he sleeps these days: one knee bent up, one shoulder dug in, like his body's trying to curl around something that isn't there anymore. Sitting on the edge of his bed, he feels small and wound-up tight, like one of those toy cars you pull back before letting go. His brown hair is shaggy and messy, and those eyes, the ones his mom used to say had "little bits of sunshine" in them, just look tired now. He wipes his face with his palm and stares at the wall for a second before forcing himself to stand.

He stares into the dusty floor mirror. The guy looking back isn't really him anymore. Just some robot going through the motions to keep everyone else happy. He's built solid enough, with decent shoulders that used to mean something when he wore pads. Captain, junior year. The guy who saw plays before they happened, who read defenses like other people read street signs, who made the calls that mattered. State semifinals. Scholarship talk. A future that actually looked like something.

Then came senior year. One bad moment. One swing he couldn't take back. And just like that, the guy in the mirror became someone else.

His eyes remind him of those birthday candles that won't stay lit no matter how many times you try. His hair's grown out too long, plastered to his forehead from sleep like a bad haircut in a school photo. His skin's about as colorful as printer paper, lips squeezed tight, jaw locked like he's waiting for life to punch him in the face again. Standing there, he notices how his neck, his shoulders, his hands are all just there. Present but hollow, like props in a play about someone else's life. The crappy light in his room highlights all the stuff he hates seeing: the bags under his eyes, the way he can't even fake a real smile anymore, how his mouth stays in that stubborn line that says "I'm fine" when he's anything but. Looking at himself is like seeing a stranger wearing his skin. Some dude who used to be a real person with dreams and stuff, not just a walking collection of ways to get through the day. Whatever spark he had is just a story he tells himself now, like Santa Claus or the idea that things might actually get better someday.

He grabs his clothes off the floor like he's picking up after a little kid. His T-shirt's got that soft feeling from being washed a million times, wrinkled where he dropped it yesterday. His pants are those jean-looking things with elastic ankles that gave up being blue a long time ago. The socks on his nightstand are just sad. Gray, with holes starting at the toes like they're trying to escape. He yanks the shirt over his head, wiggles into the pants, and pulls on the socks, smoothing them like he's petting a stray cat. His sneakers used to be white, maybe, but now they're the color of dirty dishwater. He ties them with the muscle memory of someone who's done it half-asleep a thousand times. Standing up, he checks himself in the mirror that hasn't been cleaned since Obama was president. Just another guy going through the motions because the world doesn't care if he wants to or not.

He opens his bedroom door to what looks like a trash tornado hit. Garbage bags were piled up against the walls like they were trying to hold the place together, and there was so much junk on the floor you couldn't even tell what color the carpet was supposed to be. His dad was passed out in the old recliner again, looking like a scarecrow that someone had knocked over and forgotten about. His shirt was all yellow around the neck, with food stains making a connect-the-dots puzzle down the front, and dark patches under the arms. His hair was stuck to his head like he'd gone swimming in cooking oil, his beard was growing in every direction except the right one, and his face was shiny with sweat. Empty bourbon bottles were scattered around his feet like bowling pins after a strike, and the whole place smelled like a mix of booze, B.O., and yesterday's fast food.

Cody didn't look at him. Didn't let himself.

Because when he did, something hot flickered in his chest. Not sadness. Something sharper. His hands wanted to curl into fists. His teeth pressed together until they ached. Some part of him, deep down where he kept the ugly things, wanted to grab one of those bottles and...

He killed it. Shoved it down where it couldn't breathe. Felt it settle somewhere behind his ribs, banked but not gone.

*Not today. Not ever.*

He stepped out, dodging boxes and piles of junk like he was playing a video game on expert mode. The place stank. That mix of wet basement, forgotten leftovers, and enough booze to make your eyes water. The kitchen counter was buried under dishes and takeout boxes stacked higher than a Jenga tower about to fall. He grabbed a breakfast bar from a torn-up box that looked like it had survived a flood, then fished out a water bottle that wasn't exactly cold from the sea of ketchup packets and coffee mugs with science experiments growing inside them. He headed for the door, his hand reaching for the knob like it was the finish line in a race he ran every morning.

Outside, it was freezing cold, like opening a freezer door right in your face. His crappy jacket might as well have been made of paper, and his jeans were so dirty they could practically stand up on their own. Every breath made a little cloud that disappeared in a blink. He walked to the bus stop with his hands stuffed in his pockets, watching the ground so he wouldn't trip on the busted-up sidewalk. The streetlights made that annoying buzzing sound, and half of them didn't even work right, making the street look like something out of a cheap horror movie. The bus stop sign was crooked, all beat-up looking, with a schedule you couldn't even read anymore because of all the rain.

Bus number 55 would haul him to community college. Just a bunch of ugly brick buildings where dreams went to die. He was the king of C-minus, doing just enough work not to fail, but never anything special. Some vague liberal arts degree that wouldn't get him anywhere. No real plans, just the same boring routine: get up, deal with the mess at home, ride the bus, sit through class. Rinse and repeat, until life threw another curveball his way. The bus showed up making that dying-dinosaur noise buses always make. Cody got on, swiped his pass at the little machine that never works right the first time, and found a spot in the back where nobody would bother him.

The bus was half-full. His brain did that thing it always did. Cataloged without permission. The woman in scrubs, third row, would get off at Mercy General. The kid with the backpack covered in anime pins was community college, same as him, but freshman based on the lanyard still around his neck. Nobody kept those past October. The old guy clutching the grocery bag would ride two more stops, then the senior center. The couple near the front were fighting about something, body language all wrong. Her arms crossed, his shoulders hunched forward. They wouldn't make it to Christmas.

He used to do this with defenses. Read the formation, predict the blitz, know where the hole would open before the ball was snapped. Captain of the team. The guy coaches trusted with the audibles. A lifetime ago. Now he just read bus passengers like it mattered.

Like anything mattered.

The doors shut with that snake-hiss sound, and the whole bus shook like it was cold. Cody pressed his head against the window, not because he was sad or happy or anything. He just liked how the buzzing traveled from the glass into his bones. Like one of those electric toothbrushes, but for his whole head. It reminded him he wasn't completely dead inside yet. He closed his eyes and everything turned into background noise, like when you're underwater in a swimming pool and can hear people talking but can't make out the words. His brain switched back on when the bus started slowing down like it was dragging its feet. Some garbled robot-voice shouted his stop, and he peeled his eyes open to see those ugly brick boxes they called a college staring back at him. He got up like a puppet with half its strings cut, swaying as the bus wheezed to a stop. Great. Another day of showing up just enough to not totally fail, but not enough to actually matter to anyone, including himself.

---


# Chapter 2: C-Minus

The professor droned on about something at the front of the room, voice flat as a dead battery. Some theory tied to some algorithm Cody already knew he'd never use. He copied a few lines from the board anyway, pen moving on autopilot, hand making shapes his brain didn't bother keeping. This was his life now. Same crap, different day, until it all blurred together.

He'd thought college might change things. New place. New faces. Maybe one moment that didn't feel like a rerun. But it turned out that without any direction, a new building was just a different wall to smack into. His life had always been like that. Every time something looked like it might finally be better, a brick wall went up and he'd hit it face-first. Same result every time.

His pen paused over the page as his brain drifted where it always drifted when he sat still too long. Back to that night. High school. State championships. The roar of the crowd, the white glare of stadium lights, the sharp smell of sweat and turf. One hard shove from the other player, one taunt that hit too close to home, and something in him snapped.

He remembered the heat of it. How fast it moved from his chest to his fists. How everything went red and quiet at the same time, like the world had narrowed down to just him and that kid's face. All the crap he'd been carrying around his whole life came pouring out through his fists. By the time coaches dragged him off, the other kid was on the ground and everyone looked at Cody like he was a monster.

The worst part wasn't the suspension. Wasn't losing his spot on the team or watching the scholarship talks dry up overnight. The worst part was how good it had felt. For about three seconds, before the horror kicked in, he'd felt more alive than he had in years.

That scared him more than anything.

Since then, the town had shut its doors. Old teammates stopped answering messages. Friends crossed the street when they saw him coming. Parents lowered their voices when he passed. Now he sat alone near the middle of a lecture hall, staring through notes he didn't care about. Half thinking of dropping this semester and disappearing somewhere else. But the notebook in front of him and the tuition tied to it were paid for with his own money. Nights and weekends at the burger place down the street, wiping grease off counters until morning. Same old routine. The only thing that still belonged to him.

Movement caught his eye. Third row, blue hoodie. The guy had been fidgeting the whole lecture, but now his hand dipped below the desk in a rhythm. Phone check. Glance at board. Phone check. Scribble. The reflection off his glasses showed the screen glow every time he looked down. Cody's pattern brain catalogued it without trying. The nervous bounce of the leg. The way he angled his body to block the girl next to him from seeing. The too-fast scribbling that didn't match the professor's pace. Cheating. Obvious if you knew what to look for.

*Not my problem.*

He went back to his notes.

Cody's phone buzzed against the desk. He glanced down, glad for the excuse to look away from the board. A text from his manager.

Can you cover tonight's shift

He didn't hesitate.

Sure. I got it.

Anything to keep him away from that apartment for a few more hours.

He slid the phone back into his pocket and forced his eyes to the front again. The professor was still going on about variables and equations, same flat voice from the start of class. The guy moved through the slides like a vending machine dispensing snacks. Push button, get lecture. No soul required. Cody watched him and saw another person who'd stopped trying a long time ago. Someone who just showed up, talked at a room of half-asleep students, collected a paycheck, and went home. Another life stuck in a rut.

"Make sure to read through the chapter on advanced equations before next class," the professor said at last.

Chairs scraped back. People started talking, some complaining, some already laughing about something else. Cody closed his notebook. He knew he'd forget most of what he'd written by tomorrow. He wasn't stupid. The information came easy enough when he tried. He just couldn't keep caring. Not when he couldn't see the point.

By lunchtime, he drifted into the campus main hall. The student lounge spread out under fluorescent lights that made everyone look a little sick. Scattered tables. Old couches sagging in the middle like they'd given up. Vending machines humming against the far wall. A TV in the corner mumbled the news, anchors talking about politics and weather in the same bored tone. Nearby, a cluster of students whispered and laughed about whatever students laugh about, voices going up and down. The air smelled like cheap pizza, burnt coffee, and reheated fries. Everything blended together into background noise. Another boring piece of a boring day. Cody found a table near the window and sat with his back to the wall. Old habit. He could see the whole room from here. His brain did its thing. The guy by the vending machine, hoodie up, shoulders hunched. Trying to decide if he could afford both a soda and chips. He'd go chips. More food for the money. The two girls on the couch were studying together, but one of them kept checking her phone and the other one had noticed. That friendship had maybe a month left. The kid at the next table over had his textbook open but hadn't turned a page in ten minutes. He was going to fail whatever test was coming.

Cody used to do this on the field. Read the defense before the snap. Know which linebacker was bluffing, which safety was going to bite on the fake. Patterns. People were just patterns if you watched long enough. Now he watched college kids pick at their lunches and wondered why he bothered. He poked at his food and scrolled through his phone, killing time until his next class.

---

On his way out of the lounge, he passed the lecture hall from this morning. A cluster of students stood outside, voices low but urgent. He recognized the girl who'd been sitting next to blue hoodie guy. She was crying.

"I didn't cheat," she kept saying. "I don't even have my phone. Check my bag. Check anything."

The professor stood with his arms crossed, unmoved. "The proctor saw suspicious activity in that area. You were the closest."

Blue hoodie was nowhere to be seen. Probably halfway across campus by now.

Cody should keep walking. This wasn't his fight. Getting involved never ended well for him.

His feet stopped anyway.

"It wasn't her."

Everyone turned. The professor raised an eyebrow. The girl blinked at him through wet lashes.

"Guy in the blue hoodie," Cody said. "Third row, two seats to her left. He had his phone under the desk the whole time. You could see the screen reflected in his glasses."

Silence. The professor's expression flickered. Doubt, then irritation.

"And you are?"

"Nobody." Cody shrugged. "But I was in that class. And I know what I saw."

The professor studied him for a long moment, then turned back to the girl. "We'll... look into it."

Cody didn't wait for thanks. He was already walking.

Behind him, he heard the girl's voice. "Thank you." Small. Surprised.

He didn't turn around.

By the time he got to the library that afternoon, he'd already gotten three dirty looks from people he didn't recognize. Blue hoodie's friends, probably. Word traveled fast in a small school.

*Do the right thing,* he thought. *Watch it blow up in your face. Same story, different day.*

---

Then something popped into his head. Clear as a bell.

*Study for midterms next week.*

He froze for a second. He had never planned on doing that. Cram the night before, maybe. Cheat off old notes online, sure. But actually study, days ahead? That wasn't him.

It didn't even feel like his own thought.

Cody set his phone down and stared at the table. He'd had random thoughts before. Everyone did. But this one sat different. Heavier. Like someone had dropped a rock in his skull and it was just sitting there, waiting.

*Study for midterms.*

He tried to shake it off. Went back to scrolling. But every few seconds, the thought pushed back. Insistent. Patient.

*Fine,* he thought. *You want to play this game? Let's see where it goes.*

It wasn't belief. It was curiosity. The same part of his brain that read defenses wanted to know what happened if he followed the weird impulse instead of ignoring it.

By the time his afternoon classes ended, the thought had dug in deep. He had a few hours before his shift at the burger place. Instead of killing time like usual, his feet carried him toward the library.

The campus library was big enough to hold everything the school offered, but small enough that no one ever got lost in it. It smelled like every library he'd ever been in. Paper and dust and something dry that stuck to the back of his throat. Rows of shelves lined up in neat aisles. A computer center near the entrance with old towers humming like a refrigerator on its last legs. A table of printers at the far wall spitting out papers for people who'd waited until the last minute. Cody checked his phone to see what he needed to review. As he scrolled through his notes app, the screen flickered. Went gray for a second, then snapped back.

A new line sat at the top of his notes.

*Study: The Harrowfield Incident, primary accounts and analysis.*

He blinked. He had never typed that. He'd never even heard of it. He scrolled up and down, checked the edit history, but everything looked normal. Like the line had always been there.

His pattern brain kicked in. This wasn't a glitch. Glitches didn't add complete sentences with proper punctuation. Glitches gave you gibberish or crashed the app. This was something else.

He should be freaked out. He should put the phone down and walk away and pretend this never happened.

Instead, he felt that same curiosity from before. The itch to see what happened next.

*Alright,* he thought. *Let's see what you've got.*

He walked to the front desk. The librarian on duty was a skinny nineteen-year-old with pale skin and tired eyes, a campus job lanyard hanging from their neck. Cody asked if they had anything on the Harrowfield Incident. Maybe a history text or some collection of accounts.

The librarian stared at him for a second, face blank, like the words had to travel a long way to reach them. Another zombie shuffling through the campus routine.

"I dunno," they said at last. "If we have something like that, it would be in the back corner. No one really goes there. You can check."

Cody held their gaze for a second. The kid already looked beat and he didn't feel like pushing. Not worth the effort. He gave a short nod, turned away from the desk, and headed into the library.

He passed the well-lit sections first, the shelves with colorful textbooks and new releases on display. Step by step, the lights got dimmer and the voices from the entrance faded out. By the time he reached the far corner that nobody used, the air felt heavier. Quieter. Cody turned down the narrow aisle, lined with tall shelves that looked dustier than everything else, and started looking. He ran a finger along the spines, squinting to make out the faded lettering. Cobwebs hung in the upper corners like nobody had cleaned back here in years. The air smelled stale. Dusty. Like the back of a closet that hadn't been opened since someone died.

*History of Regional Rail Systems.*
*Agricultural Policies, 1920 to 1945.*
*Collected Essays on Civic Reform.*

Nothing about Harrowfield. Nothing about any incident.

He scanned the section again, slower this time, then stood up straight with a sigh. Typical.

*Gotta do this the hard way, Guiles.*

The thought came with a dry edge he barely recognized as his own. He stepped back from the shelf, letting his eyes drift across the aisle to the next row, where the lights were even dimmer. Something pulled at him from that direction. Not a voice, but close.

Cody shifted his bag on his shoulder and moved toward the next shelf, ready to start over.

The same feeling shoved its way back into his head. Small, but heavy.

*Check again.*

It wasn't really words. More like a push that turned his shoulders before he decided to move. Cody sighed and stepped back to the shelf he'd already looked through. He let his eyes move from left to right, slower this time. Then he saw it.

*The Harrowfield Incident.*

The title sat there in dull gold letters on a dark spine, about halfway down.

His pattern brain flagged it immediately. He'd looked at this exact spot thirty seconds ago. The book hadn't been there. He was sure of it. He'd run his finger across those spines, read every title. This one would have jumped out.

But here it was. Like it had been waiting for him to pay attention.

Cody stood very still for a moment. The logical part of his brain, the part that had gotten him through eighteen years of a world that made sense, wanted to explain this away. Bad lighting. Tired eyes. He'd just missed it the first time.

The other part of his brain, the part that read patterns and predicted plays and knew when something was about to break open, that part said: *This is real. Pay attention.*

He pulled the book out. Heavier than he expected, cloth cover rough under his fingers, dust floating off the edges. He carried it to an empty desk near the window, dropped his bag on the chair next to him, and sat down. He took out his laptop, opened a blank document for notes, then flipped to the first chapter. The pages were yellow at the corners, print a little faded, but still readable. He started in.

A rural township, population listed, coordinates given. Dates. A stiff description of something that had happened in a place he'd never heard of. At first it read like every boring history book he'd ever cracked open. But the deeper he went, page after page, the more it started to get under his skin. The way the writer described the streets. The layout of the town. The way people went about their days.

Something in the back of his head stirred.

Cody frowned, leaned closer to the page, and turned to the next chapter.

And then, something happened.

---


# Chapter 3: A White Light

Cody turned the page. For a second his brain checked out, eyes sliding over the words without taking anything in. Then one word jumped out from the middle of the paragraph.

*Timberwood.*

His pulse kicked. The word wasn't bolded or anything, just regular print, but something in him reacted like someone had yelled it in his ear. Adrenaline hit him like a shot of espresso. His chest got tight, not painful, just this pressure that made it hard to breathe. The hairs on his arms stood up. His eyes went wide and for a second, just a second, something woke up inside him.

Hope.

Or wanting.

Some feeling so old and buried he couldn't even name it.

The word brought a flash with it. Not a memory he recognized, but a picture that popped behind his eyes like a camera flash. Tall, dark trees disappearing into morning fog. A two-lane road cutting between them. A green highway sign, just clear enough to read.

*Welcome to Timberwood.*

Then it was gone. The picture, the feeling, cut off like someone hit a switch.

Why that name? He didn't know anyone from Timberwood. He'd never been there, far as he knew. But the word stuck in his head like a fishhook.

He frowned, turned the page, and the world started going weird. Slow at first. The hum of computers got quieter, like someone was turning down the volume. The sound of feet on carpet faded out, like hearing things underwater. A thin ringing started in his ears, barely there. The colors around him started getting fuzzy. The desk, the shelves, even his own hands looked like they were losing their edges. His pattern brain kicked in, trying to make sense of it. Stroke? Seizure? Some kind of episode? He ran through the symptoms he knew. Vision changes. Hearing changes. Tingling. But this didn't feel medical. It felt like the world was coming apart at the seams. Then everything hit at once.

Every emotion he'd ever felt slammed together like cars in a pileup. Happy, sad, scared, pissed off, all of it mashed together into one big mess. His whole body locked up. Felt like his brain was trying to run ten programs at the same time and crashing hard. It all squeezed down into this tiny bright spot right in front of his eyes, like staring at a light bulb too long. He smelled something sharp and clean, like the air right after lightning hits.

White.

The words on the page disappeared. The ink just melted away, leaving blank paper. Then the paper was gone too. The page, the desk, the library, all of it wiped out like someone had taken an eraser to the whole world.

Part of him wanted to fight it. Dig in. Hold onto something, anything, that made sense.

But another part, the part that had been sitting on the edge of his bed every morning wondering why he bothered getting up, that part leaned into the pull.

*Anywhere but here.*

He stopped fighting.

For a second he couldn't tell if he was falling or floating. No up, no down, no sound. Just the name Timberwood echoing through all that white nothing.

Cody floated in empty white. Like being inside a blank piece of paper. No horizon, no shadows, just bright in every direction. His feet drifted down, slow and steady, until they hit something that felt like ground. He could feel pressure under his shoes, but when he looked down there was nothing there. No floor. Just more of that bleached-out white everywhere. The white started changing. First it just got fuzzy at the edges of his vision, like a TV going out of focus. Then it got thicker, shifting and warping faster, until it felt like the whole place was breathing around him. His eyes watered. Everything blurred. He squinted against the brightness. The air got heavy. Pressed down on his chest and shoulders like a weight. Each breath came harder than the one before. A thought crept in, small and scared. His heart pounded when it hit him.

*Am I dying?*

*Am I already dead?*

The second he thought it, the white answered.

It rushed at him from every direction, collapsing inward. In one silent second, all of it slammed into his chest. He felt it go through his ribs and into his lungs, a blast of cold that knocked the air right out of him.

Everything went black.

No light. No up or down. No floor. No feeling in his hands. Just thick, perfect nothing, like someone had switched off the whole idea of space.

---


# Chapter 4: Welcome to Timberwood

Black. Nothing but black. The kind of dark where you can't tell if your eyes are open or shut.

Then a feeling. Fuzzy at first, like waking up after passing out. Something solid under him. His fingers twitched and touched something soft. Grass. Blades bending under his hand, tickling his skin. His palm hit something rougher. Thin, sharp little things.

Pine needles.

*What the hell am I touching?*

That thought helped. Gave him something to grab onto. He moved his fingers again and felt more of those little sharp bits. Pine needles everywhere. Like someone had dumped a Christmas tree on him.

Then a smell. Weak at first, like his nose was stuffed with cotton. Pine, barely there. It got stronger bit by bit, the way your nose clears when a cold finally lets up. That moment when you blow your nose and can actually smell again. Then it rushed in all at once.

Pine sap and fresh grass. Mixed together into something obvious.

*Woods. I'm in the woods.*

Sound came next.

At first just muffled garbage in his ears, like water stuck in there after swimming. Then it started clearing up. The noise broke apart into pieces.

Voices.

A few of them. Low, scattered. Words he couldn't make out. One sounded like a gasp. Another like a short laugh. Chatter mixing with bird calls from somewhere up in trees he couldn't see yet.

Then one voice cut through. Deeper. Rough.

"Alright everyone, step back, nothing to see here."

Clear. Close. The kind of voice that expected people to do what it said. Cody pulled in one more breath that tasted like pine and cold air.

Then he opened his eyes.

Blurry. Everything blurry.

Light and color smeared together above him, but he could make out trees. Tall pines reaching up, branches weaving together into a dark ceiling. Light came through in streaks, dust floating around in it.

Something moved between him and that light.

A dark shape leaned over him, blocking the brightness. His eyes tried to focus but couldn't get there. All he saw was a big silhouette, shoulders wide enough to block half the sky.

Then he found the eyes.

Not human. Two amber points, like someone had switched on a pair of gold headlights behind that dark face. His vision snapped into focus around them.

Dark fur framing that stare. Almost black but silver at the edges where the light caught it. A muzzle, long and solid. Ears pointed forward. The shape became a face he'd only ever seen in movies or nature documentaries. A wolf. But standing upright. Alert. Thinking.

A big paw reached toward him. No. A hand. Padded, tipped with claws, covered in dark gray fur.

Cody jerked back before he knew what he was doing, heart hammering against his ribs like it wanted out. Pine needles stabbed into his palms as he scrambled backward, breathing way too fast.

Someone behind the wolf gasped. Others muttered. As his eyes adjusted, the rest of the scene came into focus.

They were all staring at him.

His brain kicked into gear. Same thing it did on the bus, in the student lounge, on the field. Reading the room. Finding the threats. A tall giraffe stood farther back, long neck bent down, spots disappearing into a campus jacket. Body language said scared. She kept her distance, weight shifting like she was ready to bolt. Not a threat. Near her feet, a little mongoose hung onto a backpack strap, whiskers going crazy. Curious, not aggressive. Leaning forward, not back. Also not a threat. Some gazelles huddled together, ears flicking, eyes huge. Herd behavior. They'd run before they'd fight.

The badgers were different. A bunch of them, built like linebackers even just standing there. Arms crossed, weight forward. That was challenge posture. They were deciding whether he was prey.

*Those are the ones to watch.*

A couple more wolves behind the big one, younger, skinnier, wearing team shirts and sweats. Following the leader. They'd do whatever he did.

And in front of them all, the one who'd reached for him.

Up close, he was huge. Built like a refrigerator wearing a dark Timberwood Badgers jersey and jacket. Dark gray fur, thick around the neck and shoulders, silver streaks under his eyes and across his chest. Those amber eyes locked onto Cody. Steady. Not mean, but the kind of intense that made you want to look away. Too real.

But his paw was open, not fisted. His ears were forward, attentive, not flattened back in aggression. His body was positioned between Cody and the crowd. Not cornering him. Shielding him.

*He's not hunting. He's protecting.*

The realization cut through some of the panic.

The smell hit him full force now. Pine, trampled grass, something clean and warm underneath. Like a locker room after everyone's showered, but outside.

His mouth moved before his brain caught up.

"Stay back. Do not come closer!" Cody yelled, voice cracking through the trees louder than anything he'd said in years.

The yell hung there.

The big wolf didn't flinch. Didn't even blink.

He straightened up a little, ears tipping forward, paws held out where Cody could see them. When he talked, his voice was low and steady. Same gravel as before.

"Easy," he said. "I'm not going to touch you."

Cody sucked in a rough breath. His eyes bounced around the circle. The giraffe. The mongoose. The gazelles. The badgers. More wolves. All of them standing on two legs, wearing clothes, looking at him with the same mix of curiosity and nerves he felt in his own gut.

"What the hell," Cody whispered. Then louder. "You're talking. You're all talking. How is that even possible?"

A few students backed up. Someone muttered something about humans. Another voice said, "I told you it was a human."

The big wolf glanced over his shoulder at them, then back at Cody. One ear twitched.

"Yeah," he said. "I'm talking. Welcome to Timberwood Hollow, where the animals walk upright and the humans are the weird ones."

If that was a joke, his voice didn't show it. He stayed crouched, eyes level with Cody's. One paw still out, claws tucked.

"You're freaking out the locals almost as much as they're freaking you out," he added. "Keep shouting and security will decide you're a problem instead of someone who needs help."

Cody looked at the paw, then at the crowd behind it. His breathing was ragged, but he made himself talk.

"Am I the only human here?"

The wolf made a sound that might've been a laugh.

"You're the only one on the ground," he said. "You're not the first we've ever seen. Just the first I've seen in years."

He looked past Cody. Campus security was pushing through the crowd, radio crackling.

"Crowd's getting bigger and they're already jumpy," the wolf said. "Can you sit up? Can you walk?"

Cody ran the options. Stay here, get swarmed by security, end up in some kind of holding cell while they figured out what to do with him. Or go with the giant wolf who hadn't made a single aggressive move, whose body language said protector, whose voice said I've got this handled.

His pattern brain had already made the call. The wolf was the safest bet in this crowd.

He pushed himself up. Legs felt like jelly. The wolf moved closer, slow enough that Cody could track every inch.

"What am I supposed to do?" Cody muttered. "I move, they stare. I stay here, they stare more."

"First," the wolf said. "Breathe."

Cody dragged air into his lungs. Pine and dirt and that weird warm smell coming off the wolf.

"Second," the wolf went on, "we walk. Nice and calm. My dorm's close. Quiet. Private. You can ask questions there without half the freshman class filming you for their group chats."

He glanced toward the trees.

"Third, you tell me what you remember. Library, white light, whatever happened. Might help us figure out how to get you home."

"Home," Cody repeated. The word felt thin. Like it didn't mean much anymore. "Okay. Sure. But what about this crowd?"

The wolf stood up to his full height, turning so his body blocked Cody from the students. From down on the ground, Cody got a real look at how big he was. Shoulders that could fill a doorway. Jersey stretched tight over a chest built from years of hitting people for sport. When he spoke, his voice carried without him needing to yell.

"Alright," he said, sweeping his gaze around. "Show's over. Campus security has this. Get back to class before your professors start counting heads."

The crowd shifted. Some students hesitated, but security seemed happy to let the wolf run things. One of the officers, a stocky badger with a badge clipped to his belt, caught the wolf's eye. The wolf gave him a tiny nod. *I got this.*

The officer relaxed and turned away, waving a couple gawkers down the path.

But Cody's pattern brain caught something else. A second officer, a lean coyote, stood at the edge of the clearing with a radio to his muzzle. Not managing the crowd. Reporting. His eyes stayed on Cody the whole time he talked.

On a building past the trees, a security camera sat mounted under the eaves. Red light blinking.

The crowd broke up. People started talking again, quieter. They drifted back toward the main paths. A few kept glancing back, but nobody came closer. The wolf looked down at Cody.

"Walk beside me," he said, quiet. "Don't look back."

He held out his paw again.

Cody had made his choice the moment he'd read the wolf's body language. Protector, not predator. He grabbed the paw. The pads were rough but warm, the grip careful. The wolf pulled him up like he weighed nothing.

For a second, the wolf's ear twitched. His eyes flicked to where their hands met, then back to Cody's face. Something crossed his expression. Curiosity, maybe. But he didn't say anything.

They walked out of the circle together. The wolf kept an easy pace down a narrow path between the pines, away from the main trail. His tail hung low and relaxed, his big body blocking most of the stares. Cody glanced back once. The coyote officer was still there. Watching which path they took. After a few seconds, the wolf spoke.

"My name is Lukas," he said. "What's yours?"

"Cody," he answered. Voice still shaky. "Cody Guiles."

Lukas nodded once, like he was filing it away.

"Alright, Cody," he said. "Dorms are just past those oaks, third building on the left. We get you inside, you sit down, and then we figure out what the hell happened."

They kept walking. The sounds from the clearing faded behind them. The smell of pine and grass followed them out of the woods.

---


# Chapter 5: Borrowed Space

The late October air cut through Cody's thin jacket as they stepped onto the main path. Gray sky hung low, the kind that looked like it might rain but probably wouldn't. Wind sent leaves skittering across the brick walkway. The trees lining the quad had gone bare, branches sticking up like dead fingers, though a few stubborn oaks still held onto their brown leaves like they hadn't gotten the memo.

Lukas cut across the quad instead of taking the main paths, heading toward a line of old oaks and a narrower walkway. The big wolf moved at an easy pace, but Cody noticed how he kept himself between Cody and everyone else. A walking wall of fur and muscle in a green and black team jacket.

"So," Lukas asked, eyes forward, "what's the last thing you remember? Before you woke up in our woods."

Cody reached for the strap of his backpack, then remembered it was gone. Right. Library, then nothing. His shoulder felt wrong without it.

"I was in my college library," he said. His voice sounded too loud in the open air. "Studying for midterms. I blinked, everything went white, and then I was on the ground with a bunch of... people staring at me."

Anthro people. Wolves, foxes, something that had definitely been a raccoon in a hoodie. All standing up, all watching him like he was the weird one.

"You get used to the staring," Lukas said. His ear flicked, catching some sound behind them Cody couldn't hear. "White out and a hard landing. That tracks with the stories."

"Stories?" Cody stumbled on the word. "You're telling me this has happened before?"

"Not every week." Lukas gave him a sidelong glance, amber eyes calm. "But you're not the first human to show up somewhere you didn't mean to be. Just the first I've seen in person."

That should have helped. It didn't. If this was a known thing, then how many other people had gone missing from quiet corners of their lives and never made it back?

He shoved the thought down. *One thing at a time, Guiles.*

They passed under the oaks, and the temperature dropped in the shade. Dead leaves crunched under their feet, and the air smelled like rotting leaves and wet dirt. A breeze pushed Lukas's scent toward him again: pine, grass, something clean that might have been soap. Weirdly calming, like a locker room after a game. Sweat and detergent and routine. The campus spread out around them as they walked. Old stone buildings with ivy on the walls stood next to newer glass and steel ones. A clock tower stuck up in the distance, face just visible above the trees. Ahead, clusters of students crossed the main walks. All fur and feathers and shiny scales, their voices a low hum. Their breath made little clouds in the cold as they laughed and called to each other, bundled up in jackets and scarves.

Lukas changed their route again, picking a narrow sidewalk that went around a dorm instead of through the busy paths. Cody noticed it this time. The way the wolf's eyes checked every group they might have to pass. The way his shoulders tensed whenever someone looked too long.

At one point, Lukas steered them under an awning, then around a corner, avoiding a stretch of walkway that looked perfectly fine to Cody. He glanced back. A camera mounted on the building they'd just passed. Pointed right where they would have walked.

*Huh.*

"Your dorm is really out of the way, huh," Cody said, trying for dry and almost getting there.

"Not really." Lukas's tone stayed flat. "I just like the scenic route." Scenic route, sure. Scenic and conveniently empty.

Lukas's phone buzzed in his pocket. He pulled it out, glanced at the screen. His ears flattened for a moment before resetting. He read it for a second, then pocketed it without responding. Cody waited for an explanation. None came.

"Look," Cody muttered, "if walking with me is going to get you in trouble or something, you don't have to babysit. I can just, I don't know, hide in the trees until this blows over."

Lukas snorted. "You're not hiding in the trees." His tail flicked once. "You landed on a campus, not in a lab. You're a person, Cody, not an exhibit. I'm not letting anyone turn this into a project."

The word *lab* hooked into him. He almost tripped.

"I didn't say anything about a lab," Cody said, keeping his voice level.

"You didn't have to." Lukas's ears pressed back for a second, then relaxed. "You're jumpy, you don't know the rules here, and people in charge like to turn problems into paperwork. My job is to make sure you don't end up in anyone's file folder before you've had breakfast."

People in charge. Paperwork. Cody followed Lukas's glance and saw a tall brick building with wide glass doors and a metal sculpture of a double helix out front. It sat there against the gray sky like a threat, windows reflecting bare branches. A science building. Of course. He swallowed. "So your... dean, or whatever you have, they'd freak out about me?"

"Dean would want meetings," Lukas said carefully. "Committees. Words like *unprecedented* and *opportunity.*" His muzzle flattened on that last one. "You don't need any of that today. Today you need coffee and somewhere you can sit without an audience."

Lukas didn't dress it up or make promises. He simply refused to let it happen.

They rounded a corner and the dorm came into view. Five stories of old brick, older than some of the other buildings on campus. Narrow windows, the kind of ugly that said function over form. A row of evergreen bushes lined the foundation, half-hiding a side entrance. Somewhere above, a window was cracked open despite the cold, music drifting down too quiet to make out.

Lukas swiped a keycard, pushed the door open, and checked the hall before waving Cody in.

The sound changed first. Outside noise dropped to the quiet of old carpet and distant doors closing. The place smelled like dust, cleaner, and a mix of fur and laundry soap. Normal enough, if you didn't look too close at the bulletin board, where every smiling face on the flyers had some kind of muzzle.

Cody's heart rate finally started slowing down.

"Third floor," Lukas said. "No elevator, sorry." He pointed to an out-of-order sign on the elevator doors.

"Fine," Cody said. "I have legs."

He kept his eyes on his boots as they climbed, trying not to notice the four or five students who went quiet as he passed. He felt their stares anyway, prickling along his arms. Lukas blocked most of it, a solid wall on the outside of the stairs, making it hard for anyone to stop Cody or get a good look.

They reached a door with a beat-up Timberwood Badgers sticker stuck crooked near the handle. Lukas unlocked it and pushed it open.

The place was small but it worked. Open room that doubled as kitchen and living area. A counter ran along the left wall near the door, small sink at one end, dorm fridge underneath. Coffee maker sat on the counter next to a portable cooktop and microwave. Past that, the room opened into a living area: fold-out couch facing a TV on the opposite wall, maybe fifty inches, with an entertainment center underneath holding a couple gaming consoles. Desk to the left of the TV, gaming rig glowing on its surface, fan humming. Sturdy coffee table in the middle. Two doors at the far end of the right wall. Through the one that was half-open, Cody caught a glimpse of a bed, a shelf full of trophies, and a football on a stand. The bedroom. Down the hall came a burst of laughter, then a door slamming.

"Come in," Lukas said. "Door locks. No one barges in here but me."

*Safe.* The word bounced around Cody's head, looking for somewhere to stick.

He stepped inside. The carpet felt rough under his sneakers. He noticed the dirt on his jeans, the leaf bits stuck to his jacket from the forest floor. The thing was barely holding together anymore, threads coming loose at the cuffs, but it was all he had. A team jacket hung from a hook by the door. Jerseys were pinned to the wall above the couch. The whole place smelled like coffee grounds and something warm. The kind of smell that said someone actually lived here. He caught his reflection in the dark TV screen. Human face, too pale. Dark hair messed up and pushed back with his fingers. Eyes wide and shadowed from a semester of crappy sleep. He didn't belong here. He didn't really belong anywhere, but here he stuck out like a sore thumb.

"Sit," Lukas said. He pointed at the fold-out couch. "I'll make coffee."

The couch springs complained when Cody dropped onto it. A window on the far wall looked out over a slice of campus. Brick buildings, trees, a path where students laughed at something he couldn't hear. His pattern brain tried to read them through the glass. Tails and ears moved as they talked, adding layers to the body language he was used to. Posture still worked. The way someone leaned in or held back, the angle of their shoulders. That translated. But the ear positions, the tail movements, those were new variables. A fox's ears flattened as she talked to a friend. Fear? Annoyance? Submission? He couldn't tell yet.

*You'll figure it out,* he told himself. *You always do.*

He pressed his hands together between his knees until his knuckles hurt.

Behind him, Lukas moved like he'd done this a thousand times. Scoop grounds, fill water, flip a switch. The machine sputtered and hissed, filling the room with the smell of brewing coffee. Normal. Grounding. For a second, if Cody closed his eyes, he could almost pretend he was back at the community college caf√©, nursing a too-bitter cup between classes and pretending that counted as having a future. Except he'd never had a six-and-a-half-foot wolf a few feet away, talking quiet so he wouldn't fall apart.

"You said coffee exists here," Cody heard himself say. "Phones, too."

"Coffee, phones, bills, exams," Lukas said. "Congratulations, you crossed dimensions and still landed somewhere that assigns homework. Sugar?"

"Black is fine."

Lukas brought over a chipped Timberwood Badgers mug and put it in his hands. The heat hit his fingers first, then his wrists, chasing out the cold from the woods. He took a sip. Strong and a little bitter, but real. Warmth went down his throat and loosened the tight knot in his chest, just a little.

Lukas opened the mini-fridge and pulled out a foil-covered container. "Noodles," he said, peeling back the lid. Steam curled up, carrying ginger and soy. "They're safe. Should be, anyway."

Cody's stomach growled before he could stop it. He hadn't eaten since... he couldn't remember. The library? That felt like a different lifetime.

Lukas handed him a fork and slid the container across the coffee table. "Eat. You look like you're running on fumes."

Cody didn't argue. He scooped up a forkful, blew on it once, and shoved it in his mouth.

The first second was fine. Ginger, soy, a little sesame. Normal.

Then his sinuses caught fire.

"What the..." He choked, eyes watering, tongue screaming. Heat exploded across his mouth and down his throat like he'd swallowed a lit match. He grabbed for the coffee, took a gulp, and made it worse. "Oh god, oh fuck..."

Lukas's ears flattened. "Shit." He was already moving, yanking the fridge open again. A carton of milk hit the coffee table. "Drink. Now."

Cody grabbed it with shaking hands and chugged. The cold cut through some of the burn, but his lips still felt like they were melting. He gasped, wiped his streaming eyes, and stared at the noodles like they'd personally betrayed him.

"What the hell was that?"

Lukas picked up the container and sniffed. His muzzle wrinkled. "Benji." He said the name like a curse. "Habanero mash. He sneaks it into stuff when nobody's looking." He set the container down, shaking his head. "Those were supposed to be mine. Little bastard."

Cody took another long drink of milk, breathing through his nose. The fire was fading to a dull roar. "Who's Benji?"

"Hedgehog. Lives down the hall. Thinks he's funny." Lukas slid the poisoned noodles away from Cody like they might bite again. "He and Thorne, big bear on the team with me, they're floormates. Benji's good people, but his idea of a joke is chemical warfare."

Cody's tongue felt like sandpaper. "Remind me to thank him later."

"I'll let him know his prank landed on the interdimensional visitor instead of me." Lukas almost smiled. "He'll either be horrified or proud. Fifty-fifty."

Cody finished the milk and set the carton down, still breathing a little hard. His mouth would probably taste like burning for the next hour. Great first impression on his new reality.

"You didn't cause a scene, by the way," Lukas said, leaning against the counter. "Not any more than a dragon sighting would. People talk, then they move on."

"Pretty sure being the human who popped out of nowhere isn't something this place is going to forget overnight," Cody muttered.

"By lunch, the version where you breathed fire will be more popular than the truth." Lukas shrugged. "Rumors are loud, but they're not organized. What matters is who writes reports."

Cody looked up. "Reports."

"Dean. Department heads." Lukas's eyes flicked briefly toward the window, toward that science building, then back. "If they hear about you the wrong way, they'll want meetings and scans and big words. So I'd rather they hear nothing for a while."

There it was again. That same steady refusal, without the drama or horror stories.

"And you can stop that?" Cody asked.

"I can make it harder for them." Lukas folded his arms loosely. "Keep you off main routes, tell anyone who asks that you're a visiting student from out of town until we have a better plan. It buys time."

Cody studied him. His pattern brain was working again, cataloging details. The wolf's shoulders were still relaxed, but there was something tight around his eyes he hadn't noticed outside. The way his ears kept twitching toward the door, toward sounds in the hall. The slight tension in his jaw when he mentioned the dean. He was worried. Not about Cody. About what helping Cody might cost him. He realized this wasn't nothing for Lukas. Helping some random human who fell out of nowhere. Lying to whoever ran this place. Picking side paths and side doors and watching buildings with double helix sculptures. The guy was sticking his neck out, and Cody could see the weight of it now, sitting on those big shoulders like a bag of rocks.

"Why?" Cody asked. The word came out before he could stop it. "Why help me? You don't know me. I could be anybody."

Lukas was quiet for a second. His tail moved once, a slow sweep.

"Because you needed help," he said. "And I was there."

Cody waited for more. A tragic backstory, a complicated motive, something that explained why a stranger would stick his neck out. But that was it. That was the whole answer.

He didn't know what to do with that.

Cody felt how tired he was before he admitted it to himself. Coffee had taken the edge off the panic, but once the adrenaline faded, everything underneath was just exhausted. Bone tired. Waiting-at-the-bus-stop-at-dawn tired. He rubbed at the bridge of his nose. "So what now?" he asked. "Do we just... wait until tomorrow and see if the universe spits me back out?"

Lukas's ears tilted a little, like he was thinking through answers and throwing most of them out. "Tomorrow is a problem for tomorrow," he said. "Right now you need sleep."

"Sleep," Cody echoed. "In the magical furry universe where I maybe don't exist on paper."

"Paper can wait." Lukas glanced at the dark window, then back at Cody. "You look like you've been running on coffee and stubbornness for days already. Portal didn't help."

That was way too accurate.

Cody looked around the room. One bed, clearly taken. Desk, couch. No spare mattress appeared just because he needed one.

"Sounds like a plan," he said after a second. "Where am I sleeping?"

"Couch folds out," Lukas said. He nudged the ottoman with his paw and the top shifted, hinges clicking. "Sheets are inside. Blanket's clean, I promise." He nodded toward the two doors on the right wall. "Half bath on the left if you need it. Bedroom's on the right. Communal showers are down the hall if you want a real rinse, but that can wait till morning."

Cody pushed himself up from the couch. His legs felt like wet noodles. "Wake you if the walls start breathing, got it."

"Wake me if that happens for real," Lukas said. Something in his face softened. "Or if you need anything else."

Cody paused. "Walls breathe here?"

Lukas kept a straight face. "Only after my friend Benji's ramen. Otherwise they behave."

A tired laugh came out before Cody could stop it. Thinner than he wanted, but real.

Lukas clicked off the small kitchen light, leaving just the desk lamp and the dim light from the window. The room got smaller in a way that wasn't bad. Less space to get lost in.

"Get the bed set up," Lukas said. "I'm gonna hit the showers down the hall. I'll keep my door cracked when I get back. Holler if the furniture gets chatty."

Cody opened the ottoman. A folded sheet and a mismatched pillow sat inside, smelling like laundry soap and dust. He wrestled the couch into its new shape. The metal frame unfolded with the same complaint fold-out couches always make. Turns out interdimensional travel didn't improve couch design. He stripped off his jacket and draped it over the arm, then kicked off his shoes and socks. Cold air bit at his bare feet. Without his layers, he felt smaller. More like a kid crashing at a friend's place after missing the last bus. The pipes ticked in the walls. From down the hall, the faint hiss of water. Normal sounds.

He made the bed as best he could, corners more of a suggestion than anything, then sat down to test the mattress. Thin, but softer than his own at home, which had a permanent dip where his body had given up years ago.

The main door clicked open. Lukas walked in with a towel over his shoulder, fur a little damp. He wore shorts and a soft T-shirt now, smelling like soap, clean and sharp under the usual pine. He crossed to the bedroom door and pushed it open, then turned back.

He looked over Cody's handiwork on the fold-out. "It'll hold," he decided. "If it doesn't, you've got bigger problems than the dean."

Cody huffed. "Comforting."

"It's meant to be." Lukas's mouth twitched. He tipped his head toward the light switch. "I'm killing the lamp. You good?"

Cody nodded, then realized the wolf could probably see better in the dark than he could. "Yeah. I'm fine."

The lamp snapped off. For a second, Cody couldn't see anything. Then shapes came back: the rectangle of the window, the glow of the TV's standby light, Lukas's silhouette crossing toward the bedroom. The door pulled mostly closed, leaving just a crack of darkness. From past that crack, springs creaked as the wolf lay down. The room was quiet. Just the mini-fridge cycling, wind against the glass, distant door latches from somewhere else on the floor. No TV blaring, no sports channel filling the silence. No heavy, uneven snoring from behind a bedroom door, thick with bourbon and wasted time.

"Hey, uh," Cody said into the dark. His voice sounded smaller without anything to bounce off but the ceiling. "Lukas?"

Something moved in the bedroom. An ear probably perked up, though Cody couldn't see through the crack.

"Yeah?"

"Thanks." The word felt clumsy, too light for everything wrapped around it: the coffee, the shield in the crowd, the fact that nobody here had told him he was being dramatic or ungrateful. "For all of this."

A small pause. Cody could almost hear the wolf breathing.

"Anytime," Lukas said. No bravado, no joke on top.

Just that.

Past the cracked bedroom door, the mattress creaked. Blanket shifted. Then nothing. Cody lay back and stared at a ceiling he could barely see. He could make out shapes in the dark: the TV, the desk with its sleeping computer, the little green power light on the entertainment center. He let his mind wander because trying to pin it down never worked.

Could he just... live here? The thought showed up uninvited. This place had cell service and coffee makers and annoying friends with weaponized ramen. People threw around words like *dean* and *biology department*, and that should've scared him more, but it sat weird next to the image of his dad half-slid off the recliner, bourbon bottles on the carpet like they'd lost a fight.

Back there he was the kid from the wrong end of town with a bad incident in his past and a GPA that didn't impress anyone. A liberal arts major with a bus pass and a degree he could already feel sagging in his hands. Home was mildew and trash bags and stale booze, and the sound of his father cursing at late-night reruns.

Here he was the Human. Capital H. Everyone stared, but at least they saw him.

And Lukas. Patient, solid Lukas, who moved like a starting linebacker and watched people like they were plays on a field. Cody could admit, just to himself, that the wolf was stupidly good-looking: black fur, those amber eyes, that scent that meant woods and cool air and something steady underneath. The kind of guy who probably had everyone on campus orbiting him without even trying.

*Whole different planet, Guiles. Literally.*

His brain did that thing where it runs in circles until it gets tired of itself. Thoughts got blurry, words stopped making sense. The day's weight pushed him deeper into the thin mattress. Every muscle that had been braced for the next bad thing since he woke up in the woods finally started to let go. His breathing slowed down. Somewhere on the other side of the room, springs creaked once as Lukas shifted, then went quiet. Cody's eyes closed. Last thing he thought before he passed out: for the first time in forever, if he yelled in the dark, somebody might actually give a damn.

---


# Chapter 6: The Fox in the Lab

An alarm went off somewhere down the hall.

Cody opened his eyes to ceiling tiles he didn't recognize, dull morning light coming through a window he didn't remember falling asleep next to. For a long, foggy moment he had no idea where he was. Then it came back in pieces: furry world, talking animals, humans rare, giant wolf who smelled like pine and talked like someone used to keeping people calm. Right. Still here. He stretched on the couch, joints stiff from a night on springs that had seen better days. He rubbed the sleep from his eyes with the heels of his hands, trying to drag himself back to something close to awake.

The alarm cut off. The bedroom door creaked open and Lukas walked out, fur messed up from sleep, athletic shorts hanging low on his hips. He scratched at his ribs with one paw, eyes half-closed, moving with the loose shuffle of someone who woke up early out of habit, not choice.

"Morning." Lukas glanced at the folded blanket on the couch cushion, gave a small nod like he approved. "Couch didn't bite?"

Cody turned toward the voice and his body reacted before his brain caught up. He rolled back toward the far side of the couch, heart kicking against his ribs. The massive wolf shape in the dim morning light tripped every alarm he had.

Then memory caught up. The forest. The crowd. The dorm. The coffee.

"Shit, Lukas." Cody let out a breath and pressed a hand to his chest. "You scared me for a second. Still getting used to the whole culture shock thing. Sorry."

"No blood, no foul." Lukas yawned, fangs catching the window light. "Takes longer than twelve hours to swap universes without freaking out." He moved toward the kitchenette, nudging the coffee pot toward Cody. "Pour, breathe, pretend this is normal. By lunch you'll be catalogued and maybe halfway home."

Cody grabbed the pot and filled a mug, the bitter smell cutting through the fog in his head. Home. The word landed in his chest and just sat there, heavy and confusing.

"Yeah," he said, dragging the word out longer than he meant to. "Home. Sure."

Lukas caught the hesitation. His ears tilted back a little, but his face stayed neutral. He filled his own mug, steam rising off it.

"Or stay," Lukas said. "Choice isn't a slam dunk either way." He took a slow sip. "Library first. After that, you pick the lane."

Cody stared into the black coffee. The guy looking back at him in the reflection was tired and confused. Same face that had spent too many years just going through the motions.

"I don't know, man." He shook his head. "I don't want someone digging through ancient books on my account. Makes me feel like a jerk." He glanced up at Lukas, trying to read him. "I mean, it's not like portals to alternate realities just pop up out of nowhere. Or is that normal here?"

Lukas's face stayed flat, but one ear twitched. "Portals aren't Tuesday errands," he said. "But Timberwood keeps a shelf for the weird stuff. Meteor squirrels, haunted vending machines. You're just this month's footnote." He slid the sugar closer. "Drink. If the books come up empty, we'll figure it out without the guilt trip."

Cody waved off the sugar. "Thanks, but I take my coffee black in the morning. Hits harder that way."

Lukas raised an eyebrow, looking almost impressed. "Most freshmen drown it in syrup." He leaned against the counter, drinking his own black coffee. "Finish quick. Gem texts like she's charging by the letter."

Cody drained the mug in a few long gulps. The caffeine hit his blood like flipping a switch, sharpening his thoughts, dragging his brain back to something that actually worked. He set the empty mug on the counter and rolled his shoulders.

"Alright," he said. "I'm ready."

Lukas rinsed his cup and put it in the sink. "Library's across the quad. Stick close, keep your head down. People still rubberneck." He grabbed his keys, his phone, and a half-eaten protein bar from the counter. "Save your questions for Gem. She likes being the center of attention."

"Noted. Lead the way."

Lukas pushed the door open. Morning air hit Cody's face, cold and sharp, the bite of late October. The sky hung low and gray, threatening rain but probably bluffing. Wind cut through his thin jacket like it wasn't even there. He shoved his hands in his pockets and wished, not for the first time, that he'd landed in this world with something warmer. The quad stretched out in front of them, lawns brown and dead, brick paths dark from overnight moisture. Oak trees lined the edges, branches mostly bare, though a few stubborn leaves still hung on in shades of rust and brown. The ones that had fallen covered the grass in soggy patches, and every few seconds the wind sent a handful skittering across the walkway like they were late for something. A few early joggers moved along distant paths, breath making little clouds. Lukas positioned himself between them and Cody without being obvious about it, pace easy but purposeful.

"Path cuts behind the science block," Lukas said, voice low. "Fewer gawkers."

Cody's eyes kept wanting to wander. Everything about this place pulled at him: buildings that mixed organic curves with familiar brick and glass, statues of animals in scholarly robes, lampposts that curved like they were reaching for something. His pattern brain was already working, cataloging details whether he wanted it to or not. The way the architecture blended old and new. The paths that radiated out from central hubs. The sight lines that let you see who was coming from multiple directions. Someone had designed this place to feel open but watchable. Safe but surveilled. He wanted to stop and look, to figure out how a place like this could exist. But Lukas was taking back routes for a reason. Cody still remembered all those staring faces in the clearing.

"Right," he said. "No need to draw attention. Got it."

"Eyes forward, ears open," Lukas murmured as they passed a hedgehog on a bicycle. The hedgehog glanced their way, did a double-take, then pedaled on without stopping. "You'll get the tour after we know you won't disappear mid-sentence."

Cody almost smiled. "Sorry. Explorer Cody's on hold. Noted."

Lukas made a sound that might've been a laugh. "Explorer Cody can have his turn once the liability drops."

They cut behind the chemistry building, Lukas's boots scraping against wet brick. A squirrel in a lab coat almost ran into them coming around a corner, apologized in a distracted voice, then froze when it saw Cody. Eyes went wide. Whiskers twitched.

Lukas steered him forward before the moment could drag out. "Almost there."

Cody glanced back. The squirrel had his phone out. Typing something. Not moving.

*Filing that away.*

The library announced itself before they reached it.

It sat at the end of a narrow path, a hulking pile of gray stone and old brick that looked older than everything else on campus. Three stories of gothic architecture, all pointed arches and narrow windows, with a clock tower sticking up from one corner like a raised fist.

Dead ivy crawled up the east side in a tangle of brown and rust, still clinging even though it was dead, waiting for spring to bring it back. Gargoyles crouched at the corners of the roof, faces worn smooth by decades of weather. A pair of stone badgers flanked the main entrance, sitting on their haunches with books open in their laps, carved eyes watching everyone who walked by.

The words HOLLOWELL HALL were carved into the stone above the main doors, letters dark with age.

Near the entrance, a campus security officer stood with his arms crossed. Stocky. Badge on belt. Badger.

The same one from the clearing yesterday.

Cody's pattern brain flagged it immediately. *Routine patrol? Or following?*

Lukas didn't break stride, but his route shifted. Side door instead of main entrance. Didn't even glance at the officer.

*He noticed too.*

Cody ducked his head, hunched his shoulders. The last thing he wanted was to become a problem for the only person in this world who'd been decent to him. If Gem couldn't find a portal, if there was no way back, Lukas would probably kick him out. And then what? Wander around a town full of talking animals until someone decided he was a problem worth solving?

Lukas glanced back. His eyes caught the hunch of Cody's shoulders, the way he'd folded in on himself.

"Hey." His voice was low and steady. "You're not cargo." He slowed down just enough to walk beside Cody instead of ahead of him. "If Gem comes up empty, we figure something else out. Dorm's still yours until you say otherwise."

Some of the tension in Cody's chest let go. Not all of it, but enough.

"Your bullshit detector is scarily accurate, bro," he muttered.

"Thanks." Lukas shrugged, ears flicking. "Years of reading blitzes."

Lukas steered them away from the main entrance, toward a side door half-hidden behind some evergreen bushes. Gothic arch in weathered stone, iron ring handle dark with age. He grabbed it and pulled. Cool air came out, carrying the smell of old paper and dust, that library quiet.

"Gem's waiting on the main floor," Lukas said. "Then we head down to Special Collections. Try not to stare."

The street noise cut off the second they stepped inside.

The temperature dropped right away, cooler and still, with that old library smell. Paper, wood polish, something musty underneath. Cody's eyes took a second to adjust. The ceiling was high, vaulted and dark, held up by thick wooden beams that looked like they'd been there forever. Somewhere deeper in the building, a radiator clanked and hissed, fighting the autumn cold.

The main floor stretched out in rows of tall shelves, packed tight with books whose spines showed every shade of old. Cracked leather, faded cloth, gold letters worn to nothing. Reading tables sat between the stacks, green-shaded lamps on each one, gray morning light coming through the tall windows.

A few students hunched over textbooks and laptops. A raccoon in reading glasses. A deer with antlers so wide he'd claimed a whole table for himself. A bunch of mice sharing one oversized chair.

Cody's pattern brain read the room automatically. The raccoon was stressed, flipping pages too fast, not retaining anything. The deer was calm, methodical, probably a senior who'd done this a hundred times. The mice were studying together but one of them kept checking her phone. Group project drama, probably.

*Stop it,* he told himself. *Focus.*

A massive circulation desk sat in the center of the room, carved from dark wood and cluttered with return carts and stamp pads. A sleepy-looking owl staffed it, feathers ruffled, barely glancing up as they passed.

Their shoes scuffed against the floor, and somewhere a clock ticked. That was it for noise. Lukas led him past the reference stacks, tail low, eyes checking the rows.

"Third carrel on the left," Lukas whispered. "She's already waving at us."

Cody looked up, risking being seen. And there, tucked into a study carrel near the back wall, a flash of red fur caught the lamplight.

A slim fox in an oversized cardigan waved from behind a small fortress of stacked books and a beat-up laptop. Her fur was a bright reddish-orange, brighter at the tips of her ears and along her tail. A mechanical pencil stuck out of the fur behind one ear like she'd forgotten about it.

Her amber eyes flicked to Cody, went wide for a second, then snapped back to Lukas with a look that said questions were lining up behind her teeth.

His pattern brain caught the rest. The way she leaned forward, not back. Curious, not threatened. The slight tilt of her head, cataloging him the same way he was cataloging her. The tap of her finger on the desk, impatient but controlled.

*She's smart,* he thought. *And she knows it.*

Lukas held up a palm. *Later.*

"Gem, this is Cody. Human, lost, portal mystery." He tilted his head toward the fox. "Cody, Gem. Campus dictionary with a tail."

Cody looked at those wide amber eyes and did his best to smile. "Um. Hi."

Gem's ears twitched, curiosity practically buzzing off her. She leaned forward over the carrel desk, pencil almost falling out of her fur. "Human? Like, actual skin, no fur?" She caught herself, cleared her throat, and sat back. "Right. Sorry. Research mode." She closed her laptop and stood, slinging a messenger bag over her shoulder. "Good news: I found your flavor of weird. Bad news: I need to poke at you a little to confirm it. But not here." She glanced at the library entrance, then back. Her voice dropped lower than necessary. "Too many ears. We need to go downstairs."

She led them through the stacks toward the back of the building, past rows of shelves that got older and dustier the farther they went. They reached an alcove where an ancient elevator sat behind a brass gate, the kind with an accordion door that looked like it belonged in a black-and-white movie.

Gem pulled the gate open with a screech of metal. "Special Collections. Basement level. It's where they keep the weird stuff." She grinned at Cody. "You'll fit right in."

The elevator groaned as it went down, cables creaking overhead. Cody watched the floors tick by through the gate. Main floor, then a long stretch of nothing, then a shudder and a stop.

The basement was a different world.

The ceiling hung low, close enough that Lukas's ears almost touched it. The air was heavier down here, thick with the smell of old paper and dust that had been settling for decades. Glass cases lined the walls, filled with books too old or too strange for the main collection. Wooden card catalogs stood between reading alcoves like guards, brass handles gone green. The lighting came from old gas lamps that had been converted to electric, and they flickered like they weren't sure about the upgrade.

A security camera sat in the far corner. Small, easy to miss. But Cody's pattern brain caught it.

Gem led them to a back corner where a heavy wooden table sat surrounded by stacks of open books and ancient tomes. She dropped her bag on a chair and started pulling out supplies: a small plastic baggie, a vial of silver dust, a stopwatch.

The corner she'd chosen put a bookshelf between them and the camera. Coincidence? Cody doubted it.

"Welcome to my office," she said. "Well, my unofficial office. Officially I'm not supposed to be down here without a faculty escort, but Professor Whitmore owes me for fixing his citations last semester." She flipped open a thick book, one claw tapping a hand-drawn circle on the yellowed page. "Now. Let's see what flavor of weird you actually are."

Cody kept his mouth shut. Lukas had told him to let her talk, let her get what she needed. If she asked questions, he'd answer. Otherwise, zip it.

But he was watching. Reading her movements, her confidence, the way she handled the materials like she'd done this before. This wasn't improvised. She'd prepared.

*She was already looking into this before we got here,* he realized. *Lukas must have told her last night.*

Lukas leaned over Gem's shoulder, looking at the inked symbols. "Timeline?"

"Two hours for the sigil to set, plus whatever juice ripped the hole open." Gem's eyes flicked to Cody, sizing him up. "Residue sticks to whoever falls through. You're basically a walking receipt."

Lukas nodded once, then turned to Cody. "She needs a sample. Hair, spit, whatever's easiest."

Cody blinked. The timing seemed almost too neat. "So hold on. You're telling me this is a totally normal thing that happens here? If that's the case, this portal can be opened any time?"

Gem snorted. "Normal? Please. Documented is not the same as normal." She tapped the page in front of her. "Portals leave fingerprints. Emotional spikes, time residue, the whole messy package. I map the print, I guess when the door might swing again. Could be tomorrow. Could be never." She leaned forward, pencil spinning between her fingers. "That's why we test now instead of lighting candles and hoping."

"So you're basically telling me it's decision time."

"Decision time was yesterday," Lukas said quietly, "when you landed in pine needles."

"What he said." Gem shrugged. "We test, we wait, we see if the door still remembers you. If it does, you get a choice. If it doesn't, welcome to Timberwood, population: you."

Cody's chest tightened. "Okay, okay, okay. It just seems so sudden. I didn't think I'd need to make this decision this fast." He swallowed. "But Gem, when I landed here, I didn't do any ritual that needed blood, hair, or a chant. Are you sure this is going to work?"

"Rituals are for drama queens." Gem leaned back in her chair, grinning. "Portals don't ask permission. They yank, they toss, they leave lint behind. I just read the lint." She pulled a sterile Q-tip from the baggie. "Swab inside your cheek. Thirty seconds. No biting, no overthinking."

"Okay. So what do you want me to do? Just hand you a swab?"

Gem held out the Q-tip across the table. "Twirl it inside your cheek. Thirty seconds, no biting." She set the little stopwatch on the wood next to her elbow. Lukas shifted his weight, putting his back to the nearest aisle, blocking the view while Gem uncapped the vial of silver dust.

Cody did what he was told, watching the whole thing with detached curiosity. The swab felt weird and clinical against the inside of his mouth, seconds ticking by in the basement quiet.

When he handed it back, Gem sprinkled the dust over the damp cotton. It stuck there like frost for a second, then lit up purple. Cody's breath caught.

Gem's head tilted. Her pencil stopped tapping.

For a second, the purple flickered. Something underneath. Amber? Gold? Gone before Cody could be sure he'd seen it.

Gem blinked. Her ears flattened, then relaxed. She glanced at Cody, then back at the glow, then made a small note in the margin of her book. Didn't say anything.

"There we go." Her voice was steady, but her pencil was tapping again. Faster than before. "Fourteen hours since the door opened. Emotional signature reads panic spiking into relief." She glanced at Lukas, one ear cocked. "Your stray checks out."

Lukas breathed out through his nose. "Window?"

"Hard to say exactly." She wiggled her fingers at the fading glow. "Resonance doesn't snap shut like a door. It fades. You've got maybe a week before the signal's too weak to punch through." She met Cody's eyes. "After that, the trail goes cold and I can't promise anything. Door might crack open next year, might stay shut forever."

Cody did the math. "A week."

"Give or take a day." Gem shut the book. The note she'd written disappeared with it. "Enough time to figure things out. Not enough to waste."

A week. The number landed in Cody's head and just sat there.

He thought about what was waiting for him back home. The apartment. His father. The bus rides to a campus where nobody knew his name. The job at the burger place, grease under his fingernails, counting down the hours until he could leave.

Then he thought about this place. The stares, sure. The confusion. But also the coffee this morning, and Lukas blocking the gawkers, and a fox who'd spent her night researching how to help a stranger.

*A week to decide.*

"So I'm here for the next few days, play tourist, and then decide to leave or stay." He tried to sound casual. "Simple enough, I guess."

"Tourist, refugee, transfer student." Lukas hooked a thumb toward the elevator. "Pick your label. Dorms stay open, cafeteria still serves. We'll keep you off the front page till you figure things out."

"So what now?"

Lukas tapped Gem's notes into his phone, fingers moving fast. Time stamp, location, residue color. "Now we get out of here. Library's got ears, even down here." He tilted his head toward the elevator. "Back exit, then food. You've got time to decide if Earth still owns you. No point doing it hungry."

Cody turned back to Gem. Something about how she'd handled all this, quick and capable and curious without being pushy, made him want to say something.

"Thanks, Gem."

The fox flicked a two-finger salute, quick and easy. "Don't do anything boring, human. I'll be disappointed."

The elevator groaned back up to the main floor, and Lukas led them toward a back stairwell Cody hadn't seen before. They climbed one flight and pushed through a door that opened onto a side path behind the library.
