"I've seen what happens when you don't catch things in time," Gem said, her voice dropping. "People drift. Wounds fester. By the time you notice, it's too late to fix. Don't let this be one of those times."

She stood up to leave, then paused.

"He'll come find you. Give him twenty minutes to stop being an idiot."

Her footsteps faded toward the stairs.

---

Lukas came down eighteen minutes later.

Cody was still on the couch, staring at the TV without seeing it. He heard the footsteps on the stairs, the hesitation at the bottom, the slow approach across the worn carpet.

Lukas stopped a few feet away. His fur was dry now, clothes changed, but his ears were still flat against his skull.

"Can I sit?"

Cody just shrugged. Didn't look at him.

Lukas winced at that. He sat down anyway, leaving space between them.

For a long moment, neither of them said anything. The TV droned on. Some commercial about car insurance.

"I was wrong," Lukas said finally. His voice was rough. "What I said. All of it."

Cody didn't respond.

"Rumi got in my head at practice. Said some things." Lukas's claws dug into the armrest. "About you. About me picking up strays for attention. About how I always need someone to fix so I feel important."

The words hung there.

"And you believed him?"

"No." Lukas exhaled hard. "But he knows where to hit. He always has." He turned to look at Cody, and his eyes were different now. Open. Vulnerable in a way Cody hadn't seen before. "We grew up together. Best friends since we were six. I thought he'd be my brother forever."

"Gem told me."

Lukas nodded slowly. "Then you know. He chose status over me. Over everything we had." His voice cracked slightly. "And I've never... I don't know how to talk about it. So when he pokes at it, I just... shut down. Push people away before they can do what he did."

Cody finally looked at him. Really looked.

The big wolf linebacker. The guy who'd bought him a jacket and fought off stares and made sure he had a place to sleep. Sitting there with his tail tucked and his ears flat, looking like a kid who'd just lost his best friend all over again.

Part of Cody wanted to let him sit there. Let him stew. See how it felt to be the one hurting for once.

The thing behind his ribs liked that idea.

But Lukas wasn't his father. Wasn't the friends who'd ghosted him after the assault. He was just a guy who'd been cut open by someone he thought would always have his back, and bled on the nearest person.

Cody knew what that felt like.

"You're not pushing me away," he said. The words scraped out, rough and reluctant. "I'm still here."

Lukas's breath caught. For a long moment he didn't say anything.

"I'm sorry." His voice broke on it. "I didn't mean any of it. You're not just some tourist. You're not a stray I picked up out of pity." He swallowed hard, claws digging into his own knee. "You're... you matter. Okay? You matter to me."

The thing behind Cody's ribs wanted to call bullshit. Wanted to push back, test it, make Lukas prove it.

He didn't.

"I know what it's like," Cody said. Each word a small surrender. "I had a best friend too. Only one." He finally met Lukas's eyes. "Something happened. Something I did. And after that, he pretended I didn't exist. Like we'd never known each other." He swallowed. "Everyone else I understood. But him? That one broke something. So yeah. I get it. Doesn't mean it didn't hurt."

"I know." Lukas's voice was barely above a whisper. "I know it hurt."

Silence. The TV murmured in the background, neither of them listening.

"Something you did?" Lukas's voice was careful. Leaving the door open.

Cody opened his mouth to deflect. To change the subject. To shove it back down where it couldn't breathe.

But he was tired. So tired of carrying it alone.

"Yeah." His voice came out rough. "Something I did. And I lost everything because of it." He blinked hard, swallowing against the tightness in his throat. "I'm not ready to talk about it. But... yeah. It's there."

Lukas was quiet for a moment. Then he shifted closer on the couch. Close enough to matter. His paw found Cody's shoulder. Heavy. Deliberate.

"I don't need the details," Lukas said. "I just need you to know something." He waited until Cody looked at him. "Whatever you did? It doesn't change anything. Not for me."

Cody's throat tightened.

"You don't even know what it was."

"Don't have to." Lukas held his gaze. "I know who you are now. That's enough."

Something cracked in Cody's chest. Not the rage this time. Something older. Heavier. The grief he'd been dragging behind him like a chain for years.

He let it pass through instead of shoving it down. Just this once.

It hurt. But it was a clean kind of hurt. The kind that meant something was healing instead of festering.

"We're both kind of messed up, huh?" Lukas said.

"Probably." The corner of Cody's mouth twitched. Not quite a smile. Not yet. "But at least we're messed up together."

Something in the room shifted. Still fragile. But enough.

Lukas's tail gave a small wag. Just once.

"Dinner?" he asked. "Thorne's ordering pizza. Extra cheese. My treat."

"You already bought me a jacket."

"I'll put it on your tab." Lukas squeezed his shoulder, then let go. "Come on, stray. Let's go be messed up with the rest of the pack."

Cody stood up. The thing behind his ribs had finally gone quiet. Settled, like a dog that had been given a place by the fire.

That was new.

"Lead the way, wolfy."

The nickname landed before he'd thought about it. But he didn't take it back. Lukas's ears perked up, something warm flickering behind the exhaustion.

"Wolfy?"

"You got a problem with it?"

"Nah." Lukas bumped his shoulder as they headed for the stairs. "I've had worse."

They climbed in comfortable silence, the TV fading behind them. Cody's hands had stopped shaking. His chest didn't feel like a bomb anymore.

Three days ago, he would've walked out that door and never looked back. That was the pattern. Someone hurts you, you leave before they can do it again. Protect yourself. Stay small. Stay gone.

But he hadn't left. He'd stayed. And Lukas had come back.

Maybe that was what mattered. Not the wound. The repair.

He was still figuring out if this place was home. But for the first time, he wanted to find out.

---

## MEMORANDUM: OFFICE OF THE DEAN
### TIMBERWOOD HOLLOW UNIVERSITY
### CLASSIFICATION: RESTRICTED ‚Äî ADMINISTRATIVE EYES ONLY

‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢

**DATE:** [CURRENT: Day 3 of Subject Presence]

**RE:** Anomaly Incident Report TH-2024-001: Preliminary Assessment and Acquisition Recommendation

**FROM:** V. Graves, Dean of Academic Affairs

**TO:** [REDACTED: Security Division]

‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢

**SUMMARY:**

Resonance signature detected approximately 48 hours ago in the northeast pine grove, approximately 200 meters from the athletic complex. Pattern consistent with Class-IV dimensional transit: full biological transfer, intact consciousness, stable vitalization. Campus security confirmed visual: human, male, early twenties, no visible trauma.

This is the first viable acquisition opportunity Timberwood has documented in fourteen years.

**SUBJECT PROFILE:**

- **Designation:** TH-2024-001
- **Species:** Human (Homo sapiens): CONFIRMED
- **Estimated Age:** 19-22
- **Physical Condition:** Ambulatory, alert, no apparent degradation
- **Portal Resonance:** Active (see attached spectral analysis)
- **Current Status:** Uncontained. Integrated with student population.

**OBSERVATIONS:**

Subject has been observed in the company of Lukas Raines (Senior, Athletic Scholarship, Football). Raines appears to have assumed a protective role. Motivation unclear. No prior connection to dimensional research. No known personal stake. Continued observation authorized.

Subject has not attended classes, has not registered, and does not appear in any institutional database. He is, for all administrative purposes, a ghost.

Resonance readings remain anomalously stable. Unlike previous subjects, decay has not followed expected degradation curves. This warrants immediate investigation ‚Äî the window will not remain open indefinitely. Once resonance fades to baseline, subject becomes significantly less valuable for longitudinal frequency mapping.

**RECOMMENDATION:**

Immediate retrieval and transfer to Sublevel Research Wing for preliminary examination. Full workup to include:

- Comprehensive biological sampling (tissue, marrow, cerebrospinal fluid)
- Resonance field documentation under controlled conditions
- Psychological intake and cognitive baseline
- Long-term observation protocols (minimum 6-month commitment recommended)

I am aware that retrieval presents logistical complications given subject's integration with the student body. However, I must stress: opportunities of this nature do not recur on convenient timelines. The last comparable subject (TH-2010-003) expired during transit before any meaningful data could be collected. The one before that (TH-1996-017) was claimed by Hartwell Institute within 48 hours of manifestation.

Timberwood has waited long enough.

I have authorized discreet surveillance. I am also making contact with assets within the athletic program who may prove useful in facilitating a quiet transfer.

This matter should be resolved before Friday's game. Public events create complications.

Further updates to follow.

‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢

**V. Graves**
*Dean of Academic Affairs*

*Timberwood Hollow University*

# TIMBERWOOD HOLLOW ‚Äî PART 2

## Chapters 15-22 (The Dean Saga)

**Clunky Check Complete:** 6 banned phrases fixed
**Paragraph Consolidation:** Already properly consolidated

# Chapter 15: The Open Door


Cody woke to the smell of coffee and the sound of the closet door sliding open.

He lay still for a moment, letting the room come into focus. Pale light through the blinds, the fold-out couch springs digging into his back in the same places they always did, water running through pipes somewhere down the hall.

Last night kept surfacing. The fight. The way Lukas had come back down the stairs, ears flat, tail tucked. *You matter to me.* The words had landed somewhere deep, in a place Cody didn't let people reach. He still wasn't sure what to do with them.

He turned his head. Lukas was pulling a practice jersey from the closet, already dressed from the waist down. His fur caught the morning light, silver streaks threading through the darker coat. When he moved, there was that easy looseness in his shoulders again. Whatever broke last night had started to mend.

"Hey." Cody's voice came out rough. "What time is it?"

Lukas glanced over. Something flickered in his expression, almost a smile. "Little after eight. Practice in forty, so I gotta run soon." He pulled the jersey over his head, tugging it down over his chest. The fabric stretched tight across his shoulders. Cody looked away, suddenly very interested in the ceiling.

"How you feeling? You were out cold when I got up. Thought you might actually be dead."

"Fine." Cody pushed himself up, the couch frame groaning. "Slept okay."

"Liar. That couch is a torture device." Lukas crossed to the kitchenette and poured two cups of coffee. He slid one toward the edge of the counter, same spot he always did. "Black, strong enough to strip paint. The Cody Guiles special."

Cody took it. The mug was warm in his hands. "You memorized my coffee order."

"You've been here four days. Doesn't take a genius to notice." Lukas leaned against the counter, watching him over the rim of his own mug. His tail swayed once, low and easy.

The silence stretched, comfortable but full of something unspoken. Something had shifted between them. Cody could feel it, even if he couldn't name it. The air was different, charged in a way it hadn't been before last night. He didn't know if Lukas felt it too, or if this was just him, reading patterns that weren't there. He took a sip of coffee to avoid saying something stupid.

Lukas set his mug down and grabbed his gym bag from the floor. "Film review first, then drills. Coach wants us sharp for Saturday."

"Big game?"

"Rivalry game. Rumi's been running his mouth all week." The name landed flat. His jaw didn't tighten the way it had yesterday, and Cody filed that away as progress. "Should be back by eleven." He slung the bag over his shoulder and stopped at the door, paw resting on the frame. He didn't turn around. "Stay put, okay?"

*Stay safe. Stay hidden. Don't do anything stupid while I'm gone.*

"Yeah," Cody said. "I'll be here."

Lukas nodded once. Then he was gone, the door clicking shut behind him.

---

An hour later, the room felt different without Lukas in it.

Cody stood by the window, watching the quad three stories below. Students crossed in pairs and clusters, breath fogging in the morning chill, leaves skittering across the brick paths. Normal life. The kind he used to have.

*Stay put.* The words kept circling back. Like a dog waiting for its owner.

Cody pushed the thought away. Lukas didn't mean it like that. He was just being careful. They were all being careful, all the time, and Cody was grateful for it. He was. The walls of the room pressed in. The fold-out couch where he slept, the gaming setup he didn't touch, the trophies on the shelf he tried not to look at. All of it Lukas's. None of it his. He needed air. Or at least a different set of walls.

Cody grabbed his jacket from the back of the couch, the charcoal canvas one with the Timberwood crest, and headed for the door. The common lounge downstairs had vending machines, a TV, couches that weren't his borrowed bed. It was something. He pulled the door open and stepped into the hallway. The carpet was worn down the middle, a path of footsteps from years of students trudging to class. He passed Thorne's door, then Benji's, then hit the stairwell.

Halfway down the second flight, footsteps came up from below.

A raccoon in a campus polo rounded the landing, clipboard tucked under one arm. Student worker, probably. The kind of kid who signed up for work-study because the alternative was asking parents for money. The raccoon looked up, saw Cody, and stopped.

"Oh. You're the one." He scanned Cody like he was checking a box. "Human, right? Staying with Raines?"

Cody's stomach dropped. He wasn't supposed to exist. Not officially. "Who's asking?"

The raccoon shrugged. "Got a delivery. Admin sent me to Westfall, third floor, looking for the human." He held out a folded piece of paper. "Guess I found you."

Cody took it. The paper was heavy, the kind they used for official university stuff. A crest was embossed at the top. "Thanks," he said, but the raccoon was already leaving, door swinging shut behind him.

Cody turned the paper over. No envelope. Just folded once, nothing written on the outside. He unfolded it.

---

*Cody,*

*I understand you've had an unusual introduction to our campus. I imagine the past few days have been disorienting, perhaps frightening. That's understandable.*

*I'd like to offer some clarity about your situation, if you're willing. My door is open this morning. No pressure, no obligations. Just a conversation between two people who might benefit from understanding each other.*

*You deserve answers. I may have some.*

*Warm regards,*
*V. Graves*
*Dean of Academic Affairs*
*Office 412*

---

Cody read it twice. Then a third time. His name. Just his first name, but still. How did the Dean of Academic Affairs know his name?

He leaned against the stairwell wall, the note heavy in his hands. His first instinct was to wait for Lukas. Show him the note, let him decide what to do. That was the safe play. That was what Lukas would want. But Lukas would say no. Lukas always said no. Every path they walked was chosen to avoid something. Every decision was made for Cody, not with him. He thought about the past few days. The side routes. The camera blind spots. The way Lukas checked his phone and didn't explain, the way Gem talked about him like he was a problem to be solved, the way everyone whispered when they thought he wasn't listening. They were protecting him. He knew that. But when was the last time anyone asked what HE wanted?

*Just a conversation.* This could be a trap. Obviously it could be a trap. But it could also be information. Intel. The thing you gathered before you made a move, not after.

Cody pushed off the wall. His legs felt steadier than he expected. He headed for the door.

---

The walk across campus felt different alone.

For three days, Lukas had been a wall between Cody and everything else. A warm wall, a protective wall, but still a wall. Now there was nothing between Cody and the morning air, the students cutting across the quad, the buildings that had only ever been backdrop. He took the main path. The one Lukas always avoided.

It cut past the science building, the one with the double helix sculpture out front. Stainless steel, taller than Lukas, catching the morning light in a way that made it hard to look at directly. The building itself was brick, darker than the rest of campus, six stories when everything else topped out at four. Wide glass doors showed a lobby that always seemed empty no matter what time of day it was. Cody had seen it a dozen times from a distance. Up close, it should have looked like just another building. Students sat on the concrete planters out front, coffee cups in hand, textbooks open on their laps. But none of them were facing the doors. They sat with their backs to the glass, angled toward the quad, like they'd chosen the spot for the sun and nothing else. Cody kept walking. Maybe Lukas was paranoid. Maybe they all were. But he walked a little faster anyway.

The admin building sat at the north edge of the quad, brick and ivy, older than most of the campus. Cody pushed through the main doors and the smell hit him first: floor wax and old paper and that particular staleness of places where important things got decided by people who never had to stand. The directory by the elevator said Office of Academic Affairs was on the fourth floor. He pressed the button and waited.

The elevator was slow and old, the kind that made you think about fraying cables, skipped inspections, the last time anyone bothered to check. Cody watched the numbers climb: 2, 3, 4. The doors opened with a sound like a sigh. The hallway was carpeted, muted, the kind of quiet that made you want to whisper. Doors lined both sides, nameplates in brass. Most were closed. At the far end, one stood slightly open. The brass plate caught the light:

**412**
**VICTOR GRAVES, Ph.D.**
**Dean of Academic Affairs**

Cody walked toward it. His footsteps didn't make any sound on the carpet, which felt wrong somehow, like the building was swallowing evidence of his presence. He stopped outside the door. Through the gap, he could see the edge of a window, daylight spilling across a wooden floor. He knocked.

"Come in." The voice was soft, warm even, the kind that made you think of guidance counselors and family doctors.

Cody pushed the door open.

---

The light hit him first. Floor-to-ceiling windows lined the back wall, the whole campus spread out below like a postcard. Morning sun poured in, catching dust motes, making the room feel bigger than it probably was. A view for important people doing important things.

Then the photos. They were everywhere. Framed shots on the walls, on the desk, on the bookshelf behind it. A silver-furred lynx with kind eyes, standing next to Graves at what looked like a faculty party. Two young snow leopards in graduation robes, grinning at the camera. A family vacation shot, everyone smiling, mountains in the background. More photos lined the walls: Graves shaking hands with someone official, Graves cutting a ribbon in front of a building, Graves at a podium receiving an award. A framed thank-you card covered in student signatures. A crayon drawing taped to the side of the bookshelf, the kind a little kid makes when they're proud of something. The desk was mahogany, worn smooth in the spot where paws would rest. A mug sat near the edge: "World's Okayest Dean." A bowl of wrapped candies caught the light.

And in the middle of all of it, rising from a leather chair, was the snow leopard from the photos. He was taller than Cody expected. Lean, silver-white fur with dark spots that had faded to gray at the edges. Pale blue eyes that seemed to take in everything without moving. He wore a charcoal suit, perfectly fitted, a navy tie, a watch that probably cost more than Cody's dad made in a month.

"Cody." The voice matched the note, quiet and unhurried. "Thank you for coming. Please, have a seat." He gestured to one of the chairs facing the desk. Leather, well-worn, positioned so the window was behind them. Cody would be backlit. Graves would see every expression on his face. He sat down anyway.

"Can I offer you something? Water? Coffee?" Graves nodded toward the candy bowl. "These are actually quite good. Butterscotch. My weakness."

"I'm fine."

"Suit yourself." Graves took one himself, unwrapping it with the casual ease of someone who'd done it a thousand times. He popped it in his mouth, crumpled the wrapper, dropped it in a small trash can by the desk. "There. Now you know I'm not trying to poison you." Cody didn't laugh. Graves didn't seem to expect him to. The snow leopard settled back in his chair, paws folded on the desk. His expression could have been curiosity or patience. Hard to tell.

"You know, I realized I don't even know your full name."

Cody's hands went still in his lap. His real name. The one on the police report and the local news segment and the scholarship that got revoked. The name the whole town learned to say with a certain tone. He could lie. Make something up. Lukas would probably tell him to. But Lukas wasn't here. And wasn't that the whole point? Making his own choices?

"Guiles."

"Guiles," Graves repeated, nodding once. He didn't reach for a pen or paper. The name just disappeared somewhere behind those pale blue eyes. "Thank you, Cody. I imagine you've heard things about me. About the administration. About what we might want from someone in your situation."

Cody didn't answer. His eyes drifted to the family photos on the desk. The wife, the kids. The frames looked old. Dusty on top, like they hadn't been moved in years.

"I won't pretend those concerns are baseless," Graves continued. "There are people in any institution who see anomalies as opportunities. Research subjects. Data points. I've worked with some of them. I know the type." He paused, letting that land. "But I'm not one of them. And I'm not interested in treating you like a specimen."

"What are you interested in?"

The question came out sharper than Cody meant it to. Graves didn't flinch. If anything, his expression warmed. "You," he said simply. "Your experience. Your survival." He leaned forward slightly, paws still folded. "Do you understand how remarkable you are, Cody? Cross-dimensional transit has a mortality rate we don't discuss publicly. The stress on biological systems, the cellular disruption, the neurological trauma... most arrivals don't survive the crossing. The ones who do are often... damaged. Permanently."

Cody's throat tightened. He thought about the white void. The way reality had folded in on itself. The black that came after.

"You walked through something that should have killed you," Graves said. "And you came out the other side intact. Healthy. Functional. That's not luck. That's something else entirely."

"Something you want to study."

"Something I want to understand." Graves spread his paws, a gesture of openness. "This isn't about locking you in a cage or running tests in some basement. I'm talking about learning what made you different. Documenting it. And potentially helping the next person who falls through." The next person. Cody hadn't thought about that. The idea that there might be others, falling through portals, dying in transit, alone and scared and with no one to catch them on the other side.

"What if what's in your biology could save someone?" Graves asked. "What if understanding your survival could make the difference for a kid like you, five years from now, who doesn't have a Lukas Raines waiting in a pine grove?"

The name landed like a stone in still water. "You know Lukas."

"I know everyone on my campus, Cody." The first name again. Friendly. Familiar. Like they'd known each other for years instead of minutes. "He's a good young man. Protective. Loyal. The kind of person who sees someone in trouble and acts without thinking about consequences." He paused. "Which is admirable. But not always wise."

The wrong details kept surfacing. The photos that were too old. The candy bowl that was too full. The way Graves controlled the light, the seating, the rhythm of the conversation. The way he used Cody's name like they were friends. But nothing he was saying was wrong. That was the problem. Nothing was a lie.

"Your friends care about you," Graves said. "That's obvious. But I wonder sometimes..." He let the sentence hang.

"Wonder what?"

"Whether they're protecting you. Or whether they're keeping you scared."

The words hit somewhere Cody didn't expect. Somewhere soft. "There's a difference," Graves continued, "between helping someone and making them dependent. Between keeping someone safe and keeping them small. I've seen it before. Good intentions calcifying into control. The person being protected loses their agency so gradually they don't even notice it's gone."

Cody thought about the side routes. The blind spots. The way Lukas decided where they walked, when they ate, who they talked to. The way every choice was made FOR him.

"When's the last time you decided something for yourself?" Graves asked. "Where to go. What to do. Who to speak with." The question sat in the air like smoke.

"I decided to come here," Cody said.

Graves smiled. It was a small smile, almost sad. "Yes. You did. And I suspect that wasn't easy." He was right. It hadn't been.

"You seem like someone who's used to taking care of himself," Graves said. "Someone who's had to. Because no one else would."

Cody went still.

"I recognize the look," Graves said, voice low. "I was that young man once. Capable. Determined. Surrounded by people who meant well but couldn't stop treating me like a problem to be managed. Who couldn't see that I didn't need protection. I needed trust." The words settled into Cody's chest like sand filling a hole. How did he know? How could he possibly know?

"It's exhausting," Graves said. "Being protected by people who won't let you protect yourself. Being helped by people who don't trust you to help them back. You start to wonder if you're actually the person they see, or if you're just their idea of who you should be."

Cody didn't answer. Couldn't.

Graves let the silence stretch. Then he stood, and the conversation was over. Just like that. He was in control of when it ended, and he'd decided it ended now. "I'm not asking you to decide anything today," he said. "I'm not asking you to trust me, or agree with me, or do anything at all." He walked around the desk, slowly, giving Cody space. "I just wanted you to know that my door is open. If you want answers, real answers, not filtered through people who've already decided what's best for you... you know where to find me."

He stopped a few feet away. The light from the window fell across his face, and for a moment Cody saw something underneath the warmth. Something patient. Something that had been waiting a very long time.

"Think about it," Graves said. "That's all I ask."

---

The hallway felt longer on the way out. Cody walked past the brass nameplates, the closed doors, the carpet that swallowed his footsteps. The elevator took forever. The lobby smelled like floor wax and paper and something else now, something he couldn't name.

Outside, the campus looked exactly the same. Students crossing the quad. Someone laughing near the fountain. The science building's helix sculpture caught the midmorning light, throwing a glare across the brick. The lobby behind those glass doors was still empty, but that didn't seem strange anymore. Lots of buildings had empty lobbies.

But Cody wasn't seeing it the same way.

He replayed the conversation in his head. The photos, the candy, the way Graves had controlled everything without ever raising his voice. The way he'd known exactly which buttons to push. *Someone who's used to taking care of himself. Because no one else would.* It wasn't wrong. That was the thing. None of it was wrong. Lukas did make all the decisions. Gem did talk about him like a problem. Everyone did whisper when they thought he wasn't listening. But they were protecting him. Weren't they?

He thought about telling Lukas. Showing him the note, explaining where he'd been, letting him decide what it meant. But Lukas would be angry. Lukas would say he'd been stupid, reckless, that he should have waited, should have trusted them, should have stayed put like a good little"‚Äù

Cody stopped walking. His hands had curled into fists without him noticing. There was heat in his knuckles, tight and sharp, like his body was getting ready for something his brain hadn't agreed to yet. He uncurled them. Breathed. He was doing it again. Running every decision through what Lukas would think. What Lukas would want. What Lukas would decide. *When's the last time YOU decided something?*

He folded the note and put it in his pocket. He'd think about it. That was all. Just think. The morning sun was warm on his face as he walked back toward the dorm. Somewhere behind him, a clock tower chimed ten. He had an hour before Lukas got back. An hour to figure out what any of this meant. An hour to decide if the doubt taking root in his chest was wisdom or poison. He didn't know yet.

But he was starting to wonder.

---

---

# Chapter 16: Teeth and Trust


The door opened at eleven on the dot. Lukas filled the frame, gym bag over one shoulder, practice jersey dark with sweat. His fur was damp at the edges, ears still flattened from the helmet. He looked tired, but something in his posture loosened when he saw Cody sitting on the couch.

"Hey." He dropped the bag by the closet. "You stayed."

"Said I would." Cody kept his eyes on the window.

Lukas crossed to the kitchenette and ran water over his paws, scrubbing off the grass and chalk. "Practice was brutal. Rumi's got everyone wound tight for Friday." He shook his paws dry, grabbed a towel. "You eat anything?"

"Wasn't hungry." His stomach had been in knots since Whitmore Hall.

Lukas paused. His eyes found Cody's face, and Cody felt the weight of that look. Reading him. The way he always did. "You okay?"

"Fine." The word came out too fast. Cody made himself shrug. "Just bored. Walls start pressing in after a while." Lukas studied him for another beat, then nodded, accepting it, and something in Cody's chest twisted. It was that easy to lie to him. That easy to hide.

"Got an idea." Lukas crossed to the closet and pulled out a beat-up football, spinning it once on his claws. "There's a tunnel under the practice field. Storage space, mostly. Rusted hatch, no cameras. We're going."

Not *want to go?* Not *what do you think?* Just: we're going. *When's the last time YOU decided something?*

"You played in high school, right?" Lukas asked. "Captain?"

"Running back. Three seasons." Cody watched the ball spin. He hadn't touched a football since the day everything fell apart.

"Good." Lukas tossed the ball from paw to paw. "Dark enough to disappear in. Thought we could use it."

*No cameras.* The phrase caught somewhere in Cody's head. A week ago, it would have sounded like safety. Now it sounded like something else. Isolation. Somewhere no one would see. He pushed the thought down. This was Lukas. The same Lukas who'd pulled him out of a pine grove, bought him a jacket, told him he mattered. But the doubt was there now, curled up in his chest like a stone, and he couldn't make it go away.

Cody stood, legs heavier than they should have been. "Sure. Let's go."

---

The hatch was fifty yards past the goalpost, rusted metal set into the grass like a scar. Lukas popped the padlock with a claw. The hinges groaned as he lifted it, revealing nothing but black underneath.

"Get in. I'll keep the light off until we hit the bottom."

Cody peered into the dark. "You know humans can't see in the dark, right?"

"I know." Lukas's voice softened. "Stay close. I won't let you trip."

He dropped into the tunnel first, boots ringing on metal. Cody followed, feeling his way down the ladder, the rungs cold and rough under his palms. The smell rose to meet him halfway down. Rust, damp concrete, something older underneath, like the crawlspace under his grandmother's house where he used to hide when things got loud upstairs. His borrowed shoes hit the floor with a clang that echoed too long. Somewhere ahead, water dripped in a slow rhythm, each drop landing with a small plunk that made the silence feel bigger.

Lukas thumbed his phone to life. A thin blue glow cut the shadows, just enough to see shapes. The tunnel stretched ahead of them, rebar ribs curving overhead like the inside of some giant's throat. The ceiling hung low enough that Lukas had to duck his ears. Condensation slicked the walls, catching the phone's glow in thin streaks.

"Teeth and trust," Lukas said, voice low. "That's all that matters down here." He rolled the football across his claws, then tossed it underhand. It landed at Cody's feet with a soft thud. "Pick it up. Toss it back when you're ready."

Cody picked up the ball. The laces felt familiar under his fingers, muscle memory stirring even after all these years. He hadn't wanted to remember how this felt. How natural. How easy. He stepped back a few feet, let his wrist flick on the release.

The ball cut through the dark. A soft thwap of leather hitting palm, then Lukas's voice: "Not bad for a guy who hasn't played since high school." He lofted it back, soft spiral. "Again. Loosen your wrist a little more. Let the ball do the work."

They fell into a rhythm. Each throw took Cody a little deeper into the tunnel, a little further from the phone's glow.

"Keep stepping back. I'll track your voice."

Cody backed up until he could barely see his own hands. The darkness was total now, thick enough to swallow everything. He could hear Lukas somewhere ahead, boots scuffing, breath steady. *Teeth and trust.* Cody turned the phrase over in his head. He wound up and threw harder, feeling the snap of his wrist. "Fetch."

The ball whistled through the black. A beat of silence. Then the soft pop of a clean catch, and Lukas's laugh echoed off the walls. "Good boy. Now let's see what you can handle."

The next ball came fast and low, whispering past Cody's knee. He had to bend to catch it. "Playing hard to get, wolfy?"

"Hard to get's the only game I know." The phone light vanished. Total darkness. "Voice only from here. Call out before you throw, and I'll find you."

Cody listened. He could hear Lukas moving somewhere to his left, weight shifting on concrete. He placed his fingers on the laces, stepped into it, and fired a heater into the darkness. Silence. Then a low whistle. "Almost parted my whiskers, otter."

"Otter?" Cody called back. "Why otter?"

"You showed up out of nowhere, slippery, hard to pin down." A pause. "And you've got that look. Like you're always watching the current, figuring out which way to swim."

Cody grinned despite himself. For a moment, he forgot about the note in his pocket. Forgot about Graves and the photos and the questions that wouldn't stop circling. There was just the ball, the darkness, and Lukas's voice somewhere ahead.

The next throw came without warning. One second nothing, the next the ball was inches from his face. He got his hands up on instinct, catching it but barely. "You didn't call it, asshole!"

"You want a whistle, bring one next time!" Lukas's voice echoed from somewhere across the tunnel.

Cody shook his head in the dark, still gripping the ball. Playing catch in a tunnel with a wolf. No lights, no cameras, no rules. The whole thing was ridiculous. He'd been running scared for days, and now here he was, throwing fastballs at a six-and-a-half-foot predator who called him "otter" like it was a compliment. A laugh escaped him before he could stop it. Not because anything was funny. Because nothing made sense anymore, and sometimes that was the only response left. He fired another heater into the dark, close enough to ruffle fur. The ball smacked into Lukas's palm somewhere in the black. A beat. Then: "Damn. Otter's got teeth."

"Had to. Only way to survive." The words came out before Cody could stop them. Heavier than he'd meant. The darkness held them for a moment, let them settle. Lukas's boots rang on metal, getting closer. Then Cody felt a paw brush his wrist. Lukas's thumb pressed against his pulse point, steady pressure, the way you'd check on someone who just said more than they meant to.

"Game's over." Lukas's voice was low. "Let's head back up."

---

The daylight hit like a flashbang after all that dark. Cody squinted, getting his bearings. Practice field stretching ahead, goalposts at the far end, bleachers empty. The sun had climbed high enough to wash the shadows out of the grass. Something moved in the gap under the bleachers. A shape, maybe, pulling back into the dark. Cody blinked against the glare, and when he looked again, the gap was empty. He told himself it had always been empty.

Lukas climbed up behind him and let the hatch fall shut with a thud. He dusted off his jersey, eyes already scanning the quad. "Sun's a spotlight. Anyone asks, we were checking sprinkler lines."

*Anyone asks.* Cody caught the phrase and turned it over in his head. A week ago, it would have meant nothing. Now it echoed with something Graves had said. *Secrets always have a cost.*

They walked toward the stone fountain near the fieldhouse. Water trickled from a wolf-head spout into the trough below. Lukas splashed some on his paws, washing off the dust. Cody did the same. The water was cold. It helped clear his head.

"You're quiet." Lukas glanced over, more curious than concerned.

"Just thinking." Cody kept his eyes on the fountain.

"About?"

*About a conversation I'm not telling you about. About a note in my pocket. About whether you're protecting me or managing me and whether there's even a difference.* "Nothing worth talking about."

Lukas watched him for a moment. His ears twitched once, then settled. He didn't push. But as they walked back toward the dorm, Cody couldn't shake the feeling that something had shifted. The tunnel had been good. Real. For a few minutes, he'd felt like himself again, like the person he used to be before everything went wrong. But now they were back in the light, and the doubt was still there. Waiting.

Lukas's paw brushed his shoulder as they walked. A small touch, barely there. The kind of gesture that used to feel like safety. Now Cody didn't know what it felt like.

His hand brushed his pocket. The note was still there. He didn't take it out, didn't look at it. But he knew where Office 412 was now.

And he knew the way back.

---

---

# Chapter 17: Highlighted Red

The October sun sat low and weak behind a sheet of clouds, throwing everything into that flat overcast light that made noon look like dusk. Cody's jacket, the charcoal canvas with the Timberwood crest, wasn't quite thick enough for the chill, but he didn't say anything. His legs still felt loose from the tunnel, muscles warm from throwing, and the cold air was almost welcome against his skin.

Lukas walked half a step ahead, scanning the path like he always did. Habit, probably. Or instinct. Hard to tell the difference with him.

They cut through a gap in the chain-link fence and onto the main path that wound toward the quad. Students passed in clusters, bundled in scarves and hoodies, breath fogging as they talked. A pair of rabbits in matching beanies. A squirrel darting between legs with an armful of acorns. Nobody looked twice at Cody. Four days ago, every set of eyes felt like a spotlight. Now he was just another shape moving across campus.

Lukas pulled out his phone, thumbs moving across the screen as he walked.

"Checking in with Gem," he said. "Seeing where she's at."

The phone buzzed a moment later. Lukas glanced at the screen and slipped it back in his pocket without showing Cody what it said.

*Checking in. Or checking up.*

Cody filed it away with everything else. The way Lukas always seemed to know where the pack was. The way he steered conversations, steered routes, steered everything without ever making it look like steering. Maybe that was just how packs worked. Maybe that was just Lukas being Lukas.

Or maybe Cody was being tracked, and Lukas was making sure Gem knew their location.

He shook the thought loose. Paranoid. He was being paranoid.

Near the row of evergreens that bordered the quad, a badger in a maintenance vest stood by a utility box, clipboard in hand. He looked up as they passed, eyes tracking them for a beat too long, then jotted something on his clipboard and went back to whatever he was pretending to work on.

Cody noticed. How the badger's pen moved too deliberately. How he didn't look at the utility box once.

*Probably nothing.*

But the thought felt automatic. Something he told himself because the alternative was too exhausting to consider. He was tired of seeing threats everywhere. Tired of that low hum of paranoia that had followed him since the portal. Maybe maintenance workers really did stand around with clipboards and watch students walk by. Maybe that was just how things worked here.

He didn't look back.

"Hungry?" Lukas asked.

"Getting there."

"Student Union's past the trees. Food's not bad. Better than the dorm vending machines, anyway."

They pushed through the gap in the evergreens, and the building rose ahead of them. Bigger than Cody expected. Wide brick facade, glass doors, students streaming in and out in a steady rhythm. A banner hung between two windows: GO BADGERS in forest green letters, the mascot snarling underneath.

Lukas pulled the door open.

The noise hit first. Conversations layered over each other, bouncing off the high ceilings. The clatter of trays against the long tables that ran down the center of the room, their attached benches scuffed and worn from years of use. Chairs scraping against tile. Somewhere, a burst of laughter that sounded more like a bark.

Then the warmth, wrapping around him like stepping into someone else's kitchen. The smell came with it. Not breakfast anymore. Grease from the grill, something with onions, the earthy sweetness of tomato soup. Steam rose from the food stations along the far wall, where students shuffled past with plastic trays.

The cafeteria took up most of the first floor, but it wasn't just tables and food lines. Near the windows, smaller round tables with mismatched chairs clustered together, students hunched over laptops or picking at salads. A row of vending machines hummed against one wall, a raccoon feeding coins into one while a fox waited behind him, arms crossed. Beyond the tables, a lounge area sprawled around a flatscreen TV mounted to the wall, some nature documentary playing with the sound off. A group of wolves had claimed the overstuffed couches, sprawled out like they owned the place. In the corner, an old arcade cabinet blinked and chirped to itself, ignored by everyone except a rabbit who kept glancing at it like he was working up the nerve to play.

Cody stood in the doorway, taking it in. The size of it. The noise. Wolves and foxes and bears and rabbits, all of them eating lunch, checking phones, arguing over nothing. Fur and feathers everywhere he looked. A moose with antlers that barely cleared the ceiling. A pair of otters sharing a plate of fish. A massive bear lumbering past with a tray stacked high enough to feed three people.

Four days ago, he'd been on the other side of a portal, eating gas station food in a dead-end town. Now he was standing in a college cafeteria full of talking animals, and somehow that was starting to feel normal.

"Grab a tray," Lukas said, already moving toward the food line. "Soup's usually safe. Skip the fish."

Cody grabbed a tray from the stack and followed. The line shuffled forward, students loading plates with varying degrees of enthusiasm. A wolf ahead of him was building a sandwich that could've used structural support, layers of meat and cheese stacked so high the bread looked like an afterthought. Cody waited his turn, watching the options pass. Turkey, roast beef, something that might have been ham. A vat of soup with a ladle sticking out.

"Turkey's decent," Lukas said over his shoulder. He was already at the sandwich station, hands moving with the efficiency of someone who'd done this a hundred times. "Roast beef's hit or miss. Soup's tomato today. You want coffee?"

"Sure."

Cody reached for the tongs.

By the time he got to the station, the sandwich was already made. Turkey, cheese, lettuce, no tomato. Sitting on a plate next to a bowl of soup that Lukas must have ladled while Cody was still three people back in line.

Cody looked at the plate. Looked at Lukas.

"You already made mine?"

"Faster this way." Lukas grabbed two coffees from the dispenser, steam curling up from the paper cups. "Line gets long after noon. Come on, I know a spot."

He was already moving toward the far corner of the cafeteria, weaving between tables with his tray balanced on one massive paw. Cody picked up his own tray, his lunch already assembled, already decided, and followed.

The turkey looked fine. The soup smelled fine. Everything was fine.

But something prickled at the back of his neck. That same feeling from the tunnel. From the jacket. From every moment since he'd landed in this world where someone else made the call and he just went along with it.

*He didn't even ask what I wanted.*

Not what kind of sandwich. Not if he liked tomatoes. Not if he wanted soup at all. Lukas had just... decided. The tunnel. The jacket. Everything.

Cody's grip tightened on the tray.

*He's helping. He's been helping since day one. This is what helping looks like.*

But another voice, quieter, colder, whispered: *Is it? Or is this what control looks like when it's wearing a friendly face?*

He pushed the thought down. But it didn't go quietly.

They ended up in a corner booth, partially hidden by a support column. Backs to the wall, clear view of the room. Lukas set his tray down and slid in, eyes doing a quick sweep of the cafeteria before settling on Cody. Even here, in a room full of students eating lunch, he was watching. Always watching.

*Protecting you,* Cody told himself. *He's protecting you.*

*Or keeping tabs.*

He sat across from Lukas and picked up his spoon. The soup was warm and filling, maybe a little too much basil. The sandwich was fine. Turkey, cheese, no surprises. Exactly what Lukas would have picked for himself.

Cody ate without tasting much.

Silence settled between them. The cafeteria noise filled the space, conversations rising and falling around them. A table of foxes near the windows burst into laughter at something on someone's phone. Two finches shared a bowl of seeds, pecking in synchronized rhythm.

"So," Lukas said, setting down his sandwich. "What were you studying? Back home."

The question landed somewhere between casual and pointed. Cody chewed, swallowed, bought himself a second.

He could tell the truth. English major, creative writing focus. But that felt like handing over a piece of himself he wasn't ready to give.

"Journalism," he said.

Lukas's ears tilted forward. "Yeah? You wanted to be a reporter?"

"Something like that. Asking questions. Figuring out what's really going on." Cody shrugged. "I like knowing how things work. Why people do what they do."

It wasn't entirely a lie. He'd taken a few journalism classes. Liked the structure of it, the way you had to find the truth underneath the surface. That part was real.

"Makes sense," Lukas said. "You've got that look. Like you're always watching, putting pieces together."

"That obvious?"

"Little bit." Lukas picked up his sandwich, took a bite, chewed thoughtfully. His tail flicked once under the table. "What year were you?"

"Sophomore. Would've been a junior next fall."

"Middle name?"

Cody paused, spoon halfway to his mouth. "Why?"

Lukas shrugged, tearing off another bite of sandwich like the question meant nothing. "Just curious."

"Dante. Cody Dante Guiles." He set the spoon down. "You building a dossier on me or something?"

Lukas's eyes flicked up, met his, held for a beat. Then he smiled, just a little, one corner of his muzzle lifting. "Just making conversation."

But there was more underneath. A purpose he couldn't quite name. Like Lukas was gathering pieces for what wasn't yet visible.

"What about you?" Cody asked, steering the conversation sideways. "Football's not forever. What's the plan after?"

Lukas set down what was left of his sandwich, wiped his muzzle with a napkin. For a second, something shifted in his expression. Softer, more open. Like Cody had asked a question that actually mattered.

"Physical therapy. Maybe coaching eventually." He leaned back against the booth, shoulders relaxing. "Help guys come back from injuries, keep them on the field."

"That's actually really good."

"Don't sound so surprised." But Lukas's tail wagged once, quick and involuntary, before he caught it. He looked away, cleared his throat. "Saw too many teammates blow out a knee and lose everything. Figured somebody should know how to put them back together."

Cody watched him. Lukas looking at his empty plate instead of meeting his eyes. His paw curling around his coffee cup like he needed something to hold onto.

*He actually cares about this. This isn't just small talk.*

"I'm not surprised," Cody said, quieter. "I just... didn't expect it."

"Expected what? Dumb jock, no plans past the season?"

"No. I just..." Cody searched for the words. "You seem like the type who lives in the moment."

"Maybe." Lukas pushed his empty plate aside. "But moments end. Gotta have something waiting when they do."

He said it casually, but there was weight underneath. Cody thought about the tunnel. Lukas checking his pulse after the too-heavy words. Always checking. Always watching for the thing that might break.

Lukas stood, stacking his tray. "Come on. We need to get you an ID."

The words landed heavy and sudden. Cody blinked. "Wait, what?"

"Campus ID. Gets you into buildings, lets you audit classes, makes you official." Lukas was already moving toward the tray return. "Gem's in Special Collections. She texted back, she's there all afternoon."

"Hold on." Cody stood, but Lukas was three steps ahead, sliding his tray onto the return belt. "Lukas. Wait."

Lukas turned, one ear swiveling back. "What?"

"You want to tell me what we're doing? Or am I just following?"

The words came out sharper than he'd intended. But he didn't take them back.

Something flickered in Lukas's expression. He glanced around the cafeteria. Students still eating, still talking, nobody paying attention to the wolf and the human standing by the tray return.

"Not here," Lukas said, voice lower. "Outside."

He headed for the door. And Cody, after a beat, followed.

Because that's what he did. He followed.

At least for now.

---

The quad stretched gray and quiet under the pale October sky. Brown grass, bare oaks, brick paths dark with old moisture. A few students crossed in the distance, heads down against the chill.

Lukas stopped near the fountain. Drained for the season, the stone basin empty, the wolf-head spout dry. He sat on the rim, the cold seeping through his jeans, and looked up at Cody. "Okay. I'm listening."

Cody stood there for a moment, hands shoved in his pockets. His knuckles ached. "Being in the system means being trackable," he said. "Right now I'm a ghost. Nobody knows I exist. If I want to disappear, I can."

"And what happens when someone decides you're inconvenient?"

Cody's hands curled into fists inside his pockets. "What do you mean?"

"A ghost is easy to erase." Lukas leaned forward, elbows on his knees. "No records. No paper trail. One day you're walking across the quad, next day you're gone, and nobody even knows to ask questions." His voice was steady, but his eyes were serious. "A registered student has a file. Has a schedule. Has people who notice when they don't show up to class."

"So I'm supposed to trust the system?" Cody's voice came out harder than he'd intended.

"You're supposed to trust that the system has rules. And rules leave trails."

Cody kicked at a dead leaf on the path. "And if the wrong person has access to those trails?"

"Then at least there's a record." Lukas held his gaze. "Look, I get it. Being visible feels like being vulnerable. But invisible is worse. Invisible means nobody notices when you're gone."

Cody didn't answer. Invisibility had been his armor for years. At school, in his neighborhood, at home when his father was drinking. You learned not to be seen. Not to be noticed. Not to give anyone a reason to look twice. And it had worked. He was still here. But "still here" meant something different now. Still here meant a world where people had claws and fangs and the dean of students had offered him a deal that felt more like a leash.

*Protection,* Lukas kept saying. *Paper trails. Accountability.* But Graves had said something similar, hadn't he? *I can help you.* Different words, same shape. And Cody couldn't shake the feeling that Lukas's logic sounded a little too convenient. A registered student was harder to disappear, sure. But a registered student was also easier to find. Easier to watch. Easier to control. *Who benefits from me being in the system?*

He thought about the note in his pocket. The office with the candy bowl and the family photos. The door that was still open. If he was in the system, Graves could track him. Watch his movements. Know exactly where he was at any given moment. But if he was a ghost, Graves could make him vanish without anyone ever knowing he'd existed. Two risks. Different shapes. Same teeth. And Lukas didn't know about any of it. Cody had kept that door closed, hadn't mentioned the meeting, hadn't told anyone about Office 412. He wanted to tell him. Knew he should. But the part of him that had been turning Graves's words over since this morning wasn't ready to close that door yet. He let it go.

Lukas let out a slow breath. "I'm not trying to control you." The words pulled Cody back. Lukas was looking at him differently now. Ears flat, shoulders hunched forward. "I know I... I do that. Make decisions without asking. Move before you've had time to think. Gem told me I need to stop. That you're not a project I'm managing, you're a person." He exhaled. "She's right. I'm working on it."

Cody sat down on the fountain rim. The stone was hard beneath him, but he didn't move.

"The ID thing," Lukas said. "It's not about control. It's about making sure you can't just vanish. Administration has rules. They have to follow procedures. If a registered student goes missing, there are forms, investigations, people asking questions. If a ghost disappears?" He shrugged. "Nobody even knows to look."

Cody turned it over in his head. The logic was sound. A name in the system meant accountability. Meant someone would notice if he stopped showing up. Meant the dean couldn't just make him disappear without leaving a trail. But it also meant visibility. A target on his back. A name in a database that the wrong person could find. He'd already weighed these risks. But hearing Lukas lay it out, hearing someone else say it, loosened something. Made the math clearer. Cody stared at the dry fountain, at the wolf-head spout frozen mid-howl. Then he nodded. "Okay."

Lukas looked up. "Okay?"

"The ID. We do it." Cody held his gaze. "But I need you to understand something. This is my choice. I'm not doing it because you told me to. I'm doing it because the math works out."

Lukas nodded slowly. "Fair."

"And next time you want to make a decision that affects my life, you ask first. Deal?"

Lukas's shoulders dropped. Relief, maybe. Or something close to it. "Deal."

They sat there for a moment, the empty fountain between them, the cold pressing in. Then Lukas stood and offered a paw. "Come on. Gem's waiting."

Cody took it and let himself be pulled to his feet. They started walking.

---

The library rose at the far end of the quad like something that had been there before the rest of campus existed. Gray stone and weathered brick, three stories of gothic arches and narrow windows. A clock tower climbed from one corner, its face yellowed with age. Gargoyles crouched at the roofline, worn smooth by decades of rain. Stone badgers flanked the main entrance, seated on their haunches with books in their laps. Scholars, Cody thought. Or guards. Hard to tell the difference.

Lukas pulled the heavy door open, and the smell hit first. Old paper, dust, the particular mustiness of books that had been sitting in the same place for longer than anyone could remember. The vestibule swallowed the cold air behind them, and suddenly everything was warm and quiet.

The main floor stretched ahead, vaulted ceiling rising overhead, thick wooden beams crossing like ribs. Tall windows filtered the weak afternoon light. Reading tables dotted the space, green-shaded lamps casting pools of warm light. A few students sat scattered among them, heads bent over books. A raccoon in thick glasses. A deer with antlers that nearly scraped the ceiling when he stood. The circulation desk sat at the center, massive and dark, manned by an owl who looked like she'd been fighting sleep since the library opened. A wall clock ticked above her head, each second landing with a soft click.

"Elevator's this way." Lukas nodded toward the back.

The stacks swallowed them, shelves towering overhead, spines cracked and faded. The smell got thicker the deeper they went. Paper and leather and something older underneath, like the building itself was breathing.

The elevator waited at the back, hidden behind a shelf of oversized atlases. Brass gate, accordion door, the kind of thing you'd see in an old black-and-white movie. Lukas pulled the gate open with a clang that echoed too loud in the quiet. They stepped in. The space was cramped, barely big enough for both of them, and Lukas's shoulders brushed the walls on either side. He pulled the gate shut and pressed a button that had been worn smooth by decades of fingers.

The mechanism groaned. Cables creaked somewhere above them. The floor lurched, and they began to descend.

The light changed as they dropped. Warmer, dimmer, the fluorescent brightness of the main floor giving way to amber warmth. By the time the elevator shuddered to a stop, the air had thickened, heavy with dust and the particular silence of rooms that didn't get many visitors.

Lukas pushed the gate open, and Special Collections spread out before them. Low ceiling, low enough that Lukas had to duck his ears as he stepped out. The walls were lined with glass-fronted cases, old books and strange artifacts visible behind the panes. Wooden card catalogs with tarnished brass handles. Converted gas lamps that flickered like they remembered flame. And in the back corner, behind a heavy wooden table surrounded by folios and tomes, sat Gem.

Her ears perked as they approached. Amber eyes flicked up from the document she was studying, the mechanical pencil behind her ear catching the lamplight. She looked at Lukas first, then at Cody, and something in her expression softened slightly. "Still breathing," she said. "Both of you. That's progress."

The words landed differently than they might have yesterday. Cody caught the way her eyes moved between them, assessing. She knew something had happened. Of course she did. She was the one who'd told Lukas to stop treating him like a project.

"Good to see you too." Lukas stopped at the edge of her table, paws shoved in his pockets. "Need a favor."

Gem leaned back, tail flicking. "You have that look. The one that usually means I'm about to regret answering my phone."

"Campus ID. Photo, name, barcode that scans."

"You're asking me to forge federal documents." Gem's ears flattened. "In the middle of a Tuesday afternoon. In a university basement."

"Can you do it or not?"

"I don't know, Lukas. I've never done it before." She pulled open a drawer and started rummaging. "But I've seen the templates. I know where the blank card stock is. And I'm reasonably confident I can make the barcode work." She pulled out a small camera, then a stack of blank cards, then a portable printer that looked like it had been liberated from the registrar's office. "No promises on the laminator. I borrowed it from the geology lab and I'm not entirely sure it still works."

Cody watched her gather the equipment. No practiced movements. No smooth routine. She was figuring this out as she went, pulling pieces together from different places, making it work because she was smart enough to improvise.

"Why?" Gem asked, setting the camera on the table. Her eyes found Cody's. Direct. Sharp. "Why does the human from another world need a campus ID badly enough for me to risk getting expelled?"

Cody opened his mouth. Closed it.

"Protection," Lukas said. "Paper trail. If he's in the system, he's harder to disappear."

"I wasn't asking you." Gem's pencil started tapping. "I was asking him."

The basement went quiet. Just the flicker of the gas lamps and the distant creak of the building settling. Cody thought about lying. About deflecting. About letting Lukas answer for him the way Lukas had been answering for him since he got here. But Gem had told Lukas to stop treating him like a project. She deserved honesty.

"It was his idea," Cody said, nodding toward Lukas. "But the logic makes sense. A ghost is easy to erase. A name in the system means someone notices if I disappear." He paused. "And I'm tired of other people making decisions for me. This one I'm choosing."

Gem's tapping stopped. Her ears swiveled forward, and for a long moment she just looked at him. Then the corner of her mouth twitched. "Good answer." She turned to Lukas. "See? He can think for himself. Like I told you."

Lukas's tail tucked slightly. He didn't argue.

"Stand over there." Gem pointed at the filing cabinets along the back wall. "Try to look like you've slept in the last week."

Cody walked over and positioned himself against the cabinets. Cold metal at his back, the smell of old paper all around him. He straightened his collar, tried to relax his shoulders.

Gem raised the camera. "Full name?"

Lukas opened his mouth.

"I can answer for myself," Cody said again. Quieter this time. Not angry. Just certain.

Gem's eyebrow rose. Lukas's jaw clicked shut.

"Cody Guiles." He paused. "But I'm guessing you don't want my real name on the card."

"Smart human." Gem lowered the camera. "Pick something. Something common. Something nobody's going to remember five minutes after they check your ID."

Cody thought about it. Not Otterton. Not some clever reference that would make Lukas feel like he'd contributed. Something boring. Something invisible. "Miller," he said. "Cody Miller."

"Major?"

"Journalism."

Gem typed it into her laptop, keys clicking in the quiet. "Year?"

"Sophomore."

She nodded, still typing. "Okay, Cody Miller. Journalism major. Sophomore. Look at the camera and try not to look like you're about to commit a felony."

He managed something that was probably closer to a grimace. Flash. He blinked against the afterimage, purple spots swimming in his vision.

Gem checked the screen. "You look like a student. Confused, slightly lost, definitely hasn't slept enough." She shrugged. "Perfect."

She fed the image into the laptop, connected the printer, and held her breath as the machine whirred to life. A card slid out, still warm. She ran it through the laminator twice, checked the barcode with her phone, and nodded once. "It'll scan. Probably. Don't use it anywhere with a live database check." She held up the card, studied it, then tossed it to Cody.

He caught it. His face stared back at him from the plastic. Cody Miller. Journalism. Sophomore. A person who had never existed until thirty seconds ago. He turned the card over in his hands. The plastic was still warm. The photo looked like him but didn't feel like him. Or maybe it felt more like him than the old one ever had.

*You're in the system now.* The thought should have felt like victory. Safety. A paper trail that meant someone would notice if he disappeared. But Graves's voice was still there, somewhere in the back of his head. *My door is open. If you want answers, real answers, not filtered through people who've already decided what's best for you...* The dean had offered protection too. Different words, different shape, but the same promise underneath. And now Cody had put himself in a system that Graves could access. Could track. Could use. Was this safety? Or had he just made himself easier to find?

"You okay?" Lukas's voice, soft.

Cody looked up. Both of them were watching him. Gem with her head tilted, pencil tapping. Lukas with that worried crease between his eyes. "Yeah." He slipped the card into his pocket. His fingers brushed paper, the note still there, still waiting. He pulled his hand out quickly. "Just thinking."

"Dangerous habit." Gem was already turning back to her laptop. "Don't make a career of it."

"Thanks, Gem." Lukas tilted his head toward the elevator. Not a push this time. An invitation.

They stepped into the elevator. The gate clanged shut. The mechanism groaned, and they began to rise.

Cody glanced back through the brass bars. Gem was already turning back to her desk, pulling a stack of documents toward her. But instinct made him keep watching. She stopped. Her eyes had found her monitor, and her expression shifted. Her tail, which had been flicking absently, went still. The elevator climbed. The basement fell away. And Cody lost sight of her face before he could read what was on it.

---

Gem stared at the screen.

The registration she'd just processed was highlighted red. A flag she hadn't put there.

DENIED ‚Äî ADMIN HOLD ‚Äî REF: OFFICE 412

Her pencil stopped tapping.

Office 412. That was Graves. Dean Victor Graves, who never flagged student registrations, who never touched the system directly, who operated through channels and committees and plausible deniability. Until now.

She pulled up the reference file. Empty. No notes, no reason for the hold, no explanation. Just the flag itself, blinking red like a warning. Or a claim.

Gem leaned back in her chair. Her tail curled around her legs, tight and still. She didn't call after them. Didn't send a message. Not yet.

First, she needed to understand what she was looking at.

She opened a new search window and started digging.

---

# Chapter 18: Office 412

The admin building. Fourth floor, corner office. Morning light cut through the floor-to-ceiling glass, throwing long shadows across the mahogany desk. The campus spread out below like a model someone had built to scale. The quad. The library. The science building at the far edge, its windows dark even now.

The chair faced the window. It usually did.

Victor Graves watched a pair of students cross the quad, backpacks bouncing, breath fogging in the cold. They walked close enough that their shoulders touched. Young. Careless. No idea what moved beneath the surface of their little world. His claws tapped the armrest once. Twice. A rhythm he'd developed decades ago, back when he still believed patience was a virtue instead of a necessity. The plain white mug sat at his elbow, coffee gone cold an hour ago. The "World's Okayest Dean" mug stayed in its drawer. No audience today.

Three knocks at the door.

"Enter."

The badger stepped inside. Security uniform pressed, clipboard in hand, eyes fixed somewhere around Graves's desk instead of his face. Good. The ones who made eye contact were the ones who thought they mattered.

"Sir. The human. We have eyes on him."

"I'm aware." Graves didn't turn. The window held more interest than the badger's nervous shuffling. "What's the status?"

"Spotted him leaving the library basement yesterday with Raines. Lost visual when they cut through the maintenance tunnels. Picked him up again near the athletic center this morning, then nothing." The badger consulted his clipboard like it held answers instead of questions. "Raines is keeping him moving. Using back routes, service corridors. Avoiding the main paths."

"He knows where the cameras are."

"Yes, sir. He's been here long enough to learn them."

"Raines." The name sat in the air between them. Graves let it sit. "The linebacker."

"Yes, sir. Lukas Raines. Junior. Starting lineup. His room's been flagged, but we can't move on student housing without cause. The RA would ask questions."

"Questions have answers. That's not a problem." Graves's claws stilled on the armrest. "The problem is timing. The human needs to be contained before Friday."

"The game, sir?"

"The game." Graves watched a crow land on the science building's roof, black against gray stone. "Fifteen thousand people in the stadium. Cameras everywhere. Donors. Parents. If our guest decides to walk onto that field in front of the crowd, it becomes a very different conversation. One I'd prefer to avoid."

The badger scribbled something. His pen scratched loud in the quiet office.

"Double the patrols near the athletic facilities. I want someone on Raines's schedule at all times. Practice, classes, meals. Where he goes, eyes follow." Graves paused. "Discreetly. The wolf is suspicious enough already. If he thinks we're closing in, he'll find somewhere we can't reach."

"Yes, sir."

"And send for Kashtar."

The pen stopped. "Sir?"

"Rumi Kashtar. Football team. The bull." Graves's voice stayed soft, the way it always did. "He should be finishing his morning workout about now. Have him here within the hour."

The badger hesitated. Graves could feel it without looking. The question forming, the doubt. Whether this was wise. Whether involving a student athlete in whatever this was crossed some line the badger still believed existed. The hesitation passed. It always did.

"Yes, sir."

The door clicked shut. Silence settled back over the office like dust on an old photograph.

Graves reached for his coffee, remembered it was cold, and set it back down. The mug left no ring on the desk. Nothing in this office left marks. He'd made sure of that years ago. Through the glass, the campus continued its morning routine. Classes starting. Students shuffling between buildings. The machine grinding forward, indifferent to the small dramas playing out in its gears.

Somewhere down there, a human was hiding. Breathing air that didn't belong to him. Walking on ground he had no right to touch.

Fourteen years Graves had waited. Fourteen years since TH-2010-003 expired in transit, taking with it any hope of meaningful data. Fourteen years of budget meetings and alumni dinners and watching younger researchers publish papers on subjects they barely understood. This one was different. This one was alive. Healthy. Ambulatory. Walking around like it belonged here. The preliminary observations alone suggested cellular adaptation unlike anything in the literature. If he could just get his hands on it.

His claws curled into the leather armrest. The material creaked, soft as a sigh.

Patience. The subject wasn't going anywhere. Raines thought he was protecting something precious, but all he'd done was delay the inevitable. The wolf was emotional, impulsive, too wrapped up in whatever attachment he'd formed to see the larger picture. That was useful. Emotions made people predictable.

A knock. Heavy this time. Different rhythm.

"Enter."

The door swung open and the frame filled with muscle. Rumi Kashtar stood in the doorway, arms crossed over a chest that strained against his workout shirt. Nose ring catching the light. Dark eyes that couldn't quite hide the hunger underneath the suspicion.

Graves let him wait. Five seconds. Ten. Long enough for the silence to become uncomfortable.

Then he turned his chair. The movement was unhurried. The leather barely whispered as he pivoted to face his guest for the first time. His expression softened into something that looked almost like warmth.

"Rumi." The name landed easy, familiar. "Thank you for coming. I know your schedule is demanding this time of year. Please, sit. Make yourself comfortable."

"Security guy said you wanted to see me." Rumi's voice was a rumble, the kind that came from a chest built for intimidation. "Didn't say why."

"No, he wouldn't have. I wanted to speak with you personally." Graves gestured to the chair across from him with an open paw. "Can I get you anything? Water? Coffee? I have some of those protein bars the athletic department swears by, if you're hungry."

Rumi didn't move.

Graves let the offer hang for a moment, then nodded like he understood completely. "Straight to business. I respect that." He leaned forward, elbows on the desk, paws folded. The posture of a man sharing a confidence. "I've been watching you, Rumi. On the field. In practice. You have something special. Raw talent that doesn't come along very often."

Something shifted in Rumi's stance. Not much. Just a loosening of the shoulders.

"Coach Henderson mentions you in his reports. Says you work harder than anyone on the team. First one in, last one out." Graves shook his head slowly, a hint of sadness in his voice. "It's a shame, really. All that dedication, and somehow you keep getting passed over. Raines gets the spotlight. Raines gets the scouts' attention. And you're left wondering what you have to do to get noticed."

Rumi went still. His arms stayed crossed, but his weight shifted forward. Listening now.

"I know that feeling." Graves's voice dropped, intimate. "I was young once, believe it or not. Talented. Ambitious. Watching lesser people get opportunities I'd earned." He spread his paws. "It's not fair. But the world rarely is. The question is what you do about it."

"What are you saying?"

"I'm saying I'd like to help you, Rumi. I see potential that's being wasted, and that bothers me." Graves reached for the candy bowl on his desk, took one of the wrapped chocolates, and held it out. An offering. "There's a situation on campus. Something delicate. And I think you might be uniquely positioned to assist."

Rumi didn't take the candy. "What kind of situation?"

"There's a young man. A human. You may have noticed him around Lukas." Graves set the chocolate down, unbothered by the rejection. "He's in trouble, Rumi. The kind of trouble that could hurt a lot of people if it's not handled carefully. Including Lukas."

That got a reaction. A flicker of something in Rumi's eyes. Not concern, exactly. But interest.

"Lukas thinks he's protecting this young man by hiding him. But he's not. He's putting himself at risk. His scholarship. His future. Everything he's worked for." Graves shook his head again, the picture of paternal worry. "I've tried to reach out, but Lukas and I... well. He doesn't trust easily. You know that better than anyone."

Rumi's expression shifted. Recognition, maybe. Or just the memory of a friendship that used to mean something.

"I need someone Lukas trusts. Someone who can keep an eye on the situation. Make sure things don't spiral out of control." Graves met Rumi's eyes, steady and warm. "And in return, I'd like to make sure your hard work finally gets the recognition it deserves. Full scholarship. Starting position. A real shot at the future you've earned."

The bull was quiet for a long moment. His chest rose and fell, slow and heavy.

"You want me to spy on Lukas."

"I want you to help me protect him from himself." Graves's voice stayed gentle. Understanding. "Lukas is emotional. He makes decisions with his heart instead of his head. That's admirable in some ways, but it's also dangerous. He's going to get hurt if someone doesn't intervene. I'm just asking you to be that someone."

Rumi's arms uncrossed. His hands hung at his sides, fingers flexing like he was working through a problem he couldn't quite solve.

"And if I say no?"

"Then you walk out that door and we never had this conversation. No hard feelings. No consequences." Graves spread his paws, palms up. Nothing to hide. "I'm not here to threaten you, Rumi. I'm here to offer you an opportunity. What you do with it is entirely up to you."

The silence stretched.

Then Rumi's shoulders dropped, just slightly. The resistance going out of him, replaced by something that looked almost like relief. Like he'd been waiting for permission to do what he'd wanted to do all along.

"What do you need from me?"

Graves smiled. It looked warm. It looked kind. It looked like exactly the right response to a young man making a difficult choice.

"Just stay close to Lukas. Let me know where he goes, who he talks to. If the human surfaces, I need to know immediately. That's all." He reached into his desk drawer and pulled out a card. Plain white. A phone number printed in simple black text. "My direct line. Use it anytime, day or night. You're not alone in this, Rumi. I want you to know that."

Rumi took the card. Turned it over once. Slipped it into his pocket. "Yeah. Okay."

"Thank you." Graves stood, extended his paw across the desk. A handshake between equals. "I knew I could count on you. Coach Henderson was right about you. You're exactly the kind of young man this program needs."

Rumi shook his paw. His grip was strong, but there was something uncertain in his eyes. Something that hadn't quite settled.

"I'll be in touch." Graves released the handshake and settled back into his chair. "And Rumi? This conversation stays between us. For Lukas's sake."

The bull nodded once. Then he turned and walked out, footsteps heavy on the carpet.

The door clicked shut.

Graves sat still for a moment, listening to the heavy footsteps fade down the hall. Then he exhaled, slow and measured, and the warmth bled out of his expression like frost sliding off a window. His pale blue eyes went flat as old ice. His claws curled into the leather armrest, leaving marks he'd have someone buff out later.

Rumi Kashtar. Resentment wrapped in muscle. The boy had been nursing that wound for years, letting it rot. All Graves had done was give it a target.

Useful. Predictable. Disposable when the time came.

He watched the campus through the glass. The crow was gone from the science building roof. A maintenance cart trundled across the quad, slow and purposeless. His computer chimed. The sound was soft. Almost polite. A notification he'd set up years ago, back when he still believed in being thorough. He turned to the monitor. Pressed a key.

**SECURITY ALERT: UNAUTHORIZED ACCESS ATTEMPT**
**Archive Section: Portal Research / Restricted (Pre-2015)**
**User Terminal: Library Basement, Station 7**
**Access: DENIED**
**User ID: G. Kindler (Student)**

Graves's claws stilled on the keyboard.

Kindler. The fox. The one who ran the unofficial lab in the library basement. The clever one who'd been poking around since the human arrived. He pulled up her file. Academic record. Spotless. Research interests. Dimensional theory, portal mechanics, cellular adaptation. Faculty references. Glowing. Clever girl. Too clever for her own good.
