She was looking for something. His old research, probably. The early work he'd done before administration swallowed his career. Trying to understand what the human was, where he came from, what it meant. And now she'd tripped a wire she didn't know existed.

Graves leaned back in his chair. Miss Kindler had just made herself useful.

He opened his email. Began to type.

*Miss Kindler,*

*I couldn't help but notice your recent inquiry into restricted research archives. Fascinating curiosity.*

*I'd hate for your academic standing to be affected by any... misunderstandings about unauthorized system access. And I'm sure Mr. Raines would prefer his scholarship review to proceed without complications.*

*I'm confident we can resolve this simply. Have the human come to Office 412. Alone. By 5 PM.*

*Warm regards,*
*Dean Victor Graves*

Send.

The email disappeared into the system. A small thing. A stone dropped into still water. But ripples had a way of spreading.

Graves turned back to the window. The quad was quiet now. Students drifting between classes, unaware. The library sat in the distance, its old stone walls holding secrets they couldn't begin to imagine.

Somewhere inside, a clever fox was about to learn what happened when you dug too deep.

He settled back in his chair and folded his paws. Friday. It would all be over by Friday. The trap was set. Now all he had to do was wait for the prey to walk into it.

---

# Chapter 19: The Flag

They walked.

The path curved around the edge of the quad, past the drained fountain and the old oaks with their bare branches scratching at the gray sky. A few students passed them going the other way, a raccoon with headphones and a pair of rabbits arguing about something that made one of them laugh. The lampposts were starting to flicker on, that in-between hour when the campus couldn't decide if it was still afternoon or already evening. Leaves skittered across the brick path ahead of them, rust and copper against the dark stone. The air had that crisp bite to it, the smell of cold dirt and dying grass.

Cody noticed it somewhere past Whitmore Hall. Lukas wasn't leading, wasn't steering him around cameras or pointing out shortcuts or explaining which buildings had side entrances. He was just walking beside him, their shoulders close enough that Cody could feel the warmth radiating off his fur. For once, that was enough, and it felt new.

The silence between them didn't itch the way silence usually did. Cody didn't feel the need to fill it with something, to perform, to be interesting enough to justify taking up space. He just walked, and Lukas walked with him, and the evening wrapped around them, comfortable and unhurried.

"Koi still spelling things at midnight?" Cody asked.

Lukas's ear flicked toward him. "Only if you're drunk enough to read it."

"Seems like a design flaw. Mystical fish that only talk to alcoholics."

"Campus has priorities." The corner of Lukas's muzzle twitched into something that was almost a smile. "You get used to it."

They passed a cluster of students on a bench, a fox and two deer huddled over a shared textbook. One of them glanced up as they walked by, then went back to the book without a second look. Another evening on campus, a wolf and a human walking together like it was the most normal thing in the world. Cody filed that away. A week ago, he would have felt like a target, an anomaly, something that didn't belong. Now he just felt like a guy walking with his friend. Friend. The word fit strange in his head. Like a jacket he'd forgotten he owned.

"You always like this?" The question came out before he'd decided to ask it.

Lukas glanced at him. "Like what?"

"I don't know." Cody shrugged, hands in his pockets. "Taking in strays. Showing them around."

Lukas was quiet for a few steps. The path curved past a row of evergreens, their branches dark against the fading sky. When he spoke, his voice was lower. Less guarded. "Someone did it for me once. Long time ago."

He didn't elaborate. Cody didn't push. Some doors you let people open on their own time.

They walked in silence for a while after that. The sky was turning purple now, first stars starting to show through gaps in the clouds. Somewhere behind them, a door opened and closed, laughter spilling out and fading.

Lukas slowed. Stopped near a bench under one of the old oaks. He sat down, leaning back against the worn wood, and looked up at the darkening sky. There was something different in his face. Softer. Like he'd set something heavy down and wasn't in a hurry to pick it back up.

"So what's it like?" he asked.

Cody lowered himself onto the bench beside him. "What's what like?"

"Your world." Lukas turned his head, those amber eyes catching what was left of the light. "What's it like?"

The question landed heavier than it should have. Four days Cody had been here. Four days of longer routes and careful paths and Lukas quietly putting himself between Cody and anything that might be a problem. A constant, steady presence steering him through a world he didn't understand yet. And this was the first time anyone had really asked about him. Where he came from. What his life had been like before all this.

Lukas waited patiently, ears forward.

Cody let out a breath. "When I was a kid... it was good. Really good." The memories came easier than he expected. "Summer barbecues in the backyard. The smell of charcoal and cut grass. My mom had this laugh that could fill up a room without even trying." He smiled a little. "My dad used to hoist me onto his shoulders at the county fair. Made the world twice as tall. All those lights and sounds, and I'm up there like I'm king of everything."

Lukas hummed, a low rumble in his chest that said *keep going.*

"We had this thing on Sundays," Cody said. "Pancakes. My mom always burned the first one on purpose. Called it the sacrifice pancake. Said the griddle gods demanded tribute before they'd cooperate."

"She had this garden. Tomatoes and basil, mostly. The whole backyard smelled like it in summer. She had a system. Little stakes with labels, watering schedule on the fridge, the whole thing. My dad used to tease her about it. Called her the plant warden." The smile widened a little. "There was this spot by the lake we'd go every year. Rent the same cabin. Mom would sit on the dock and read. Dad and I would take the rowboat out, try our luck with these old fishing rods that probably belonged to the cabin before we got there. Never caught much. Didn't matter. We'd just sit there, him and me, not saying anything. Watching the water. He'd point out the birds sometimes, tell me which ones were which. I don't remember most of the names now. But I remember the way it felt. Being out there with him."

The smile stayed for a moment longer.

"She got sick when I was eleven." The words came out quiet, and whatever warmth had been in his face drained away, slow and unstoppable.

Lukas leaned back against the bench, his shoulder pressing closer to Cody's. A quiet weight that said *I'm here.*

"Some rare thing they didn't catch until it was too late. One of those cancers that hides until it's everywhere." Cody kept his eyes on the darkening sky. "Took almost a year. I watched her shrink. That's the only word for it. Like she was disappearing a little more every day. Her voice got quieter. Then her laugh. Then her eyes. By the end she was already somewhere else. Even before she stopped breathing."

The words came out flat. Not because he didn't feel them, but because he'd felt them so many times they'd gone numb at the edges, like a scar you forget is there until someone asks about it.

"My dad too," he said. "Not his body. His everything else. He'd sit in that hospital room every day, and I could see pieces of him leaving with her. The part that laughed at her jokes. The part that pointed out the birds. The part that carried me on his shoulders. By the end, I'd lost both of them. She was just the only one who got a funeral."

Lukas didn't say sorry or try to fix it. He sat there beside him, solid and present, and that was enough.

Cody was quiet for a long moment. The campus had gone still around them, just the distant hum of traffic and the rustle of leaves in the wind.

"There's this little cemetery outside town," he said finally, his voice rougher now. "Chain link fence with a gate that never locks right. Grass that gets too long between mowings. An old oak in the back corner with a split trunk from a lightning strike years ago, still growing anyway. Her name's on a marker under that tree. I used to go every Sunday. Sit there. Talk to her about nothing." He swallowed. "Tell her about the book I was reading. Complain about my chemistry teacher. Sometimes I'd bring her flowers from the grocery store, the cheap ones in the plastic wrap. It was all I could afford back then."

He tipped his head back and looked at the sky, all wrong stars and strange patterns, and the weight of it hit him fresh. He'd never stand at her grave again.

"I'm never going to see it again."

His throat tightened. He blinked hard, jaw clenching against the pressure building behind his eyes. Something flickered in his chest, a heat that didn't belong to the grief. He breathed through it until it settled.

They sat in silence. Somewhere across campus a door slammed, someone laughed, life going on the way it does. Cody didn't have to explain what any of it meant. Lukas already got it.

"What would you tell her?"

Cody looked over. Lukas was watching him with that same quiet attention, the kind that didn't demand anything, just waited.

"If you could go back one more time," Lukas said. "What would you say?"

The question hit harder than he expected. Cody sat with it. The seconds stretched out. He turned it over, looking for an answer that didn't feel like too much or not enough. What would he tell her? That he survived? That he found people who actually gave a damn? That for the first time since she died, he didn't feel like he was just waiting for the next bad thing to happen?

He looked at Lukas. The fading light caught the gold in his eyes. Cody opened his mouth.

Lukas's phone buzzed.

The sound shattered the moment like a rock through a window. Lukas blinked, pulled the phone from his pocket, and whatever had been open in his face closed off the second he read the screen.

"It's Gem. We need to go. Now!"

He was already on his feet and moving. Cody pushed himself off the bench and followed, the question still stuck in his throat, the answer he'd almost given already fading into something he might never get a chance to say.

---

They found Gem in the library basement, hunched over her terminal with the kind of focus that said something had gone very wrong. The screens cast blue light across her face, and she didn't look up when they came in.

"Took you long enough." Her voice was flat. Controlled. But Cody could hear the current running underneath it.

"What happened?" Lukas moved to stand behind her, eyes scanning the screens.

"I was digging. After the ID got flagged, I wanted to know why. What we're actually dealing with."

"And?"

"And I found out." She pulled up a window. Text and file names, most of them redacted or incomplete. "Portal arrivals. There have been others, Lukas. Not many. But enough to have a system. Catalogue numbers. Dates. Status reports."

Cody stepped closer, reading over her shoulder. TH-1996-017. TH-2003-005. TH-2010-003. Letters and numbers that used to be people.

"What happened to them?" His voice sounded strange to his own ears.

"That's what I was trying to find out. I went deeper. Tried to access the full records. And I hit this." Gem clicked. The screen flashed red.

**ACCESS DENIED**
**ARCHIVE SECTION: PORTAL RESEARCH / RESTRICTED (PRE-2015)**
**UNAUTHORIZED USER**

"That was two hours ago," Gem said. "Then this showed up."

She opened her email. One new message, sitting at the top of the inbox like a bomb with a blinking timer. She clicked it open. Cody read the words on the screen, and something cold slid down his spine.

*Miss Kindler,*

*I couldn't help but notice your recent inquiry into restricted research archives. Fascinating curiosity.*

*I'd hate for your academic standing to be affected by any... misunderstandings about unauthorized system access. And I'm sure Mr. Raines would prefer his scholarship review to proceed without complications.*

*I'm confident we can resolve this simply. Have the human come to Office 412. Alone. By 5 PM.*

*Warm regards,*
*Dean Victor Graves*

The timestamp on the email read 4:47 PM. It was past seven now.

"The deadline passed," Cody said. "What does that mean?"

"It means he's not waiting anymore." Gem finally looked at him. Her eyes were steady, but he could see the fear underneath. The anger. "It means whatever he was going to do if you didn't show up, he's already doing it."

Lukas's paw clamped around Cody's arm and yanked him toward the door.

"Lukas, wait!" Cody's heart was hammering, the email's words still burning in his vision.

But Lukas was already pulling him toward the door, grip tight enough to bruise. "Maintenance tunnels. No cameras. You stay hidden until we figure out a plan."

"Shouldn't we talk about this?!"

Lukas didn't answer. He was already moving, hauling Cody with him. Behind them, Gem's fingers flew across the keyboard, her face lit blue by the screen.

---

The tunnels swallowed them. Pipes ran along the ceiling, some wrapped in insulation that was peeling off in strips, others sweating condensation that dripped onto the concrete below. Caged bulbs threw pools of yellow light every twenty feet or so, leaving long stretches of shadow between. Lukas moved fast, boots echoing off the walls, taking turns without hesitation.

Cody followed, his mind still stuck on the words from that email. Academic standing. Scholarship review. Complications. Because of him. All of it because of him.

They stopped at a heavy metal door, rust creeping up from the hinges. A faded sign read VALVE ROOM 7 - NO ENTRY. Lukas pushed it open, revealing a cramped space lit by a single bulb that buzzed and flickered. Old pipes ran along the walls, valves frozen with age, gauges cracked and dark. A cot sat in the corner, the kind you'd find in an army surplus store, its canvas sagging in the middle.

"Stay here." Lukas's voice was tight. "Don't open the door for anyone. Three short knocks, two long. That's me. Anyone else, you don't answer."

"Lukas, this is my problem." Cody's voice cracked on the last word.

"This is me keeping you safe. Let me do that. Please!" Lukas's eyes were wet, his jaw tight, the panic barely held together by the need to move.

Cody nodded.

Lukas turned and pulled the door shut behind him.

The footsteps soon faded and silence pressed in.

Cody stood in the dim room, surrounded by dead gauges and rusted pipes, the conversation that never got to end still rattling around in his head. The email played on loop. Gem's face when she showed them. The way Lukas had grabbed his arm like he was afraid Cody might disappear. Academic standing. Scholarship review. Gem could lose everything she'd worked for. Lukas could lose his future, the scholarship that was supposed to carry him to professional ball, to a life beyond this place. Because of Cody. Because he'd fallen through a hole in the sky and landed in their lives like a grenade with the pin already pulled.

He crossed to the cot and sat down, the canvas creaking under his weight. The peace from the walk felt like it belonged to someone else now, some version of him from an hour ago who didn't know what was coming.

*That I found people who give a damn.*

He'd almost said it. Almost told Lukas what he meant. What he was starting to feel. Now he was sitting in an abandoned valve room while everyone he cared about scrambled to protect him from a threat he'd brought to their door.

Something stirred behind his ribs, heavier than rage. Guilt wrapped in helplessness, coiling tight.

His mother's face flashed through his mind. Not the smiling one from the pancake mornings. The other one. The one at the end, when she was too tired to pretend anymore. When she'd looked at him and he'd seen the apology in her eyes, like dying was something she was doing wrong. He'd sat there every day after school, holding her hand, watching her fade, and there was nothing he could do. Not a damn thing. Just watch.

And now people he cared about were in danger. This time it wasn't some disease he couldn't fight. This time it was him. He was the problem.

The email's words burned in his skull. Have the human come to Office 412.

Alone.

The word sat there. Waiting.

---

# Chapter 20: Cody's Choice

The word sat there. Waiting.

*Alone.*

Cody stared at the rusted pipes running along the ceiling. The cot's canvas dug into his back. The email kept circling, the same words wearing a groove into his skull: *Academic standing. Scholarship review. Have the human come to Office 412.*

He already knew what he was going to do. He'd known since Lukas pulled the door shut and left him here with three short knocks and two long. Maybe he'd known before that, when he saw Gem's face in the blue light of her terminal, the fear she was trying to hide underneath the anger. The logic was simple, the kind of calculation his pattern brain did without being asked. Dean wanted the human. Gem and Lukas were in the crosshairs because they'd helped the human. Remove the human from the equation, and the crosshairs had nothing to aim at.

*If I go, he leaves them alone.*

He sat up, the canvas groaning. The deadline had been five o'clock. It was past seven now. Whatever Graves was going to do, he was already doing it, and every minute Cody sat here hiding was another minute the people who'd helped him were paying for it.

And maybe, if he played it right, Graves would make good on that offer from this morning. *A way home.* The words surfaced unbidden, and Cody let himself turn them over. Back to the apartment with the stained carpet and the empty fridge. Back to his father's hollow eyes and the bottles lined up like soldiers on the kitchen counter. Back to the bus stop and the community college and the C-minus life he'd been sleepwalking through before the portal swallowed him whole. It wasn't much of a life. He'd hated most of it. But it was his, and going back to it meant Gem kept her research and Lukas kept his future and nobody else had to bleed for the mistake of caring about Cody Guiles.

Four days. That's all it had been. Four days of someone making him coffee in the morning. Four days of walking next to someone who didn't flinch when their shoulders brushed. Four days of almost saying something on a bench under the stars before a phone buzzed and stole the moment. And now he was going to trade all of it for a drunk father and a dead mother's grave and the same empty routine he'd been drowning in before.

It should have felt like a sacrifice. It felt like coming home to a house that had already burned down.

Cody crossed to the door and put his hand on the cold metal. The rust was rough under his palm. He thought about Lukas coming back to find the room empty, the cot still warm, no answer to the knock. He thought about the look on Lukas's face when he figured out where Cody had gone.

He pushed the door open anyway.

---

The tunnels swallowed him the same way they had before, pipes running overhead like exposed veins, caged bulbs throwing pools of yellow light every twenty feet with long stretches of shadow between. Cody moved fast, his footsteps echoing off the concrete walls, the smell of rust and damp filling his nose. He didn't know these tunnels the way Lukas did, but he remembered enough from the walk down to find his way back to the surface.

A set of metal stairs brought him up through a service entrance behind the library, and then he was outside, the cold night air hitting his face like a slap. The campus was quiet at this hour, most of the students either in their dorms or gathered somewhere with better lighting and warmer company. A few lampposts threw circles of orange light across the brick paths, and the trees stood bare and dark against a sky that couldn't decide if it wanted to be cloudy or clear.

Whitmore Hall sat at the far end of the quad, the same brick and ivy he'd walked into this morning when everything still felt like it might be okay. He knew the route now: front doors, past the reception desk, old elevator to the fourth floor, carpeted hallway to the door at the end. He'd done it once already today, following a note that promised answers. Now he was doing it again, following a threat that promised consequences.

He kept his head down as he crossed the quad, hands in his pockets, trying to look like any other student heading somewhere boring. A pair of rabbits passed him going the other way, arguing about a group project, and neither of them gave him a second glance. A raccoon in a maintenance uniform was fiddling with something near one of the lampposts, and Cody felt the guy's eyes on him for a second before he looked away. Probably nothing. Probably just some guy on the night shift. But the back of his neck prickled anyway.

The front doors of Whitmore Hall were unlocked, the lobby quiet and empty except for the same bored-looking badger behind the reception desk. The badger glanced up when Cody walked in, and this time there was no flicker of confusion, no moment of processing. Just recognition, and something that might have been expectation.

"He's waiting for you," the badger said. "Fourth floor."

Cody didn't ask how he knew. He just nodded and walked past the desk, feeling the badger's eyes on his back the whole way. The elevator was an old one, the kind with a metal grate that had to be pulled closed by hand, and it groaned and shuddered as it climbed. Fourth floor. The hallway was carpeted, muffling his footsteps, the walls lined with framed photos of past deans and graduating classes going back decades, everyone smiling, everyone looking successful and happy and like they belonged here.

Office 412 was at the end of the hall, heavy wooden door, brass nameplate: *Victor Graves, Ph.D. ‚Äî Dean of Academic Affairs.*

The door was already open. Just like this morning. *My door is always open.*

Cody stopped at the threshold. Through the gap, he could see the edge of the window, the campus lights glittering below, the Dean's silhouette already turned toward the door. Waiting. He'd known Cody was coming. Of course he had. The window faced the quad. He'd probably watched Cody cross the whole way, counting his footsteps, timing his arrival.

"Come in, Cody." The voice was soft, warm, carrying that same friendly-uncle tone from this morning. "I was hoping you'd change your mind."

Cody stepped inside.

---

The office looked exactly the same as it had this morning.

Floor-to-ceiling windows with the campus spread out below. Family photos on the shelves: the silver-furred lynx with kind eyes, the graduation robes, the vacation shot with mountains. Ribbon-cutting photos on the walls, the thank-you card from the Class of 2019, the crayon drawing taped to the bookshelf like it meant something. The mahogany desk with the worn spot where paws rested, the "World's Okayest Dean" mug, the bowl of wrapped butterscotch candies catching the light.

The whole performance, unchanged. The mask made of furniture and frames.

This morning, Cody's pattern brain had flagged all of it: the dusty frames that hadn't been moved in years, the photos positioned outward instead of toward the desk, the candy bowl that was always the same amount. He'd walked out of here with doubt planted in his chest, wondering if the people protecting him were really protecting him or just keeping him scared. Now he was back, and nothing had changed. The room was still a stage. The props were still in place. The only difference was that this time, Cody knew he was walking into a trap.

Dean Victor Graves sat behind the desk in his charcoal suit, pale blue eyes taking in everything without giving anything back. He gestured to the chair across from him, and the smile on his face was warm, almost relieved, like a father seeing his kid come home after curfew.

The two chairs faced the desk, positioned so visitors sat backlit against the windows. Cody had noticed it this morning, the way Graves controlled the light, and he noticed it again now as he lowered himself into the left chair. The question was whether Graves knew that Cody knew.

"I have to admit, I wondered how long you'd wait," Graves said. "After our conversation this morning, I thought you might need more time. But here you are." He spread his paws, that gesture of openness he'd used before. "I'm glad you didn't let pride get in the way."

"You threatened my friends."

Graves tilted his head, mild surprise crossing his face. "Threatened? Cody, I simply pointed out the potential consequences of certain actions. Miss Kindler accessed restricted archives without authorization. That's a serious academic violation. I was trying to help her understand the situation before it escalated."

"And Lukas's scholarship?"

"Mr. Raines has been making some questionable choices lately. Missing practices. Avoiding his academic advisor. Using university resources in ways that raise questions." Graves spread his paws again. "I'm not the one who decides these things, Cody. I simply wanted you to understand the position your friends have put themselves in. For your sake, not as a threat."

The reasonable tone. The framing of everything as concern, as help, as the logical position of someone who just wanted to make sure everyone was okay. This morning it had worked on him, at least partly. The doubt had taken root, and he'd spent the whole day looking at Lukas and Gem through a poisoned lens, wondering if they were protecting him or controlling him.

But that was before the email. Before the threat. Before Cody realized that all of Graves's reasonable concern had been building toward this moment.

"What do you want?" Cody asked.

"I want to help you." Graves leaned forward, his pale eyes holding Cody's gaze. "We talked about this this morning. You fell through a dimensional rift. You're alone in a world you don't understand, surrounded by people you've known for less than a week. And your friends, as well-meaning as they are, have been keeping you hidden, keeping you in the dark, making decisions for you instead of with you."

The script hadn't changed. The manipulation was the same. But this time Cody let him talk.

"I know you want to go home," Graves said, and the words landed harder than they should have. "I could see it in your face this morning when I mentioned the possibility. The way you went still. The way something flickered behind your eyes." He paused, letting that sit. "You've wanted it since the moment you arrived, haven't you? Even if you couldn't admit it to yourself."

That wasn't true. This morning, Cody hadn't known what he wanted. He'd walked out of this office more confused than when he'd walked in, doubt and hope tangled together in a knot he couldn't untie. But the way Graves said it, so certain, so knowing, made Cody wonder if he was misremembering. Maybe he had wanted to go home all along. Maybe he'd just been too scared to see it.

That was the trick, wasn't it? Telling you what you felt until you believed it.

He thought about the apartment. The stained carpet, the empty fridge, the bottles on the counter. His father's eyes that looked through him instead of at him. The bus stop in the morning cold, the classrooms where no one knew his name, the grave he visited every Sunday because it was the only conversation he had left. He'd hated that life. He'd been drowning in it. But it was his, and there was something in his chest that ached for it anyway. Or maybe Graves was just good at making him think there was.

"What if I could send you back?" Graves asked. "The portal that brought you here isn't fully understood. But with proper study, with the right resources, there might be a way to recreate the conditions. The university has archives going back over a century. Equipment. Expertise. If anyone can find a way to return you to your world, it's us."

Home. His mother's grave under the oak tree with the lightning-split trunk. The chain-link fence that never locked right. Cheap flowers in plastic wrap. Sunday mornings talking to a headstone because there was no one else left to talk to.

He could go back to that. He could leave this place, leave Lukas and Gem and the pack, leave behind four days of feeling like he might actually belong somewhere. He could trade all of it for a drunk father and an empty apartment and the same gray routine he'd been suffocating in before the sky opened up and swallowed him whole.

And Gem would keep her standing. And Lukas would keep his scholarship. And no one else would have to pay for helping Cody Guiles.

"What would I have to do?" The question came out steady. Flat. Like he'd already made up his mind.

Something flickered in Graves's eyes, there and gone. Satisfaction, maybe. The look of a fisherman feeling the first tug on the line.

"Nothing invasive," Graves said smoothly. "Some tests. Observation. Documentation of your biology, your adaptation process. We'd want to understand what made you different, what allowed you to survive when others didn't." His paws opened, palms up, the picture of reasonableness. "You'd be helping us save lives, Cody. Future arrivals who might not be as lucky as you."

And there it was. The tell.

When Graves said "observation," his claws flexed against the armrest, just once, a tiny curl of the tips into the leather. His voice stayed smooth, his face stayed warm, but his body knew what "observation" really meant. Cody's pattern brain flagged it the way it flagged a defensive lineman shifting his weight before the snap. The Dean was lying. Not about all of it, maybe, but about enough.

He'd walked into a trap. He'd known it was probably a trap, and he'd walked in anyway, and now the jaws were closing.

"Why me?" Cody asked, keeping his voice steady. "You said others came through before. What happened to them?"

Graves's smile didn't waver. "You're unique, Cody. A human who survived dimensional transit intact. Do you have any idea how rare that is? The others... didn't fare as well. But the scientific implications of your survival are extraordinary. And I want to make sure you're treated with the respect and care that your situation deserves. Not hidden in maintenance tunnels like a problem to be managed."

"And if I say no?"

The smile stayed in place, but something shifted behind the Dean's eyes. The warmth was still there, but it felt thinner now, like paint over rusted metal.

"Then I'd be very disappointed," Graves said. "And I'd worry about your friends, Cody. Miss Kindler's inquiry into restricted archives has already been flagged. Mr. Raines's scholarship is already under review. These processes move forward whether you cooperate or not. But if you work with me, if you let me help you, I can make sure those processes reach the right conclusions." He spread his paws again. "I'm not your enemy. I'm offering you a path forward. The only question is whether you're brave enough to take it."

Cody's hands had curled into fists on the armrests, and he made himself relax them. The rage was there, coiling tight behind his ribs, the same thing that had gotten him in trouble on the football field. He could feel it pushing at the edges of his control, wanting out.

He forced himself to breathe.

"I need to think about it," he said.

"Of course." Graves nodded, still smiling. "Take all the time you need." He glanced at the clock on the corner of his desk. "Although I should mention that the situation with your friends will continue to develop regardless of your decision. The wheels are already in motion. But I understand if you need a moment to process."

Cody stood up. The chair creaked beneath him, and he turned toward the door, every instinct screaming at him to get out of this room, to find Lukas, to run.

He heard the click of a lock engaging.

"I'm afraid I can't let you leave, Cody."

The voice was different. Still soft, still quiet, but the warmth had bled out of it like heat escaping through a cracked window. Cody turned back, and the man behind the desk wasn't the same man who'd welcomed him in. The smile was gone. The pale blue eyes had gone flat and cold, and his claws were visible now, curved tips pressing lightly into the leather armrest.

"You came willingly," Graves said. "That simplifies the paperwork."

"What‚Äî"

"You really thought you could read me?" The Dean's voice was clinical now, precise, every word measured and placed. "I've been doing this for forty years, Cody. I saw you cataloguing the room the moment you walked in. The photos, the positioning, the tells. You thought you were being clever. You thought your little pattern recognition trick gave you an advantage." He stood, and the movement was slow, deliberate, a predator who didn't need to hurry. "I let you see exactly what I wanted you to see. And you walked right into it."

Cody lunged for the door, but it didn't budge. The lock was electronic, something built into the frame, and the handle turned uselessly in his grip. He spun back around, and Graves was already reaching for something on his desk, a small radio that looked like it belonged in a security office.

"Two for escort," the Dean said into it. "Office 412. The subject is secured."

*Subject.* The word landed like a punch to the gut.

"You can't do this." Cody's voice came out steadier than he felt. "People know I'm here. Lukas knows‚Äî"

"Mr. Raines knows you were in a maintenance room. He'll return to find it empty. He'll search the tunnels, the dorm, the library. By the time he thinks to check my office, you'll be somewhere else entirely." Graves set the radio down and clasped his paws behind his back, watching Cody the way you'd watch a bug in a jar. "The science building has an isolation ward in the sub-basement. Room B-7. No windows. Excellent soundproofing. You'll be very comfortable there."

The rage hit him then, fast and hot, flooding up from his chest into his arms. His hands curled into fists and his vision narrowed, and for a second he was back on that football field, back in that moment when everything went red and the only thing that mattered was making someone pay. He took a step toward Graves, and his whole body was shaking with the need to do something, to fight, to make this stop.

Graves didn't flinch. He just watched, those flat blue eyes taking in every detail, and the corner of his mouth curved upward.

"There it is," he said quietly. "The stress response. I've seen it before, in the others. Something in the biology of cross-dimensional survivors, something that runs hotter than it should." He tilted his head, studying Cody the way you'd study a specimen under glass. "The last one showed the same pattern before she expired. Elevated heart rate, pupils dilated, hands shaking. We never got to document it properly. She didn't last long enough." His smile returned, thin and clinical. "You will."

The door behind Cody opened. He turned to find two security guards filling the frame, a bear and a wolf in dark uniforms, both of them built like they spent their off-hours throwing around weights. The bear was holding something that looked like a heavy-duty zip tie, the kind you'd use on luggage or industrial equipment.

"Restrain him," Graves said. "Carefully. I don't want any marks that would complicate the documentation."

Cody swung at the bear, and the punch connected with something solid before the wolf's arm hooked around his throat from behind and yanked him backward. He thrashed, kicked, tried to break free, but they were stronger than him and there were two of them and his arms were being wrenched behind his back before he could land another hit. The zip tie bit into his wrists, plastic edges digging in, and then something else was going around his ankles and he was on the ground, the carpet rough against his cheek, his breath coming in ragged gasps.

"Transport him to B-7," Graves said from somewhere above him. "I'll be along shortly to begin the intake process."

Cody felt himself being lifted, slung over the bear's shoulder like a sack of laundry, and then they were moving. Down the hallway. Into the elevator. The metal grate rattled closed, and the last thing he saw before the doors blocked his view was Dean Victor Graves standing in the doorway of Office 412, straightening his cuffs, his face as calm and empty as a frozen lake.

The elevator shuddered and began to descend.

Something pricked the side of his neck. The wolf's paw, holding a syringe. Cody tried to jerk away but there was nowhere to go, and the bear's grip tightened, pinning him in place while the plunger went down.

"Standard sedative," the wolf said to no one in particular. "He'll be out in thirty seconds."

The fluorescent lights in the elevator ceiling started to blur. Cody blinked, trying to hold on, trying to stay awake, but his thoughts were already going soft at the edges, dissolving like sugar in water. The last thing he felt was the bear's shoulder digging into his stomach, and the last thing he heard was the elevator grinding to a stop somewhere far below.

Then nothing.

---

---

# Chapter 21: Room B-7

The first thing Cody noticed was the cold.

It seeped through his clothes, through his skin, settling somewhere deep in his bones like it had always been there and he was just now waking up to it. His head felt stuffed with cotton, his thoughts moving slow and thick, and when he tried to open his eyes the fluorescent light above him hit like a camera flash set off two inches from his face.

He tried to move his arm. It didn't move.

The restraint around his wrist was padded, medical-grade, the kind you'd see in a hospital psych ward. His other wrist had one too, and when he tried to shift his legs he found the same thing at his ankles. He was lying on something flat and hard, slightly reclined, and the surface under him was cold metal covered by a thin layer of paper that crinkled when he moved. An examination table. The kind a doctor would use for a physical, except doctors didn't usually strap you down first.

His mouth tasted like pennies and his throat was dry. They'd given him something. In the elevator, maybe, or right after. He couldn't remember. Everything after the guards hauled him out of Office 412 was a blur of motion and voices and then nothing.

He blinked until his eyes adjusted, and the room came into focus around him. Maybe fifteen feet square, walls painted the same institutional beige you'd find in a DMV waiting room. No windows. One door, heavy and metal, with a small reinforced glass panel at eye level and an electronic keypad on the wall beside it. A camera mounted in the corner near the ceiling, red light blinking steady. Equipment lined the walls: a heart monitor on a rolling stand, a tray of instruments covered with a blue cloth, some kind of machine with dials and readouts that looked like it belonged in a Cold War bunker. The air smelled like disinfectant and something else underneath, chemical and sharp, making his nose itch.

Cody's pattern brain did its thing automatically, cataloguing details whether he wanted it to or not. The door opened inward. The keypad had a three-second delay after the beep before the lock engaged, he could hear it cycling when footsteps passed in the hallway outside. The camera covered most of the room but had a blind spot near the instrument tray. The ventilation grate in the ceiling was maybe eight inches wide, bolted in place, way too small for a person but big enough to carry sound. He could hear the hum of machinery somewhere above him, the building breathing.

None of it mattered. He was strapped to a table in a soundproofed basement and nobody knew where he was.

The word came back to him, landing with the same weight it had in Graves's office. *Subject.* That's what he was now. A subject. Something to be documented and studied and eventually filed away when they were done with him. Not Cody Guiles from nowhere Pennsylvania with a dead mother and a drunk father and a pattern brain that couldn't stop noticing things. A specimen on a table.

He'd walked right into it.

He made himself breathe. Slow. Steady. The way his old coach had taught him before games, back when football was still something he cared about. If he looked at the restraints too long he could feel the panic starting to build in his chest, that animal urge to thrash and pull and tear until something gave. So he stared at the ceiling tiles instead, at the water stains in one corner spreading outward like tree rings counting the years since anyone bothered to fix the leak.

Dean Graves had played him. Every step of it, from the first conversation in Office 412 to the email to the moment Cody pushed open that valve room door, all of it had been exactly what Graves wanted. The reasonable tone, the concern, the offer of help. The doubt he'd planted about Lukas and Gem, making Cody look at the people protecting him through a lens of suspicion. The threat to their futures, delivered with such calm professionalism that it didn't even sound like a threat.

And Cody had done exactly what Graves predicted. He'd let his wound drive him. *People get hurt when I'm around.* The same thought that had followed him since his mother's hospital bed, since his father started disappearing into bottles, since his best friend crossed the street to avoid him. He'd taken that wound and let Graves use it as a lever, prying him out of the valve room and straight into the trap.

The sacrifice wasn't noble. It was stupid. It was exactly what a manipulator would expect from someone who believed they were poison to everyone around them.

Gem was probably still flagged for the archive access. Lukas's scholarship was probably still under review. Cody had traded his freedom for nothing, and now he was strapped to a table in a basement while a snow leopard with forty years of practice at this game decided what to do with him.

His hands curled into fists against the restraints, and he felt that familiar pressure building behind his ribs again. The anger wanted out. It always wanted out when he felt trapped, cornered, powerless. But losing control now wouldn't help anyone.

He let it burn. But he didn't let it take over. Not yet.

Because his brain didn't stop working just because he was strapped to a table. And somewhere in the back of his skull, behind the panic and the anger, the pattern recognition was still running.

---

The door beeped and swung inward, and Dean Victor Graves walked into Room B-7 like he was entering his own living room.

He'd changed out of the charcoal suit. Now he wore a white lab coat over a simple button-down, sleeves rolled to the elbows, reading glasses perched on his nose. The costume of a researcher rather than an administrator. He was carrying a tablet in one paw and a coffee mug in the other, steam curling up from whatever was inside, and he moved to the instrument tray without looking at Cody first. Set the mug down. Tapped at the screen. Checked one of the machines along the wall.

When he finally turned to face the table, the mask was gone. The smile, the warmth, all of it stripped away, leaving nothing but calm, clinical interest, the way a mechanic might look at an engine he was about to take apart.

"You're awake," Graves said. "Good. The sedative dosage was estimated based on your body weight. I wasn't certain how your physiology would process it."

Cody's throat was still dry, but he made himself speak anyway. "Let me go."

Graves didn't even acknowledge the words. He pulled a rolling stool from under the instrument tray and sat down, positioning himself at Cody's eye level the way a vet might sit beside a dog on an examination table. "I'll explain what's happening so you can process it. Understanding tends to reduce stress responses in subjects, and I want clean data."

"I know what's happening. You're a psychopath who kidnaps people."

Something flickered across Graves's face, there and gone. Mild disappointment, maybe, the way a teacher might look at a student who gave the wrong answer. "I'm a scientist, Cody. I've spent forty years studying dimensional anomalies, portal events, and the biological implications of cross-dimensional transit. When I started this work, my colleagues called me a crackpot. A fringe theorist chasing shadows. I published papers that were rejected by every major journal in my field. I sat through faculty meetings where people made jokes about my research while pretending I couldn't hear them."

He paused, adjusting the tablet on his knee, scrolling through data Cody couldn't read from this angle.

"Fourteen years ago, a portal event occurred on this campus. A human subject came through, similar to you. TH-2010-003, that was the designation. Female, early twenties, severely disoriented upon arrival. She survived the initial transit, which was remarkable in itself. The biological stress of dimensional crossing usually kills within hours. But she lasted three days." Graves looked up from the tablet, those pale blue eyes holding Cody's gaze. "Three days of observation before her system failed. Not enough time to complete the documentation. Not enough data to prove anything."

Cody felt something cold slide down his spine that had nothing to do with the room temperature.

"You're going to last longer," Graves said. "You've already lasted longer. Four days and counting, with no signs of systemic breakdown. Your cellular structure has adapted to this dimension's baseline reality in ways I've never seen before. Do you understand what that means?"

"That I'm a person?" The words came out harder than Cody intended, sharp and raw. "Not a lab rat?"

Graves tilted his head, considering that. "You're both, actually. You're a person who happens to be the most significant scientific specimen to appear in my lifetime. Your biology could unlock answers to questions no one has even learned to ask yet. Portal mechanics. Dimensional stability. The nature of reality itself." He leaned forward slightly. "You're going to make history, Cody. My history. When I publish these findings, when the data is undeniable, every journal that rejected me will be begging to print my name. Every colleague who laughed will have to cite my work in their own papers. I've waited forty years for vindication. And here you are."

The camera in the corner was still blinking. Recording everything. Graves didn't even glance at it. Why would he? This was his building, his basement, his kingdom. No one was coming to review the footage. No one even knew this room existed.

"What are you going to do to me?" Cody asked.

"Document you." Graves stood, tucking the tablet under his arm. "Blood work, tissue samples, neurological scans. We'll monitor your cellular adaptation over time, track any anomalies in your physiology. I have equipment here that most universities can't afford, legacy systems from research programs that were quietly defunded when the government decided portal science wasn't worth pursuing." He walked to one of the machines along the wall, running his paw along its surface with what looked almost like affection. "Tomorrow we'll begin the intake protocols. Tonight, you rest. The sedative will be out of your system by morning, and I want clean baselines for the initial readings."

He gathered his coffee mug and moved toward the door, then paused with his paw on the keypad.

"I know you think I'm a monster," he said, not turning around. "I don't expect you to understand what this work means. You're twenty years old. You've never dedicated your life to something, never sacrificed everything for a truth no one else could see. But someday, when the textbooks are written and the history is settled, people will know that Victor Graves was right. That his research changed the world." He punched in the code, and the door beeped. "Get some rest, Cody. We have a lot of work ahead of us."

The door swung shut behind him. The lock cycled with a heavy click.

Cody lay there in the fluorescent buzz, staring at the ceiling, running the variables without meaning to.

Graves hadn't rushed. Hadn't checked the time or mentioned any deadline. He'd talked like a man who had all the time in the world, savoring the moment like a meal he'd been waiting forty years to eat. A scientist with his specimen, settling in for the long haul.

The door opened again maybe twenty minutes later. The wolf guard who'd helped wrestle Cody to the ground in the office stuck his head in, did a visual check, grunted, and pulled the door shut. Cody heard him talking to someone in the hallway, the words muffled but audible through the gap before the door sealed.

"Radio's worthless down here. Gotta walk all the way to the goddamn stairwell just to check in."

A second voice, deeper, probably the bear. "At least we're not dealing with that busted fire door. Thing's been on the maintenance list since I started."

"Right next to that service access in storage. When's the last time anyone even opened that thing?"

The bear laughed, a low rumble. "Probably before we were born. Come on, I need coffee."

Footsteps receding down the hallway. Then silence.

Cody let the words settle into the back of his skull, filing them away automatically. Radio dead zone. Fire door on the maintenance list. Service access in storage that nobody ever used.

He didn't know where the storage room was. He didn't know where the service access led. He didn't know if Lukas and Gem even knew he was gone yet, or if they'd have any way to find him even if they did. The pack might already be scrambling, or they might think he just ran off. He had no way to communicate, no way to send a message, no way to do anything except lie here and wait.

But his pattern brain kept working anyway, because that's what it did. It took the pieces and arranged them, looking for the shape underneath.

Radio dead zone meant the guards couldn't call for backup from down here. They had to physically walk to the stairwell to check in. That was a window. Maybe thirty seconds, maybe a minute, depending on how far away the stairwell was.

Service access in storage. A back way. Something old and forgotten, probably connected to the same tunnel network that ran under the whole campus. The tunnels Lukas knew better than anyone.

If they came for him. If they found this place. If everything lined up just right.

He'd know what to do.

Cody tested the restraints one more time, feeling the padded edges dig into his wrists. No give. No slack. He wasn't going anywhere under his own power.

But he wasn't helpless either. He had information. He had a brain that wouldn't stop working. And he had one more thing, too, one thing he hadn't had back home in that apartment with the stained carpet and the empty fridge.

He had people who might actually come for him.

The fluorescent light buzzed overhead. The camera blinked its steady red. Somewhere above him, the building breathed through its vents, and Cody lay there in the cold, running the variables, waiting for a moment that might never come.

But if it did, he'd be ready.

---

---

# Chapter 22: The Extraction

Time had stopped meaning anything.

Cody lay on the examination table, staring at the same water-stained ceiling tiles he'd been looking at for what felt like forever. The fluorescent light buzzed overhead, the camera blinked its steady red, and the restraints held his wrists and ankles in the same padded grip they'd held him in since he woke up. Nothing had changed. Nothing was going to change. Graves had left hours ago, or maybe it had been minutes, and the guards had checked on him twice, or maybe three times, and somewhere along the way Cody had stopped counting.

His pattern brain kept running anyway, cycling through the same information like a song stuck on repeat. Radio dead zone. Service access in storage. Guards have to walk to the stairwell to call for backup. The pieces were there, arranged and ready, but they didn't matter if no one came to use them. And with every hour that passed, the possibility that anyone was coming at all felt thinner and thinner, like a balloon slowly losing air.

He thought about Lukas coming back to the valve room and finding it empty. The cot still warm. The door unlocked. He thought about the look on Lukas's face when he realized what Cody had done, and whether that look was angry or hurt or just exhausted. He thought about Gem and Benji and Thorne and the rest of the pack, and whether they even knew he was gone, and whether knowing would make them come running or just make them shake their heads and move on with their lives.

Maybe this was it. Maybe he'd finally found the place where his pattern of driving people away ended, because there was nowhere left to drive them. He was strapped to a table in a basement that didn't exist on any map, and the only person who knew exactly where he was had no intention of letting him leave.

The fluorescent light buzzed overhead while the camera blinked its steady red, and Cody closed his eyes and tried not to think about anything at all.

---

The footsteps woke him up.

He hadn't meant to fall asleep, but the exhaustion had crept in somewhere between the third and fourth hour, dragging him down into a shallow, dreamless dark. Now his eyes snapped open and his whole body went rigid, straining against the restraints as he tried to figure out what had changed.

Footsteps in the hallway. That wasn't new. The guards checked on him regularly, heavy boots on concrete, the same rhythm every time. But these footsteps were different. Too many of them, moving too fast, and the rhythm was all wrong, like someone had kicked over a drum set in the middle of a song.

Then the shouting started.

"Hey! You can't..."

The voice cut off with a heavy thud, the sound of something large hitting the floor, and Cody's heart slammed into his ribs hard enough to hurt. He pulled against the restraints, craning his neck toward the door, trying to see through the small reinforced window. Shadows moved in the hallway. Another impact, another grunt of pain, and then footsteps again, closer now, almost running.

The keypad beeped.

The door swung open.

Lukas stood in the frame, chest heaving, fur disheveled, eyes wild and searching until they landed on Cody. For a second neither of them moved. Relief and anger and fear all tangled together on Lukas's face, and then he was across the room in three long strides, paws gripping the sides of the examination table, pulling Cody into a hug that bent him awkwardly against the restraints.

"You stupid..." Lukas's voice cracked, and he couldn't finish the sentence. His arms tightened, his muzzle pressed against Cody's shoulder, and Cody could feel him shaking. Whether it was adrenaline or emotion or both, he couldn't tell.

"Lukas..."

"I know." Lukas pulled back, and his eyes were wet but his jaw was set, the anger winning out over the relief. "I know. We're gonna talk about this later. Right now we move."

A massive shape filled the doorway behind him. Thorne, shoulders barely fitting through the frame, paws taped and ready. He stepped over the unconscious guard in the hallway without looking down and moved straight to the examination table, claws working at the buckles on Cody's restraints with surprising precision for paws that size. He didn't say anything, didn't need to, just met Cody's eyes once with a brief nod, and then the first restraint came loose and blood rushed back into Cody's wrist with a pins-and-needles burn.

"How did you..." Cody started.

"Later." Lukas was watching the door, ears swiveling toward the hallway. "Can you walk?"

Cody flexed his ankles as Thorne freed them, testing the feeling. His legs were stiff and his muscles ached from hours of lying in the same position, but the sedative had worn off a long time ago. "Yeah. Yeah, I can walk."

"Good." Lukas grabbed his arm and hauled him upright. The room tilted for a second, Cody's vision going gray at the edges, but he blinked it away and steadied himself against the edge of the table. Lukas was already moving toward the door. "Let's go."

They moved into the hallway, Thorne leading. The guard on the floor was the wolf, out cold, a bruise already forming on the side of his muzzle. Thorne stepped over him like he was stepping over a crack in the sidewalk, and Lukas kept one paw on Cody's arm, steadying him as they walked.

They made it maybe twenty feet before Cody heard it.

Footsteps. Coming from the direction they were heading. More than one set, moving fast.

Thorne stopped. Lukas stopped. They exchanged a look that said everything without words: the way they'd come in was blocked.

"Wait!" Cody's voice came out rough, but steady. "There's another way."

Lukas turned to him. "What?"

"Storage room." Cody pointed down the hallway, toward the end where it curved out of sight. "End of the hall. There's a service access that connects to the tunnels. And the radios don't work down here. The guards have to walk all the way to the stairwell to call for backup." He met Lukas's eyes. "That's our window."

"How do you know that?"

There was something in Lukas's voice Cody had never heard before. Surprise, maybe, the kind that comes when the person you've been protecting suddenly has the answer you need.

Cody felt his spine straighten. He'd been strapped to that table for hours, helpless, useless, and the whole time his brain had been doing what it always did. Gathering pieces. Finding patterns. Waiting for a moment that might never come. But the moment was here now, and he had an answer to give.

"I listened."

For a second Lukas just stared at him. Then he nodded once, sharp and decisive. "Lead the way."

Cody moved.

His legs protested with every step, muscles cramping and joints popping after hours of immobility, but he pushed through it because there wasn't any other option. The hallway stretched ahead of him, fluorescent lights throwing harsh shadows, doors on either side labeled with numbers and letters that meant nothing to him. Behind him he could hear Lukas's breathing, quick and controlled, and Thorne's heavier footfalls, steady as a heartbeat.

The storage room was exactly where the guards had said it would be, a heavy metal door with a faded sign that read MAINTENANCE: AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY. Cody tried the handle, but it was locked.

Thorne shouldered past him without breaking stride. One massive paw gripped the handle, the other braced against the frame, and with a single sharp motion the lock gave way with a crack like a branch snapping. The door swung inward, revealing a cramped room full of shelves and cleaning supplies and, in the far corner, a metal hatch set into the floor.

"There." Cody crossed to the hatch and dropped to his knees, fingers finding the handle. It was old and rusted, the kind of thing that hadn't been touched in years, and when he pulled it groaned in protest but swung open, revealing a ladder descending into darkness.

The smell hit him immediately: rust and damp and old concrete. The tunnels. The same network that ran under the whole campus, the ones Lukas had led him through on his first day here.

"Go," Lukas said. "I'm right behind you."

Cody went. The ladder rungs were cold under his palms, slick with condensation, and he climbed down into the dark as fast as he dared. His feet hit solid ground and he moved aside, making room as Lukas dropped down after him, then Thorne, the big bear somehow fitting through the hatch despite his shoulders.

Above them, voices echoed in the storage room. The guards had found the broken door.

"Move," Thorne said, the first word he'd spoken since they'd found Cody. His voice was low and calm, like they were walking to practice instead of running for their lives.

They moved.

The tunnels swallowed them whole, pipes running overhead, caged bulbs throwing pools of yellow light every thirty feet or so. Cody didn't know this section the way he knew the route between the valve room and the library, but the layout was familiar, the same industrial bones running under the whole campus. He picked a direction and ran, trusting his pattern brain to find the way out, and behind him Lukas and Thorne kept pace, their footsteps echoing off the concrete walls.

They ran for what felt like forever but was probably only a few minutes, the tunnel branching and splitting, Cody making choices based on instinct and the faint movement of air that suggested an exit somewhere ahead. His lungs burned and his legs screamed and his heart pounded so hard he could feel it in his teeth, but he didn't stop, couldn't stop, because stopping meant getting caught and getting caught meant going back to that table and he was never going back to that table again.

The tunnel ended at a set of metal stairs leading up.

Cody took them two at a time, shoulder slamming into the door at the top, and then he was outside, cold night air flooding his lungs, stars scattered across a sky that had never looked so beautiful. He stumbled forward, almost falling, catching himself on a chain-link fence as Lukas and Thorne burst out behind him.

They were in a parking lot. Small, tucked behind a row of trees, the kind of overflow lot that probably only got used during football games. And sitting in the middle of it, engine idling, headlights off, was Lukas's truck.

Gem was behind the wheel, her face pale in the dashboard glow. Benji was in the back seat, laptop closed on his knees, watching the tree line with an intensity Cody had never seen from him before.

Gem saw them coming and her whole body sagged with relief. She didn't say anything, just reached back and shoved the rear door open as they sprinted toward the truck.

They piled in fast. Thorne took the front passenger seat, somehow folding his bulk into the space. Lukas slid into the back with Cody and Benji, pulling the door shut behind him. The doors slammed and Gem had the truck moving before anyone could catch their breath, pulling out of the lot slow and quiet, no lights, gravel crunching under the tires.

Nobody spoke.

The campus slid past the windows, dark buildings and empty paths, and then they were on the road, trees closing in on either side, the headlights finally clicking on as they put distance between themselves and the science building.

Cody sat in the back seat, lungs still heaving, hands shaking. Lukas was pressed against his side, warm and solid, and Cody found himself staring out the window without really seeing anything. They'd come for him. They'd actually come for him. He'd walked into a trap like an idiot and they'd come anyway, busted through guards and broken down doors and followed him into the dark.

Benji shifted beside him, and when Cody glanced over, the hedgehog's face was uncharacteristically serious.

"Pack protects pack," Benji said quietly.

Something cracked in Cody's chest. He tried to respond, tried to find words that would fit the weight of what they'd just done for him, but his throat closed up and his eyes burned and all he could do was dip his head once. He turned toward the window so no one would see his face, watching the road disappear behind them, and let himself breathe for the first time in hours.

The truck's engine hummed as the trees flew past, and somewhere behind them Room B-7 sat empty.

---

---


---

# Chapter 23: The Cabin

The turnoff was easy to miss if you didn't know where to look. Dirt road, no sign, trees closing in on both sides. Gem took it slow, the truck bouncing over ruts, headlights cutting through the pre-dawn dark. Nobody had said much since they left campus. Thorne was a solid wall of silence in the front passenger seat, and in the back Benji had his laptop closed on his knees with his head tipped against the window. Lukas sat pressed against Cody's side, close enough that their shoulders touched every time the truck hit a bump, but he hadn't said a word since they'd piled in.

Then the trees opened up and there it was.

The cabin sat in a clearing, backed up against a ridge. Single story, wood siding that had weathered to gray, a porch with two chairs that looked like they hadn't been sat in for months. Lukas's dad and uncle had built the place when he was a kid, and even though the Raines family hadn't used it much in the last few years, Lukas still came out to do upkeep every spring. Twenty minutes from campus, far enough to feel like another world.

Gem killed the engine. Out here, silence meant crickets and wind through the pines, and somewhere nearby the faint sound of running water.

Thorne opened his door and the cold rushed in, which was enough to get them moving. Lukas grabbed a bag from the truck bed and headed for the porch, crouching down to pull a key from under the third plank on the left. The door swung open with a creak that echoed in the quiet.

Inside smelled like dust and old wood. Lukas hit the light switch and a single bulb flickered on, casting yellow light across the main room. Not much to see: a couch older than Lukas, worn soft in the middle, a small kitchen along the back wall, a door on the left that led to the bedroom, another to the bathroom. Thorne moved toward the couch and started pulling cushions off to check the frame, already thinking about where everyone would sleep. Benji collapsed into the nearest chair like his strings had been cut. Gem stood in the middle of the room, looking around, then headed for the kitchen and started opening cabinets.

"Not much here," she said, closing the last one. Dust and a few ancient cans. It was the first thing anyone had said in twenty minutes.

Cody stayed near the door, not sure where to put himself. The cabin was safe, he knew that. Off campus, out of Graves's reach, hidden in the county somewhere between Timberwood and nowhere. But his body hadn't caught up yet. His hands still wanted to shake, his chest still tight. Every time he closed his eyes he saw fluorescent lights and felt the cold of that metal table against his back.

"Hey."

Lukas was standing by the bedroom door, one paw braced against the frame. He jerked his head toward the back of the cabin. "Come here a minute."

Cody followed him into a small room with a window facing the trees. A bed with a quilt that looked handmade, a dresser with a lamp on it, nothing else. Lukas closed the door behind them and stood there for a second, not saying anything, just looking at Cody with those amber eyes that always seemed to see more than Cody wanted them to.

"You're an idiot," Lukas said finally.

The word landed like a slap, but Cody didn't flinch. He'd been calling himself worse for hours. "I know."

"You walked right into his office. Alone. After everything we told you."

Cody's chest ached. He could hear the hurt underneath the anger, could see it in the tight line of Lukas's shoulders. "I know."

"He could've killed you." Lukas's voice cracked on the last word, and he turned away, one paw running over his muzzle like he was trying to hold himself together. "He had you strapped to a table, Cody. Do you get that? We didn't know where you were. We didn't know if you were alive. Benji had to hack into a security system older than my truck just to find out which building they'd taken you to, and the whole time I kept thinkin'..." He stopped, shook his head. "Don't matter what I was thinkin'."

"It does matter."

Lukas went still. Cody could see the muscles in his jaw working, the way his paws curled at his sides.

"No, it don't, because you're here now and you're fine and that's what matters." Lukas faced him again, and his eyes were wet but his jaw was set, anger and relief fighting for control of his face. "But you don't get to do that again. You hear me? You don't get to make that call alone. Not anymore."

"He was going to hurt Gem," Cody said. "He was going to take your scholarship. Because of me. Because I'm here."

"So you just handed yourself over? That was your plan?"

"I thought if I went, he'd leave you alone. All of you. I thought..." Cody's throat tightened, and he had to look away. "I thought I was protecting you."

The room went quiet. Outside, a bird started up somewhere in the trees, the first sound of morning. Cody stared at the floor and waited for Lukas to tell him how stupid that was, how naive, how he should have known better.

Instead, Lukas said, "That ain't how this works."

Cody lifted his head.

"You don't get to trade yourself for us," Lukas said. His voice had gone softer, the anger bleeding out of it, leaving something rawer underneath. "That ain't protection. That's just you decidin' you matter less than we do, and that ain't true. You hear me?"

"Lukas..." Cody's voice broke on the name. He wanted to argue, wanted to explain that this was different, that he'd done the math and it made sense at the time. But standing here with Lukas looking at him like that, all he could feel was how wrong he'd been.

"It ain't true." Lukas crossed the room in two steps and grabbed Cody's shoulders, not hard, just firm enough to make him meet his eyes. "You're pack now. That means we protect each other. Not you throwin' yourself on the fire so the rest of us stay warm. That ain't how it works. That ain't ever gonna be how it works."

Part of him wanted to push back. To explain that this was what he did, what he'd always done, that people got hurt when he stuck around too long and leaving first was the only way to keep the damage contained. But the words wouldn't come. His eyes burned and his throat felt like someone had their fist around it, and all he could do was stand there and let Lukas hold him in place.

"Okay," he managed. "Okay."

Lukas's grip loosened, but he didn't let go. For a long moment they just stood there, close enough that Cody could feel the heat coming off Lukas's fur, could hear the steady rhythm of his breathing. Then Lukas leaned forward and pressed his forehead against Cody's, just for a second, a gesture that seemed older than words.

"Get some sleep," Lukas said quietly. "We'll figure out the rest later."

Early morning light was already creeping through the window. In the main room, Cody could hear Thorne settling onto the couch, the old frame groaning under his weight. Benji muttering something as he curled into the armchair. Gem's footsteps padding toward the kitchen table, then stopping.

Lukas pulled back, but not far. "Bed's yours. I'll take the floor."

"Lukas, you don't have to..."

"Ain't askin'." Lukas grabbed a spare blanket from the dresser and dropped it on the worn rug beside the bed. "Go on. Lay down before you fall down."

Cody didn't have the energy to argue. He sat on the edge of the mattress, then let himself tip back onto the handmade quilt. The ceiling was plain white, cracked in one corner. Beside him, he heard Lukas settle onto the floor, the blanket rustling as he got comfortable.

*You're pack now.*

It was such a simple thing to say. Four words. But lying here in the thin morning light, with Lukas's breathing slowing into something like sleep just a few feet away, Cody finally understood what it meant. Not just words. Not just sentiment. These people had broken into a building and fought through guards and followed him into the dark, and they'd done it because he was theirs and they were his and that was just how it worked.

Outside, the birds were getting louder as the sun climbed higher. Somewhere nearby, the creek made a sound like a whisper through the trees.

Cody closed his eyes, and this time the darkness was just darkness, and he let it take him.


---

# Chapter 24: What We Carry

Cody woke to the smell of food and the sound of paper crinkling. For a second he didn't know where he was. The ceiling was wrong, the light coming from the wrong direction, and his body ached in places he didn't remember having. Then it came back: the cabin, the extraction, Lukas on the floor beside the bed. The floor was empty now, blanket folded and set on the dresser. He sat up slowly, wincing at the stiffness in his shoulders, and followed the smell out into the main room.

The pack was gathered around the small kitchen table, which was covered in white deli paper and wrapped sandwiches. Thorne was already halfway through something that looked like it weighed about two pounds, and Benji had his laptop open while picking at a bag of chips. Gem sat with a sandwich unwrapped in front of her, making notes in a small notebook. Lukas leaned against the counter, arms crossed, watching the door Cody had just come through.

"Sleeping beauty rises," Benji said without looking up.

Cody's stomach growled loud enough that Thorne's ears twitched. He hadn't eaten since lunch with Lukas yesterday, and that felt like it belonged to a different lifetime. "Where'd all this come from?"

"Deli about ten minutes down the road," Gem said. "Thorne made a run while you were out."

Cody looked at the spread: turkey, roast beef, something with peppers. His mouth watered. "You got hoagies?"

Benji's quills twitched. "Hoagies. That's such a coastal thing to say."

"It's what they're called."

"They're called subs. Or sandwiches. Like normal mammals."

"Normal mammals are wrong." Cody grabbed the roast beef and unwrapped it, not bothering to sit down. The first bite hit him harder than he expected, and he had to close his eyes for a second just to remember what real food tasted like.

Lukas moved to stand beside him, close enough that their shoulders almost touched. Cody looked up to find those amber eyes watching him, checking. He gave a small nod. *I'm okay. Or I will be.* Lukas's tail did a single slow wag, barely visible, and he turned back to lean against the counter.

They ate. The cabin was quiet except for chewing and the occasional chip bag crinkle. Cody glanced at the kitchen cabinets, which were bare except for dust and a few ancient cans. The deli run made sense. There was nothing here to work with. After a few minutes, Gem set down her pen and turned to Benji. "Show them what you got."

Benji wiped his paws on his jeans and turned the laptop so everyone could see. "Okay, so. You all know I got into the science building's security system. Fifteen-year-old protocols, default passwords, the whole thing's held together with duct tape and wishful thinking." He clicked through a few windows. "But here's the part that matters." The screen showed grainy footage, a timestamp in the corner. A room Cody recognized instantly: beige walls, metal table, that red blinking light in the upper corner that he'd stared at for hours while strapped down and trying not to panic.

"B-7's camera was recording the whole time," Benji said, his voice losing some of its usual bounce. "Everything Graves said to you, Cody. I have all of it."

Cody watched himself on the screen, head turned toward that camera he'd memorized every detail of. Then Graves walked into frame, lab coat crisp, that patient smile already in place. The audio crackled but came through clear enough.


His sandwich turned to chalk in his mouth. He set it down. The room went still as the recording played. Cody couldn't look at the others, so he stared at the wood grain of the table while his own voice came through the speakers, thin and scared, asking questions that got calm non-answers. When Graves mentioned TH-2010-003, the female subject who lasted three days, Gem made a soft sound. When he talked about journals begging to print his name, Thorne's claws scraped against the table.

The footage ended. Benji closed the laptop carefully, like it might bite him. For a long moment nobody spoke.

"I cut the feed before Lukas and Thorne went in," Benji said finally. "Guards never saw the extraction. As far as Graves knows, you just vanished."

Gem was leaning forward now, that sharp focus Cody had seen before locked onto the closed laptop. Her tail had gone completely still. "Benji. That footage. It's timestamped?"

"Yeah. Date, time, location metadata. The whole thing."

"And it shows his face clearly? His voice is identifiable?"

"Crystal clear. Old camera, but decent quality."

Gem sat back, recognition crossing her face like a puzzle piece clicking into place. "That's not just evidence," she said slowly. "That's a confession. Recorded. Timestamped. On university equipment, in a university building, showing a dean admitting to kidnapping and unlawful imprisonment." She glanced around the table, and for the first time since Cody had met her, Gem Kindler was almost smiling. "That's how we take him down."

Another silence settled over the table, but this one felt different. Charged instead of heavy. Then Benji's laptop made a sharp beeping sound, and he flinched hard enough to knock his chips off the table. "Shit." He grabbed for the computer, paws fumbling. "Shit, shit, come on..."

"What?" Gem's voice went sharp.

"Nothing, it's fine, just..." Benji's paws were shaking as he typed. The screen flickered, went dark, came back with an error message. "No. No no no, not now." He kept typing, faster, more frantic. The error message multiplied, cascading across the screen.

"Benji." Gem again, harder this time. "What's happening?"

"I don't know! It was fine, it was working, everything was backed up and now it's just..." He slammed his paw against the table hard enough to make the sandwiches jump. "Come ON!" The outburst hung in the air. Benji stared at the screen, shoulders hunched, breathing hard. Then all the fight drained out of him at once and he slumped back, paws over his face. "Sorry," he said, muffled. "Sorry, I just... I can't be the reason this doesn't work."

"It's probably just a glitch," Gem said. "We can fix it."

"It's not about the glitch." Benji dropped his paws. His eyes were wet. "It's about... okay, this is going to sound stupid, but I've never had this before? Like, any of this?" He gestured vaguely at the room, at all of them, his quills flattening against his head in a way Cody hadn't seen before. "People who actually show up," he continued, the words coming faster now. "People who stick around when things get hard. My whole life it's been backup plans and exit strategies because everyone leaves eventually, right? That's just how it works. You learn not to count on anyone because counting on people is how you end up alone at three AM wondering what you did wrong this time." He let out a shaky breath. "But then there's this. You guys. And it's real, and it matters, and I keep waiting for the part where it falls apart. Where I mess something up and everyone disappears. And if I screw up this one thing, this one important thing, maybe that's when it happens. Maybe that's the reason everyone finally..."

He couldn't finish. Thorne reached over and put one massive paw on Benji's shoulder, steady and solid, not saying anything.

Cody's throat tightened. He didn't know Benji well, not really, but he knew that math. He'd done it himself more times than he could count.

"You're not going to screw it up," Gem said, her voice softer than Cody had heard it before. "And even if something goes wrong, we figure it out together. That's the whole point."

Benji nodded, wiping at his eyes with the back of his paw. "Yeah. I know. I know that, I just..." He let out a shaky laugh. "Wow, okay, that was way more feelings than I planned for a Thursday. Can we pretend I said something clever instead? I have a reputation to maintain."
