"We know," Lukas said quietly from the counter. "We all know." The laptop screen flickered again, then stabilized. The error messages cleared.

"See?" Benji's voice was still rough, but steadier. "Just a glitch."

"Just a glitch," Gem agreed.

But the room felt different now. Cody could see it in the way Thorne's paw lingered on Benji's shoulder before pulling back. In the way Gem was looking at the hedgehog without her usual clinical edge. In the way Lukas had gone still, arms crossed, watching Benji with that same protective focus he usually saved for Cody. A wall had come down. Not a big one, maybe, but enough to let some light through.


They took a break after that. Cody stepped outside to clear his head, leaning against the porch railing and watching the trees sway in the afternoon breeze. The creek was louder out here, a constant murmur underneath the wind.

The door opened behind him. Gem came out, notebook still tucked under one arm, and leaned against the railing beside him.

"You holding up okay?" she asked.

Cody shrugged. "Weird question to answer. I'm not strapped to a table anymore, so that's an improvement."

"Low bar." The corner of her mouth twitched, not quite a smile but close.

"It's the one I've got." He glanced over at her. "What about you? You've been scribbling in that notebook since we got here."

Gem's ears twitched. "Habit. When things get complicated, I make lists. Helps me feel like I have some control over the chaos." She tapped the notebook against the railing. "Probably looks neurotic from the outside."

"Looks like someone who's trying to keep track of a lot of moving pieces."

"That's a generous read." She was quiet for a moment, watching the tree line. When she spoke again, her voice had shifted, gone softer. "I couldn't save someone once. That's why I am the way I am. The patterns, the data, the obsessive checking. It started because I missed something I should have caught."

Cody waited. Gave her the space to find her own way into it, the same way she'd given Benji space ten minutes ago.

"Freshman year." Gem's grip on the notebook had gone white-knuckled. "There was a girl in my cohort, a vole named Petra. Sharp mind, good instincts, working on cross-dimensional energy signatures. Her advisor was this professor, big name in the department, lots of publications." Her mouth twisted. "He stole her work. Published it under his name, claimed she was just a lab assistant who'd misremembered her contributions."

Cody's hands curled around the railing. He thought about Graves in that lab coat, that patient smile, all that calm certainty while he explained how Cody was going to make history. "Damn."

"I saw pieces of it happening," Gem said. "Little things. The way he'd dismiss her ideas in meetings and then bring them up later as his own. The way her name kept sliding off drafts. The way she started flinching when he walked into the lab." She shook her head. "I had all the data points, Cody. Every single one. But I didn't connect them fast enough. By the time I figured out what was happening, she'd already gone to administration."

"What did they do?"

"Buried it." Gem's tail flicked once, sharp. "Called it a misunderstanding. Suggested she might be struggling with the pressure of graduate work. The professor kept his position, kept his grants, kept his reputation. Petra transferred out a month later. I never heard from her again."

Cody thought about that. A girl with a sharp mind and good instincts, ground down by someone with more power and better connections. A system that protected the wrong people because protecting them was easier than asking hard questions. His chest ached, and he wasn't sure if it was for Petra or for himself.

"So now you build the patterns before they finish forming," he said quietly.

Gem nodded. "I track the data before it goes missing. I make sure that if someone's being hurt, I see it coming in time to actually do something about it." She finally met his gaze, and the exhaustion in her face was the same kind he saw in the mirror. "Petra was the first person I failed to protect. I made myself a promise after she left. Never again."

The name Graves sat heavy in Cody's throat. He remembered Gem in that basement, the day she'd tested him. The way she'd kept her voice lower than necessary, positioned them in a corner where the camera couldn't reach. He hadn't understood it then. He was starting to now.

"Thank you," he said finally. "For catching it this time."

Gem's smile was small but real. "Thank me after tomorrow. We've still got a Dean to destroy." She paused at the door, looking back. "And Cody? I'm glad you're here. Even if the circumstances are terrible."

She went back inside, and Cody stayed on the porch, watching the trees bend and straighten in the wind. He thought about his own weight, the thing he'd been carrying since before he fell through a portal into a world that shouldn't exist. Sooner or later they were going to ask. Sooner or later he was going to have to decide whether to answer.

The wind picked up again, cold against his face, and Cody stayed where he was, letting it push against him while he tried to figure out what he was willing to let go.


The planning session lasted most of the afternoon.

They gathered back around the kitchen table, sandwiches pushed aside to make room for Benji's laptop and Gem's notebook. The footage sat on the screen like a loaded weapon, paused on Graves's face mid-sentence.

"The problem," Gem said, "is that official channels won't help us. Graves has fifteen years of institutional protection. Connections to the board, donors, everyone who matters. We take this to administration, it gets buried. Police means months of investigation, and he has plenty of time to destroy evidence and control the narrative."

"So we skip the middlemen," Benji said, drumming his claws on the table. "Go straight to the people." His eyes lit up the way they did when he was three steps ahead of everyone else. "Game Day. Forty thousand mammals in one place, half the state watching on TV. We put that footage on the Jumbotron and let them all see exactly what kind of Dean they've been cheering for."

Gem leaned forward, running the numbers in her head. "That's... actually not insane. Media presence, witnesses everywhere, no way to spin it quietly. By the time he could even try damage control, the story's already out."

"Benji, can you actually do it?" Lukas asked. "The PA system, the screens, all of it?"

"Stadium IT is somehow worse than the science building," Benji said with a grin that was half manic, half proud. "I could probably run the whole system from my phone if I wanted to. The hard part isn't getting in. It's timing it right so nobody cuts the feed before the good stuff plays."

"Halftime," Thorne said. Everyone looked at him. "Biggest crowd attention. Everyone's watching the field, waiting for the show. Nobody's expecting the real show to be on the screens."

Gem nodded. "That's our window. Lukas and Thorne are on the field for the first half anyway, so we can't move before that. Cody, Benji, and I position in the stands. At halftime, we slip backstage. Benji handles the tech, I run interference if needed."

She paused, pen hovering over her notebook, and turned to Cody. "And you make the speech."

Cody blinked. "Wait, me? Why me?"

"Because you're the victim," Gem said, not unkindly. "You're the one he strapped to a table. You're the one with the bruises and the story. A recording is powerful, but a face standing in front of forty thousand people saying 'this happened to me' is something else entirely."

"She's right," Lukas said quietly. His amber eyes were steady on Cody's face. "People need someone to believe in. Someone real, standin' there, tellin' the truth. That's you."

Thorne nodded. "Footage alone, they might think it's fake. Edited. You standing there makes it real."

Cody looked around the table. They were all watching him, waiting, no pressure in their faces. They were offering him a role that mattered. A chance to be more than just the guy things happened to.

"Okay," he said slowly. "Okay, I'll do it."

The tension in the room eased. Benji let out a breath. Gem made a note in her notebook.

"But I have a condition," Cody added.

Lukas's ears flicked forward. "What kind of condition?"

"Before halftime. Before any of this." Cody's hands were flat on the table, steadying himself. "I want to face him."

The room went still.

"Why?" Gem asked carefully.

"Because he made me feel like nothing." Cody's voice was steady, but anger threaded through it, building since he woke up in that cabin. "Like I was just a thing to be studied. A specimen. And I want to look him in the eye and show him that I'm still here. That I'm not afraid of him anymore."

"That's a risk," Gem said. "If he knows you're there, he might..."

"He won't do anything," Cody cut in. "Not if I do it right. The concourse, ten or fifteen minutes before kickoff. He'll be doing the rounds, pressing flesh, working the crowd. I walk up to him in front of everyone, smile, say something innocent. Nice day for football, something like that. He can't touch me. Can't even react without looking crazy."

"And if he does react?" Lukas asked, wariness creeping into his voice.

"Then we have even more witnesses." Cody leaned forward. "I'm not trying to provoke him into something stupid. I just want him to know. Before the footage plays, before his whole world falls apart, I want him to look at me and see that I'm not broken. That whatever he tried to do to me didn't work."

Gem considered this, tapping her pen against the notebook. "If he's rattled when the footage drops, he's more likely to crack publicly instead of keeping his composure." She glanced at the others. "It could actually help."

Thorne shrugged. "His choice. His risk."

Benji was grinning. "I love it. Psychological warfare. Very dramatic."

Lukas was the last to speak. His posture was tense, shoulders tight, but when he met Cody's eyes, the worry there gave way to reluctant respect. "You sure about this?"

"I'm sure."

A long pause. Then Lukas nodded. "Alright. We do it your way."

The plan was set. Benji started pulling up stadium schematics on his laptop, muttering about access points and signal boosters. Gem made lists, her handwriting small and precise. Thorne leaned back, arms crossed, watching everything with that steady focus he brought to everything.

And Cody sat there, feeling for the first time since Room B-7 like he might actually have a say in how this story ended.


The planning wound down around four, and for a while nobody knew what to do with the silence. The cabin felt different without a crisis to fill it, smaller, more real.

Benji found a deck of cards in one of the kitchen drawers, warped and sticky from humidity but still usable. "Poker?" he offered, fanning them out. "I promise I only cheat a little."

"You cheat a lot," Gem said, but she pulled up a chair anyway.

They played for chips from the bag Benji had been working through all day, which meant the stakes were literally just crumbs. Thorne won three hands in a row with a face so blank it could have been carved from stone. Benji accused him of counting cards. Thorne pointed out that you couldn't count cards in poker. Benji accused him of cheating anyway.

"He's not cheating," Gem said, tossing her cards down. "He just has the worst poker face in history, and somehow that makes him impossible to read. It's statistically infuriating."

"I'll take it," Thorne rumbled, raking in his pile of crumbs.

Lukas sat out the first few rounds, leaning against the counter with his arms crossed, but Cody caught him watching the game with a half-smile he was trying to hide. After a while he pushed off and dropped into the chair next to Cody. "Deal me in."

"You any good?" Cody asked.

"Terrible." Lukas's tail wagged once. "But I like watchin' Benji lose."

"Hey!" Benji threw a chip at him. It bounced off Lukas's chest and landed on the floor. "I'm not losing, I'm strategically redistributing my assets."

The game continued. Cody lost badly, which he blamed on never having played poker with people who had ears that swiveled and tails that twitched. Benji insisted that was speciesist. Gem pointed out that Cody also just had a terrible poker face. "You do this thing with your eyebrows when you're bluffing," she said. "It's very obvious."

"I don't have a thing with my eyebrows." Cody raised both of them as high as they would go, holding the expression for a beat. "See? Completely normal."

Lukas snorted. "You absolutely do. It's kind of cute."

Cody felt his face heat and focused very hard on his cards.

The afternoon stretched into early evening, the light through the windows going gold and then orange. At some point Thorne got up and started rummaging through the kitchen cabinets, emerging with a can of beans and a dented pot that looked older than all of them combined.

"Dinner?" he offered.

"I'm not eating mystery beans the night before we take down a dean," Benji said. "What if we all get food poisoning?"

Gem was already on her phone. "There's a pizza place about twenty minutes out that delivers. We're off the grid enough, should be fine."

Two large pepperonis and a veggie later, they ate pizza out of the box, sitting around the table that was still covered in chip crumbs and scattered cards. It should have felt pathetic, five people hiding in a cabin eating delivery pizza while they planned to take down a corrupt dean. But it didn't. It felt like the family dinners Cody had stopped hoping for years ago. It felt like belonging.

After dinner, Lukas caught his eye and tilted his head toward the back door. Cody followed him out onto the small deck that overlooked the creek, the water catching the last of the sunset in flashes of copper and pink.

They stood there for a while, shoulders almost touching, listening to the water move.

"You doin' okay?" Lukas asked. "Real answer, not the one you give everyone else."

Cody thought about it. "I don't know," he said finally. "I keep waiting to feel something. Fear, anger, I don't know. But mostly I just feel tired."

"That's fair. You've earned tired."

Cody's mouth twisted, something between a grimace and a smile that didn't quite land. "Have I?"

Lukas turned to look at him, amber eyes catching the fading light. "You got kidnapped, strapped to a table, threatened, escaped, and now you're plannin' to walk up to the guy who did it and smile in his face. Yeah, Cody. You've earned tired."

Cody let out a breath that was almost a laugh. "When you put it like that."

"I put it like that 'cause it's true." Lukas's paw found his, warm and solid. "You don't gotta be brave all the time. Not with me."

"I'm not being brave. I'm just too worn out to be scared."

"Same thing, sometimes."

The creek kept moving, catching light and losing it. Somewhere inside, Benji was arguing with Gem about the proper way to shuffle cards. Thorne's low rumble cut in occasionally, settling disputes with single words.

Cody leaned into Lukas's shoulder, just a little. "Thank you."

"For what?"

"For this. All of it. The pack, the cabin, the cheap pizza." He paused. "For not giving up on me when I made it really easy to."

Lukas squeezed his hand. "Wasn't ever gonna happen."

They stayed like that until the sun finished setting and the stars started coming out. Lukas's thumb traced slow circles on the back of Cody's hand, steady and unhurried, and Cody found himself thinking that maybe, when all this was over, there might be something worth staying for beyond just survival.

For a few minutes, the weight he'd been carrying felt almost manageable.


Evening settled over the cabin. The pizza boxes were stacked by the door, the cards put away, and the pack had drifted into their own corners. Benji was back on his laptop, running diagnostics. Gem had her notebook open, refining tomorrow's timeline. Lukas had gone to check on the truck, make sure it would start in the morning.

Cody found Thorne on the front porch, sitting in one of the old wooden chairs that creaked under his weight. The bear was staring out at the tree line, paws resting on his knees, taped knuckles catching the last of the light.

"Mind if I sit?" Cody asked.

Thorne tilted his head toward the empty chair. Permission granted.

They sat in silence for a while. Cody had learned by now that pushing Thorne was pointless. The bear talked when he was ready, and not a second before. So Cody watched the trees instead, listened to the creek, let the stillness stretch between them until it felt natural rather than awkward.

"You ever lose someone?" Thorne asked eventually. His voice was low, almost lost under the sound of the water.

Cody thought about his mother, the slow fade of her smile as the cancer took hold. "Yeah."

Thorne nodded, like that was the answer he'd expected. He was quiet for another long moment, and when he spoke again, his words came deliberate, each one placed like a stone in a wall.

"My dad left when I was twelve. Didn't say goodbye, didn't leave a note. Just gone one morning, like he'd never been there at all." His massive shoulders rose and fell. "Mom never talked about it. I never asked. Figured if she wanted me to know, she'd tell me."

Cody felt the weight of that settle over him. He thought about his own father, the bottles, the silence, the way absence could fill a room just as heavy as presence.

"I'm sorry," he said. The words sounded small, but they were the only ones he had.

"Spent a lot of years thinking it was my fault." Thorne's voice stayed even, but his claws had dug shallow grooves into the arm of the chair. "Thinking if I was stronger, bigger, maybe I could have made him stay. Could have held on tight enough that he couldn't leave."

"Thorne..." Cody's chest ached. He'd watched the bear move through the world like nothing could touch him, seen the way other mammals stepped aside when he walked past. To hear him talk about not being strong enough was wrong, like finding out a mountain had doubts about being tall.

"Doesn't work that way." Thorne finally looked at him, and the weariness in those dark eyes looked like it had been living there a long time. "Can't hold onto someone who wants to leave. Doesn't matter how strong you are. That's not what strength is for."

The creek kept murmuring. Somewhere in the cabin, Benji's laptop beeped twice.

"I watched that footage today," Thorne said. "The stuff Benji pulled. Graves standing over you, talking about his research, his history." His claws flexed against his knee. "Talking about that girl who lasted three days."

Cody's stomach tightened. He remembered those hours on that table, Graves's patient smile, the monologue that went on and on while Cody catalogued every detail of the room, looking for a way out.

"You didn't panic." Thorne's voice was simple, factual. "He had you strapped down, alone, telling you what he was going to do. And you just... watched. Waited. Stayed calm enough to find a way out."

"I wasn't calm. I was terrified."

"Doesn't matter. Terror makes people beg. Makes them scream, thrash, give up." Thorne shook his head slowly. "You kept your head. That's not nothing."

Cody didn't know what to say. He'd spent those hours cataloguing door timing and camera angles because it was the only thing keeping him from screaming. It hadn't felt like strength. It had been the only option that wasn't falling apart.

"That's what pack means," Thorne said. "You don't hold on too tight. You just show up. Over and over. Until showing up is the same as staying."

The door opened behind them. Lukas stepped out, saw them sitting there, and hesitated.

"You two okay?"

Thorne stood, joints popping. "Fine. Just talking." He moved past Lukas into the cabin, one massive paw briefly squeezing the wolf's shoulder as he passed.

Lukas took the empty chair, and the silence between them was different than the one with Thorne. Heavier. Cody could feel things unspoken pressing at the edges.


For a while, neither of them said anything. The creek filled the space, and somewhere inside the cabin Benji was laughing at something Gem had said. Normal sounds. Safe sounds.

"You and Thorne have a good talk?" Lukas asked finally.

"Yeah." Cody glanced at him. "He's not what I expected."

"Nobody ever is." Lukas's tail brushed against the porch boards, slow and rhythmic. "First time I met him, I thought he was gonna eat me. Turns out he just wanted to know if I had any snacks."

Cody laughed, short and surprised. "Seriously?"

"Hand to God. Big scary bear, askin' if I had any granola bars." Lukas shook his head, a small smile tugging at his muzzle. "Benji was worse. Tried to prank me with hot sauce on my first day. Didn't know wolves could smell capsaicin from across the room."

"So you've always been this paranoid."

"Cautious. There's a difference."

They sat with that for a moment. The stars were fully out now, scattered across the sky like someone had spilled salt on black velvet.

"I just..." Lukas ran a paw over his muzzle. "Someone taught me, once. To look out for people. To pay attention to back routes and safe paths and ways to keep the people you care about from getting hurt. And I've been doing it so long I forgot there's a difference between helping and hovering."

"Who taught you?"

Lukas didn't answer right away. His ears flicked back, then forward again. "Guy named Marcus. Older player, three years ahead of me. When I was a freshman, he took me under his wing. Showed me the ropes, covered for me when upperclassmen got territorial, taught me where the cameras were and which routes to take and how to survive this place." A pause. "He graduated. Moved on. Life happened. Haven't heard from him in years."

"And that's why you helped me."

"He did it for me. Felt right to pass it on." Lukas finally met his eyes. "Difference is, I'm not going anywhere. But that doesn't mean I get to decide how you fight your own battles. I know that. I do. It's just hard to turn off."

The tension in Cody's shoulders eased, just a little. "So this is you trying to turn it off?"

"This is me tryin' to figure out where the line is." Lukas's drawl thickened, the way it did when he was fighting to stay calm. "I know what I said earlier, in front of everyone. About the speech. I meant that. You're the right one to do it." He paused. "But the confrontation. Walkin' up to Graves before we drop the footage... I've been thinkin', and that's a risk I ain't comfortable with."

"You agreed to it."

"I got caught up in the moment. Everyone was there, you were fired up, and I wanted to support you." Lukas leaned forward, elbows on his knees. "But I've had time to think now. And Graves had you strapped to a table, Cody. He talked about keepin' you there for days. You wanna walk up to him with a smile and poke the bear?"

"I want him to see that I'm not afraid."

"But you are afraid. We all are. That's not weakness, that's sense." Lukas shook his head, ears pinning back. Lukas shook his head, ears flattening. "And pretendin' otherwise is how people get hurt."

Cody felt something coiling tight behind his ribs, that familiar pressure that always came before he did something stupid. "So what, I just hide until halftime? Let him think he won?"

"Nobody's talkin' about hidin'. I'm talkin' about not givin' him a heads up that we're comin' for him."

"He already knows we're coming for him!" Cody was on his feet now, pacing the small porch. "He knows I escaped, he knows the pack helped, he knows we have something planned. The only thing he doesn't know is when and how. Walking up to him doesn't change that."

"It changes everything!" Lukas stood too, and for a moment they were face to face, close enough that Cody could see the worry lines between his eyes. "You get in his face, he starts thinkin'. Starts plannin'. Maybe he pulls extra security, maybe he doesn't show up to the concourse at all. You're gamblin' the whole operation on a moment of satisfaction."

"It's not about satisfaction!"

"Then what's it about?" Lukas's voice cracked, just slightly. "Because from where I'm standin', it looks like you're tryin' to prove somethin' to yourself, and you're willin' to risk everything to do it."

The words hit harder than Cody expected. He stopped pacing, breathing hard, and for a moment all he could hear was his own heartbeat and the distant sound of the creek.

"You don't know what it was like," he said finally. His voice came out rough. "Being strapped down. Listening to him talk about me like I was already dead. Like I was just... a thing."

"I know." Lukas's voice softened. "I saw the footage, remember? I watched him standin' over you while you couldn't move."

"Then you know why I need to do this." Cody's eyes scrunched shut for a moment, jaw tight.

"What I know is that you're angry. And scared. And you're not thinkin' straight." Lukas took a step closer, and his paw landed on Cody's shoulder, warm and solid. "I've seen you when you get like this. The way you go quiet right before you blow up. You get hot, and you stop seein' the bigger picture."

Cody's hands were shaking now, that old familiar anger looking for somewhere to go.

"You don't know anything about me."

"Then tell me." Lukas's eyes held his, steady and patient. "Tell me what's really goin' on. Because this ain't just about Graves. This is about somethin' older."

The words hit too close. Cody felt a wall crack open in his chest, and behind it was heat, too much heat, clawing at his ribs like it wanted out.

"You don't get to do that." His voice came out wrong, shaking. "You don't get to dig around in my head and pretend you know what's in there."

"Cody, wait..."

"No." He stepped back, shaking Lukas's paw off his shoulder. The heat was spreading now, down his arms, into his hands. Wrong. This was wrong. "You want to know what's really going on? You want to know what I'm carrying? You're not ready for that. None of you are."

He couldn't breathe. The porch was too small and Lukas was too close and the thing inside him was clawing at his ribs like it wanted out.

He yanked open the cabin door and went inside.


The main room went still when Cody walked in.

Thorne lifted his head from the couch. Benji glanced up from his laptop. Gem emerged from the kitchen area, wiping her hands on her jeans. They all saw his face and nobody said a word.

Cody made it halfway across the room before his legs stopped working. He stood there shaking, pressure building behind his ribs until it twisted into panic.

Behind him, the porch door opened. "He needs to sit down," Lukas called out, his voice tight. "Now."

Gem moved without hesitation, pulling out a chair at the kitchen table. The gesture said everything without a word. Cody's feet carried him to it without his permission. He sat down heavily, put his elbows on the table, pressed the heels of his hands against his eyes. The others gathered around him, settling into chairs, moving without crowding. Giving him space. Giving him time.

"I know about the girl you couldn't save," Cody said to his hands, the words coming out jagged, each one dragged up from somewhere deep. "I know about Benji waiting for everyone to leave. I know about Thorne's dad and Lukas's mentor who taught him to look out for people." He dropped his hands and met their eyes, one by one, jaw set, that pressure still coiled behind his ribs. "You want to know about me? Fine. You want to know what I'm carrying? I'll tell you."

Benji's quills flattened against his head. Thorne went very still. Gem's pen had stopped moving over her notebook, her ears angled forward. Lukas stood behind Cody's chair, close enough that Cody could feel the warmth of him.

"There was a guy," Cody started. His voice came out rough, like something rusted that hadn't been used in too long. "Back home. Senior year. Football teammate. Running back, same as me. We'd been competing for the starting spot all season, and he got it. I was backup. Told myself I was fine with it."

The memory was right there, vivid as a photograph. The locker room before the big game. The fluorescent lights. The smell of sweat and industrial cleaner. Guys suiting up all around them, coaches yelling about formations.

"My mom died when I was twelve. Cancer. Took about eight months from diagnosis to the end, and I watched the whole thing." Cody's hands curled on the table. "This guy, he knew. Everyone knew. Small town, word gets around."

His grip tightened. The wood of the table creaked under his fingers.

"He started talking. Loud enough for everyone to hear. About my mom. About what he'd do to her if she was still alive. The things he'd make her do." Cody's voice went flat, dead. "Detailed. Graphic. Smiling the whole time, watching my face, waiting to see what I'd do."

The room was so silent Cody could hear the creek outside, could hear his own heartbeat pounding in his ears.

"I asked him to stop. Once. He laughed and kept going." Cody exhaled slowly. "So I stopped asking."

He could feel their eyes on him. Could feel the pressure building behind his ribs, that familiar weight, that thing that had been growing since he fell through the portal.

"I don't remember most of it. I remember the first punch. I remember him going down. And then it was just red. Noise and heat and my fists hitting something over and over until I couldn't feel my hands anymore."

His voice cracked.

"They had to pull me off. Three guys, maybe four. I don't know. I was still swinging when they dragged me away." His gaze dropped to his hands, to the white knuckles, to the way they were shaking. "They said if they hadn't stopped me, I would have killed him. And the thing is..." He swallowed hard. "The thing is, I believe them. I wasn't stopping. I couldn't stop. I didn't want to stop."

The heat was building now, pulsing in time with his heartbeat. He could feel his fingers pressing harder into the table, could feel the wood groaning, could feel something shifting under his skin that shouldn't be there.

"He was in the hospital for two weeks. Broken jaw, broken orbital, broken ribs. I got expelled. Dad had to pay off the family to keep me out of juvie." A bitter laugh escaped him. "Didn't matter. Word got around. Schools wouldn't take me, teams wouldn't touch me. I was done."

Lukas reached across and put his paw over Cody's hands. 

Cody shrugged it off. "Don't!"

Lukas came around the chair and crouched in front of him, both paws gripping Cody's shoulders, solid and warm. "Cody. Look at me."

He couldn't. If he looked up, they'd see. They'd see whatever was happening to him, whatever was wrong with him, whatever that portal had woken up or created or unleashed.

"Cody!" Lukas's grip tightened, trying to shake something loose. "Look at me!"

Cody kept his head down, eyes squeezed shut.

Lukas's paw came up under his chin, gentle but firm, lifting until Cody had no choice but to meet those amber eyes. They were steady, holding neither fear nor judgment, just that patient, stubborn care that had been there since that first day in the pine grove.

"Hey." Lukas's voice dropped, soft now. "Hey, Cody. That's what you did. It ain't who you are."

Cody's throat closed up. His eyes burned.

Gem's certain voice softly spoke up. "Tomorrow you use words. That's who you're choosing to be. That matters more."

Thorne spoke without looking up from his hands. "He said that about your mother. I understand."

Benji was watching with wet eyes, chin trembling, but he didn't look away.

A wall cracked in Cody's chest. Not the heat this time, not the anger. The thing underneath it, the thing he'd been holding together with wire and spite for years.

The tears came before he could stop them. He reached forward and grabbed Lukas, pulling him into a hug that was probably too tight, burying his face against the wolf's shoulder. Lukas didn't hesitate, wrapping his arms around Cody and holding on like he was the only thing keeping him from falling apart.

Maybe he was.

They stayed like that for a long moment, Cody's shoulders shaking, Lukas's paw steady on the back of his head. The others didn't move, didn't speak, just let it happen.

Finally, Cody pulled back, wiping his face with the back of his hand. He stared down at his fingers, the same fingers that had broken bones and ruined a life, the same hands that had wanted to keep swinging until there was nothing left to hit.

Lukas reached over and wrapped both paws around them, holding them steady.

"I don't know what's happening to me," Cody whispered. "Since I got here. There's something... I can feel it. Getting stronger."

Lukas squeezed his hands. "We'll figure it out. After tomorrow. Together."

Then he pulled Cody into another hug, and this time Cody let himself sink into it. Let himself believe, just for a second, that he wasn't alone anymore. That pack meant something. That together wasn't just a word.

When they finally separated, the others had moved closer. Thorne's massive paw landed briefly on Cody's shoulder. Benji gave him a watery smile. Gem reached across and squeezed his arm, just once.

The night settled around them, quiet and full of everything tomorrow would bring. But sitting there at that table, surrounded by people who knew the worst thing he'd ever done and had chosen to stay anyway, Cody felt a warmth in his chest that had nothing to do with anger.

Hope.




---

# Chapter 25: Before the Roar

Friday morning came through the cabin windows soft and slow. Cody blinked awake, aware of warmth along his back before anything else. The bed was small, barely a full, and sometime during the night Lukas had shifted closer. His arm was draped loosely over Cody's side, his breath slow and even against the back of Cody's neck.

For a few seconds, Cody didn't move. Just lay there, letting himself have this one quiet moment before everything started.

Through the open bedroom door, he could see the main room. Thorne was up, folding the blanket from the couch. Two sleeping bags lay empty near the cold fireplace. Benji sat cross-legged on his, laptop balanced on one knee, and Gem stood at the window with her arms crossed, watching the trees.

Behind him, Lukas stirred. The arm around Cody's waist tightened briefly, then relaxed.

"You awake?" Lukas's voice was rough with sleep.

"Yeah." Cody watched Thorne open a cabinet, stare into it, and close it again. The cabin didn't have much. "We should find food."

"Diner on the way in. Saw it when we drove up."

From the main room, Benji's voice cut through. "If this diner doesn't have coffee, I'm going to do something dramatic. I haven't decided what yet, but it'll be memorable."

"You'll survive," Gem said without turning from the window.

"I won't. I'll die. You'll have to explain to everyone that Benji Hartwell perished from caffeine withdrawal on the most important day of his life."

Cody felt Lukas's chest shake with a quiet laugh. He made himself sit up, already missing the warmth, and went to find his shoes.

It didn't take long to get ready. Nobody had much to pack. Benji shoved his laptop into his bag, Gem did a final sweep of the cabin to make sure they weren't leaving anything behind, and Thorne stood by the door like he was ready to physically carry anyone who moved too slow. Cody splashed water on his face in the tiny bathroom, stared at himself in the mirror for three seconds, and decided that was enough self-reflection for a morning like this.

They piled into Gem's car, Thorne folding himself into the back seat with a look of resigned suffering. The gravel crunched under the tires as they pulled away from the cabin, and Cody watched it shrink in the side mirror until the trees swallowed it up. Nobody suggested stopping at the deli from yesterday. They all wanted something different, something that didn't remind them of hiding.

Lukas spotted the diner about twenty minutes down the road, tucked between a gas station and a bait shop that looked like it hadn't seen a customer in years. The sign out front said ROSIE'S in letters that used to be red, now faded to something closer to salmon. A pickup truck and a station wagon sat in the gravel lot, both covered in a fine layer of dust.

"This looks like the kind of place that either has the best pancakes you've ever tasted or gives you food poisoning," Benji announced as Gem pulled into a spot. "No middle ground."

"Fifty-fifty odds," Thorne said from the back seat, where he'd spent the drive with his knees practically in his chest. "I'll take 'em."

The bell above the door jingled when they walked in. The inside matched the outside: worn linoleum, booths with cracked vinyl seats, a counter with red stools that had seen better decades. The smell hit Cody immediately, bacon grease and coffee and something sweet baking in the back. His stomach growled before he could stop it.

A tired-looking raccoon behind the counter looked up, did a quick headcount, and nodded toward the back corner. "Booth in the back'll fit you. Menus are on the table."

They squeezed in. Thorne took up most of one side, his shoulders pressing against the wall. Gem slid in next to him, tail curling around her feet. Cody ended up between Lukas and Benji, close enough that he could feel the warmth of Lukas's leg against his.

Benji grabbed a menu and started scanning it like he was looking for contraband. "They have something called a Lumberjack Special. Three eggs, four strips of bacon, two sausage links, hash browns, and a short stack. That's not breakfast, that's a challenge."

"That's Tuesday," Thorne said, not looking up from his own menu.

"I'm getting it." Benji slapped the menu down. "If I die, tell my laptop I loved it."

The raccoon waitress appeared with a pot of coffee and a pad, looking like she'd seen every type of customer walk through that door and stopped being surprised years ago.

Benji's eyes locked onto the coffee pot like she was carrying liquid gold. "Oh thank god. Yes. Please. Fill it to the brim. I will name my firstborn after you."

The waitress didn't blink. "It's just coffee, hon."

"It's *salvation*."

She poured, unimpressed, and pulled out her pad. They ordered. Thorne got the Lumberjack Special too, plus a side of biscuits. Gem asked for eggs and toast, practical as always. Lukas just wanted coffee and some wheat toast. Cody stared at the menu for too long before settling on scrambled eggs and bacon, his stomach too knotted to commit to anything heavier.

When the waitress left, Benji wrapped both paws around his mug like it might try to escape. "So. Game day. You know the stadium's gonna be packed, right? Like, standing room only. They've been hyping this game for weeks."

"It's the playoff decider," Lukas said. "Course it's packed."

"Right, right." Benji took a long sip, then pointed the mug at him. "And you're gonna be out there, doing your whole linebacker thing, looking scary. Meanwhile I'm gonna be in the stands, sweating through my hoodie, pretending I'm not about to..."

He caught himself, glanced around the diner. Empty except for an old badger at the counter nursing his own coffee.

"Pretending I'm not nervous," he finished. "About the game. Because sports."

"Smooth," Gem said.

Silence settled over the table for a moment. Cody could feel the tension underneath everything, the thing none of them wanted to name out loud. They'd talked it to death last night. They knew the plan. Saying it again wouldn't make it any more real.

Gem's pencil tapped against the table twice, then stopped. "So Thorne. You ever eat at a place like this before a game?"

Thorne considered the question like it had actual weight. "Once. Sophomore year. Ate a western omelet the size of my head, then threw up behind the bleachers during warmups." He paused. "Still played the whole game though."

"That's disgusting," Benji said, delighted. "I love it. Tell me more."

And just like that, the tension cracked. Thorne started talking about the game, the one against Ridgewood's rival two years back, how their center had tried to start a fight and ended up flat on his back wondering what happened. Benji interrupted every thirty seconds with questions and commentary. Gem's mouth twitched in that almost-smile she did when she was actually enjoying herself but didn't want to admit it.

The food arrived. Benji stared at his Lumberjack Special like it had personally offended him, then picked up his fork with the determination of a man going into battle. Thorne demolished his plate with methodical efficiency. Gem ate her eggs in small, precise bites, watching the rest of them with quiet amusement.

Cody picked at his bacon. Lukas's paw found his knee under the table, squeezed once, then stayed there.

"You know what I've been thinking about?" Benji said around a mouthful of hash browns. "That time Thorne got stuck in the elevator in Whitmore. You remember that? Security had to call maintenance, and he just sat in there for like two hours reading a book."

"It was forty-five minutes," Thorne said.

"It was two hours and you finished the whole book."

"It was a short book."

"It was like eight hundred pages!"

Gem nearly choked on her coffee. Lukas let out a sound that might have been a laugh if you knew what to listen for. Cody felt something loosen in his chest, some knot he hadn't realized he'd been carrying.

This was what pack meant, he thought. The hard stuff and the easy stuff, all tangled together. Terrible diner coffee and arguments about elevator incidents and Benji trying to eat more food than any hedgehog should be able to fit in his body.

He took a bite of his eggs. They were overcooked and underseasoned and somehow exactly what he needed.

They finished eating without anyone bringing up the plan again. Thorne paid the bill before anyone could argue, dropping cash on the table with a look that shut down any protests. Benji made one last desperate grab for the coffee pot before the waitress took it away, failed, and mourned loudly all the way to the parking lot.

The drive to the stadium took another hour. Gem handled the wheel with the same focused efficiency she brought to everything else, navigating the increasingly heavy traffic with minimal commentary. Benji had called shotgun before anyone else could claim it, which meant the back seat was a wall of fur and muscle that had no business being crammed into a space meant for two. Thorne sat with his knees practically at his chin, taking up half the seat by himself. Lukas was pressed against Cody so tight there wasn't a hair's width between them, and every time Gem took a turn, all three of them shifted like cargo that hadn't been tied down right.

"This is inhumane," Thorne rumbled from somewhere behind his own knees.

Benji twisted around from the front seat, where he had enough room to practically lie down. "Should've called shotgun faster, big guy."

"I will remember this."

"Worth it."

Benji turned back to his laptop, fingers flying across the screen, occasionally muttering to himself. Thorne closed his eyes, not sleeping but doing that thing bears did where they went somewhere quiet inside their own heads. Probably imagining revenge. Lukas caught Cody's eye and shrugged as much as the cramped space allowed, which wasn't much.

The stadium appeared on the horizon like a promise and a threat all at once. Cody had seen it before, that day Lukas took him on the tour, but it looked different now. Bigger. The parking lots were already filling up, cars streaming in from every direction, and even from this distance he could hear the bass thump of music playing somewhere near the tailgate area.

"Forty thousand people by kickoff," Gem said. "Give or take."

Forty thousand witnesses. The thought sat heavy in Cody's stomach alongside the eggs.

They parked in a lot reserved for players' families, thanks to some arrangement Lukas had made. Getting out of the car felt like stepping into another world. The noise hit first, a wall of sound made up of a hundred different conversations, car horns, someone's sound system blasting fight songs. Then the smell: grilled meat, beer from a dozen open coolers, the particular funk of too many people packed together in late autumn air. Early arrivals, tailgaters setting up grills and canopy tents, but already enough of a crowd to disappear into.

Lukas checked his phone. "Gotta go. Warmups in twenty."

This was it, then. The moment where they split up and the plan started for real. Cody felt something tighten in his chest.

Thorne clapped Benji on the shoulder hard enough to make the hedgehog stumble. "Don't do anything stupid."

"That's literally the whole plan," Benji said.

"Stupider than the plan, then."

Lukas stepped closer to Cody, and for a second it looked like he wanted to say something. His paw came up, hesitated, then landed briefly on Cody's arm. "See you after."

"Yeah." Cody's throat felt thick. "Go be scary."

Lukas's mouth twitched. Then he and Thorne headed toward the players' entrance, two massive shapes cutting through the crowd until the sea of fans swallowed them up.

Gem watched them go, then turned to Cody and Benji. "Alright. Let's find somewhere to wait."

They made their way through the tailgate area, weaving between grills and folding chairs and groups of fans already deep into pre-game rituals. Cody stuck close to Gem and Benji, scanning automatically, reading the crowd the way he always did. Looking for the shape of danger before it had a chance to form.

That's when he saw him.

Dean Graves stood about fifty yards away, near the main concourse entrance. He was surrounded by a small cluster of people in expensive coats, the kind of people who had buildings named after them. Donors. Board members. The dean was in full performance mode, gesturing expansively, his smile wide and warm and absolutely fake if you knew what to look for.

Cody's feet stopped moving.

He felt the pull immediately, that familiar heat flickering to life somewhere behind his ribs. Fifty yards. He could close that distance in thirty seconds. Walk right up to him, get in his face, let him see exactly who was standing there in front of all his important friends. Let him know that the subject, the data point, the opportunity had a name and a voice and wasn't afraid anymore.

"Cody." Gem's voice, sharp. She'd stopped a few feet ahead, noticed he wasn't following. "What is it?"

He didn't answer. The heat was spreading now, down his arms, into his hands. He remembered the weight of his fists, the way it felt to stop asking and start swinging. Three seconds of satisfaction before the red cleared and he saw what he'd done.

*That's what you did. It ain't who you are.*

Lukas's words from last night cut through the noise. And then Gem's, right after: *Tomorrow you use words. That's who you're choosing to be.*

Cody stood there, people flowing around him like water around a rock, and felt the heat slowly bank down to something manageable. Graves was still talking, still performing, completely unaware that the person who was about to destroy his entire world was standing fifty yards away with the means and the motive and the opportunity.

But not yet. Not like this.

This wasn't the moment. The confrontation on the concourse, the one he'd talked about last night, the one Lukas had tried to argue him out of ‚Äî that would have been about anger and proving something to himself. The old Cody who didn't know any other way to fight back except with his fists.

The speech was different. The speech was about justice and evidence and forty thousand witnesses who couldn't look away.

*Tomorrow you use words.*

Today. Today he used words.

Cody turned his back on Dean Graves. The man never even looked his way, too busy charming people who would hate him by halftime.

"Come on," Cody said. His voice came out steadier than he expected. "Let's find our seats."

Gem was watching him with something sharp in her eyes. She glanced past him, toward where Graves was still holding court, then back. Her tail flicked once.

"You saw him," she said. Not a question.

"Yeah."

"And you're walking away."

"Yeah."

She studied him for another second, like she was running calculations. Then something shifted in her expression, just a fraction. "Good. That's the smart play." She turned toward the stadium entrance. "Let's go."

They pushed through the crowd toward the student section, Benji falling into step beside them. The stadium was still filling up, maybe ten thousand people scattered across the stands so far, but more streaming in every minute. By kickoff there'd be forty thousand. Forty thousand witnesses.

Cody didn't look back.



---

# Chapter 26: Game Day

The stadium filled around them seat by seat, section by section. What had been scattered clusters of fans when they sat down was now a solid mass of green and black, bodies pressing into seats, voices layering over each other until the noise became its own kind of weather. Vendors worked the aisles hawking hot dogs and sodas, their calls cutting through the din. Music thumped from speakers somewhere overhead, bass heavy enough that Cody could feel it in his chest.

He'd been to games before, back when he played. But never anything like this. Forty thousand seats, and it looked like every single one was going to be filled.

Beside him, Benji had his laptop balanced on his knees, fingers moving across the keyboard in short bursts between glances at the field. His leg bounced constantly, that restless energy looking for somewhere to go. On Cody's other side, Gem sat perfectly still, watching the empty field with the kind of focus she usually reserved for test results. Her tail curled around her ankles, the only sign she wasn't carved from stone.

"How long?" Cody asked.

Gem checked her phone. "Twelve minutes to kickoff."

Twelve minutes until kickoff, a couple hours until halftime, and then everything changed. One way or another.

The scoreboard flickered to life, cycling through stats and graphics. The crowd noise swelled and dipped in waves, conversations and laughter and the occasional burst of a fight song from somewhere in the student section. Cody watched a family squeeze into seats three rows down, a kit in a tiny Badgers jersey clutching a foam finger almost as big as she was.

Normal people having a normal day, no idea what was coming.

A roar started somewhere near the tunnel and spread through the stadium like a lit fuse. The speakers crackled, and a voice boomed across the field: "Ladies and gentlemen, please welcome YOUR TIMBERWOOD BADGERS!"

The tunnel doors burst open. Green and black flooded out, a wall of helmets and pads and muscle, the team spreading across the field in formation. The crowd was on its feet instantly, Cody included, the noise so loud it stopped being sound and became pressure.

He scanned the numbers. 54, 71, 89... there.

Number 87. Lukas jogged out near the middle of the pack, helmet already on, jaw visible beneath the facemask. Even from this distance, Cody could see the set of his shoulders, the coiled tension in the way he moved. Thorne ran beside him, massive even among the linemen, his number 74 stretched across a back wide enough to block out the sun.

They couldn't wave, couldn't acknowledge each other at all. Lukas's eyes swept the stands once, quick, not searching for anyone specific, just taking it in. Then he was past, heading for the sideline with the rest of the defense, and Cody was just another face in forty thousand.

But he'd seen him. That was enough for now.

The anthem played, followed by the coin toss and captains meeting at midfield. Then the kicker was setting up, the crowd holding its breath, and the ball was in the air.

Kickoff.

The first quarter was a knife fight. Ridgewood scored first, a quick drive that made the crowd go quiet, then Timberwood answered with a grinding twelve-play march that ended in a field goal. The lead changed hands twice before the quarter ended, and Cody found himself leaning forward in his seat, his old instincts waking up like a muscle he'd forgotten he had.

He could read the formations, see the mismatches before they developed. When the Ridgewood quarterback dropped back on third and long, Cody knew what was coming before the ball left his hand. Screen play. The lineman in front was letting defenders through on purpose, not blocking them, which meant someone was about to catch a short pass behind all that chaos. The running back was drifting toward the sideline too casually, trying to look like he wasn't the target. Classic misdirection.

Lukas saw it too. He didn't chase the quarterback like the play wanted him to. Just read the setup, ignored the fake, and broke toward the running back before the pass even arrived. The tackle was clean and hard, two yards behind the line of scrimmage, and the crowd erupted.

But something was off. Cody watched Lukas get up, watched the way he shoved away a teammate who tried to slap his helmet. He was playing angry, hitting harder than he needed to, staying in piles longer than necessary. The coiled tension from the tunnel entrance hadn't loosened. If anything, it had gotten tighter.

"He okay?" Benji asked, not looking up from his laptop.

"I don't know."

The second quarter was more of the same, the score seesawing back and forth. Timberwood up by three, then tied, then down by four, then a touchdown made it 17-14 Badgers with two minutes left in the half. Benji's fingers never stopped moving, pulling up windows and closing them, running through whatever sequence he'd memorized. At one point he muttered "PA's clean" to no one in particular. A few minutes later: "Jumbotron feed's ready."

Gem checked her phone every thirty seconds. Cody stopped asking how long.

The clock wound down. Ridgewood tried a last-minute drive that stalled at midfield, and the halftime whistle split the air.

Gem was already standing. "Now."

---

The halftime whistle meant one thing: get off the field.

Lukas pulled his helmet off as he jogged toward the tunnel, sweat cooling on his fur in the November air. Around him, the rest of the defense was doing the same, a stream of green and black jerseys flowing toward the locker room while the marching band took the field behind them. Thorne fell into step beside him, breathing hard but steady, his massive frame blocking out the stadium lights.

"You good?" Thorne asked, low enough that only Lukas could hear.

Lukas huffed, pulling at his chin strap. "Fine."

"You're hitting like you're trying to kill someone."

"Maybe I am."

Thorne didn't push it. That was the thing about Thorne, he knew when to talk and when to just be there. They'd figure it out later, after. After whatever was about to happen in those stands, after whatever Cody was about to do. Lukas had seen Gem stand up right before the whistle, had caught that flash of movement in his peripheral vision even from fifty yards away. It was starting.

And he was stuck down here.

The tunnel swallowed them, concrete and fluorescent lights replacing sky and grass. The noise of the crowd faded to a dull roar, then disappeared entirely as they pushed through the double doors into the locker room. Coach Hendricks was already at the whiteboard, marker in hand, diagramming adjustments for the second half. Guys were peeling off pads, grabbing water, finding their lockers.

Lukas dropped onto the bench in front of his and started working on his shoulder pads. His muscles ached in that good way, the kind that meant he'd been playing hard. The score was close, 17-14, and Ridgewood wasn't gonna roll over easy. He needed to focus on the game. Needed to trust that Cody and Gem and Benji knew what they were doing up there.

Easier said than done.

"Raines."

The voice came from behind him, and Lukas didn't need to turn around to know who it was. He'd known that voice since they were six years old, running around Rumi's backyard pretending to be pro players.

"Not now, Rumi."

"Yeah, now."

Lukas turned. Rumi Castillo stood three feet away, still in full pads, helmet dangling from one hand. His eyes had that wild look, the one Lukas remembered from middle school when someone said the wrong thing and Rumi couldn't let it go. Back then it had been about respect and proving himself. Now it was about something uglier.

"I know what you're doing," Rumi said. "Hiding your little pet up in the stands. You think the Dean doesn't know? You think this is just gonna blow over?"

"Walk away."

"He's not done with this, Lukas. None of you are. And when it all comes down, I'm not gonna be the one standing in the wreckage."

Lukas stood up slowly, using every inch of his height. "You already are."

Something flickered across Rumi's face, doubt or fear or maybe both. But it got swallowed up by anger before it could take root, and his free hand shot out and grabbed a fistful of Lukas's jersey.

"You used to be somebody," Rumi hissed. "You used to matter. Now look at you. Throwing everything away for some freak who fell out of the sky."

"Let go."

"Or what? You gonna hit me? Get yourself thrown out of the game?"

The locker room had gone quiet. Lukas could feel eyes on them, the whole team watching to see what happened next. Rumi's grip tightened, knuckles pressing into Lukas's chest.

Then a shadow fell over both of them.

"He said let go."

Thorne's voice was calm, almost conversational. He'd appeared beside them without making a sound, which shouldn't have been possible for someone his size. His arms hung loose at his sides, but there was nothing relaxed about the look in his eyes.

Rumi's head snapped toward him. "This ain't your business, bear."

"It is now."

For a long moment, nobody moved. The tension in the room was thick enough to choke on, everyone holding their breath, waiting. Rumi's jaw worked, teeth grinding, and Lukas could see him calculating. Too many witnesses. Too many people who'd tell the coach exactly what happened if this went sideways.

But Rumi had never been good at math.

He let go of Lukas's jersey and swung at Thorne instead.

The punch caught Thorne square on the jaw, hard enough that his head snapped to the side. The crack echoed through the locker room, and then everything exploded at once.

"HEY! HEY!"

Two linemen grabbed Rumi from behind, hauling him back. Someone else was shouting for coach. A water bottle went flying, knocked off a bench in the chaos. Lukas felt hands on his shoulders too, teammates pulling him away even though he hadn't moved, everyone trying to separate bodies that weren't even fighting anymore.

Because Thorne hadn't swung back.

He just stood there in the middle of the chaos, perfectly still while bodies moved around him. A trickle of blood ran from the corner of his mouth, bright red against dark fur. His right hand had curled into a fist, knuckles popping, but he didn't use it. Just looked at Rumi with something that might have been pity. Then he uncurled his fist, wiped the blood from his lip with the back of his hand, and said nothing at all.

"ENOUGH!"

Coach Hendricks pushed through the knot of players, face red, veins standing out on his neck. The linemen holding Rumi let go but stayed close. Hendricks looked at Thorne's bloody lip, at Rumi's still-clenched fist, at the circle of players who'd witnessed the whole thing.

"I saw everything," Hendricks said, voice like gravel. "Castillo, you're done. Benched for the half, maybe for the season. Get out of my sight."

Rumi opened his mouth like he was going to argue, then closed it. The fight drained out of him all at once, shoulders slumping, fist finally dropping to his side. He looked at Lukas one more time, and there was something in his eyes that hadn't been there before. Something quieter than anger, rawer than defiance.

Understanding. The first crack of it, anyway. The realization that he'd just thrown away everything he'd been chasing, and for what? The Dean had offered him a path, and he'd taken it. Made his choice. And now he was standing in a locker room full of teammates who'd never look at him the same way again, and no scholarship or roster spot was going to fix that.

Rumi turned and walked to the far end of the bench, sat down, and stared at the wall.

Lukas and Thorne exchanged a look.

"You okay?" Lukas asked quietly.

Thorne shrugged, reached up to touch his jaw, winced slightly. "He hits like a kitten." Then he picked up his helmet and walked back to his locker like nothing had happened.

Coach Hendricks didn't go back to the whiteboard. He stood in the middle of the room, arms crossed, letting the silence stretch until it hurt.

"Fifteen years I've been coaching," he said, voice low and hard. "Fifteen years. And I have never, NEVER, seen someone throw away their future like that in the middle of a playoff game." He pointed at Rumi without looking at him. "You want to fight? You want to act like a damn fool? Do it on your own time. Not in my locker room. Not when we're three points up and thirty minutes away from the championship bracket."

Nobody moved, nobody breathed.

Hendricks swept his gaze across the room, making sure every single player felt it. "Anyone else got something to get off their chest? Now's the time." Silence. "Good. Then sit down, shut up, and remember why you're here."

He turned back to the whiteboard, snatched up the marker, and started diagramming with short, angry strokes. The tension in the room didn't bleed away so much as get shoved down, buried under the familiar rhythm of halftime prep. But it was still there, humming underneath everything.

Lukas grabbed his water bottle, took a long drink, and made himself focus on the whiteboard. Hendricks was adjusting the coverage on third down, shifting responsibilities, talking about Ridgewood's tendency to go to their tight end in the red zone. Lukas nodded in the right places, committed the changes to memory. This was his job. This was what he could control.

But when Hendricks paused to erase a line and redraw it, Lukas's eyes drifted to the locker room door. Just for a second. Somewhere out there, past the concrete and the tunnel and the forty thousand people in those stands, Cody was about to do something that couldn't be undone.

He hoped it was enough.

Then Hendricks was talking again, and Lukas shoved the thought down with everything else.

---

Gem was already moving before the echo of the halftime whistle faded.

"Stay close," she said, not looking back. "Don't run. Don't make eye contact. Just walk like you belong."

Cody followed her down the stadium steps, weaving through fans heading for concessions and bathrooms. Benji brought up the rear, laptop bag bouncing against his hip, quills flattened with focus. The noise of the halftime show thumped from the field behind them, drums and brass and the roar of a crowd that had no idea what was about to happen.

They hit the concourse level and Gem cut left, away from the food vendors and the merchandise stands, toward a stretch of wall that looked like nothing special. Plain concrete, a fire extinguisher, a door marked STAFF ONLY.

"Benji," Gem said.

He was already fishing a keycard from his pocket. "Cloned it off a maintenance guy last week. Should still work." He swiped it. The lock clicked red. "Or not."

"Try again."

Another swipe, red again, then green. The door popped open.

"Third time's the charm," Benji said, grinning. "Definitely charm."

They slipped through into the service corridor. The air changed immediately, stadium noise muffling behind the heavy door. Fluorescent lights buzzed overhead, pipes running along the ceiling.

"This is where we split," Gem said. She pointed down the corridor. "Benji, AV control is two levels up, third door on the right. You've got maybe six minutes to get the PA and Jumbotron ready."

Benji was already moving. "PA's prepped, video file's loaded, just give me six minutes."

"Six minutes," Gem repeated.

"Got it, got it." He paused, looked back at Cody. "When you're ready, say something into the mic. Anything. I'll know you're live and I'll be listening for the cue."

"What cue?"

Benji grinned, some of his usual chaos creeping back. "You'll know. Just make it dramatic." Then he was gone, footsteps echoing down the corridor until they faded.

Gem turned to Cody. "You ready?"

"No."

"Good answer." She started walking the opposite direction. "This way. Service tunnel comes out at field level, near the visiting team entrance. Should be clear during halftime."

They moved fast through the concrete maze, taking turns Gem seemed to know by instinct. The halftime show thumped above them, muffled bass making the walls vibrate. Twice they passed maintenance workers who barely glanced up from their phones.

Then Gem stopped at a door marked FIELD ACCESS - AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL.

"This is you," she said. She pulled something from her pocket and pressed it into his hand. A wireless microphone, small and black, the kind coaches wore on the sidelines. "Benji rigged it to patch into the PA system. Soon as you turn it on, the whole stadium hears you."

Cody turned it over in his hand. Such a small thing. Such a big moment.

"I'll be watching from the tunnel," Gem said. "Soon as you're done, we move. You say what you need to say, Benji plays the footage, and we get you somewhere safe before security figures out what happened."

Cody nodded. His pulse was up, adrenaline already starting to hum through his veins, but it wasn't fear. It was the feeling he used to get before a big game. The feeling right before the play clock hit zero and everything started moving.

"Hey." Gem's paw landed on his shoulder, squeezed once. "You've got this."

"I know." He meant it. He'd been through worse than a speech. He'd survived worse than a crowd. Last time, strapped to that table, his brain had found the way out even when his body couldn't move. This time, he got to use both.

"Go get him," Gem said.

Cody pushed through the door.

The light hit him first. Stadium lights blazing down, bright enough to wash out the afternoon sky. Then the noise, the sheer wall of it, forty thousand voices and the halftime show's final crescendo mixing into something that felt less like sound and more like weather. The field stretched out in front of him, green and perfect, yard lines crisp white against the turf.

The marching band was finishing up, filing off toward the far end zone. Cheerleaders clustered near the sideline. Camera crews repositioned for the second half. Nobody was looking at him yet.

That was about to change.

Cody walked onto the field.

He made it to the forty-yard line before people started noticing. A few heads turning, then more, then whole sections of the crowd rising to their feet. The murmur started small and spread fast, confusion and shock rippling through the stands. Fingers pointing. Phones coming up. He could almost hear the questions bouncing through forty thousand minds at once: What is that? Is that a... is that a HUMAN?

Most of them had probably never seen one in real life. 

Security would be coming soon. He had maybe a minute, probably less.

He clicked the microphone on.

The PA system crackled, sharp and loud, slicing through the murmur. The crowd noise shifted, forty thousand people realizing at once that this wasn't just some streaker or halftime prank. The human had a microphone. The human was about to speak.

Cody looked out at the sea of faces. Wolves and bears and foxes and deer, thousands of them, staring back at him with expressions ranging from confusion to fear to fascination. He'd never felt so small in his life, and he'd never felt so sure of what he needed to do.

He took a breath, let it out slow, and started talking.

"My name's Cody."

His voice boomed across the stadium, echoing off concrete and steel, and the last of the halftime buzz died like someone had hit a switch. Forty thousand faces locked onto him, waiting, barely breathing.

"Week ago, I fell through a hole in the sky and landed in your woods. I didn't ask for it and I sure as hell didn't want it, but here I am."

He could see security moving now, coming from both sidelines, but they were hesitant and confused. This wasn't in the playbook. Nobody had told them what to do about a human standing at the forty-yard line talking into a microphone.

"First guy I met here was kind to me. Gave me a place to sleep, food to eat, made me feel like I wasn't alone. I started thinking maybe this place wasn't so bad."

His voice found its rhythm. The shaking in his hands steadied. This was just talking. Just telling the truth. He'd done harder things than this.

"Then I met Dean Graves."

The name landed like a stone dropped into still water. He could feel the ripple spreading through the crowd, the sudden sharpening of attention. Security had stopped moving, caught between confusion and curiosity like everyone else.

"He sat me down in his office, smiled at me, offered me candy from a little bowl on his desk. Told me he wanted to help. Told me he understood what I was going through. And I believed him. Because I wanted to."

His voice hardened without him meaning it to. He could feel his pulse in his throat, the anger trying to take over, but he kept it leashed. Controlled. This wasn't about rage. This was about truth.

"And because I believed him, I pushed away the folks who actually cared about me. The ones who'd taken me in, fed me, kept me safe. I called them liars. I walked right into his hands."

He let that sit for a beat. Owning it. Then he kept going.

"And what did I get for trusting him? He threatened the folks who'd taken me in. Told them their scholarships, their academic careers, everything they'd worked for would disappear unless they handed me over."

His grip on the microphone tightened, but he kept his voice steady.

"So I went to talk to him. Face to face. Thought maybe I could reason with him, protect the folks who'd been protecting me. And you know what I got? Sedated and hauled away like cargo."

He let that land before continuing.

"I woke up strapped to a table in a room in the basement of the science building. And that same man who offered me candy stood over me with a clipboard and told me exactly what he was gonna do. Every test. Every sample. Every way he was gonna cut me open to get his name in a journal."

Silence now. The kind of silence that happens when forty thousand people stop breathing at the same time.

"He told me I wasn't the first. That other people fell through before me. That one of them died on that table, and he buried it. No authorities. No investigation. Just gone."

Cody paused. Let that sink in. Then he felt something shift in his chest, the nervous energy transforming into something else. Something that felt almost like confidence. Former captain energy, Thorne might have called it. The feeling of standing on a field with everyone watching and knowing exactly what to do next.

"Now, I could stand here and tell you what kind of man Victor Graves really is." He turned toward the Jumbotron, arm sweeping up in a gesture that felt natural now, like calling a play. "But I think it's better if you hear it from him."

He pointed at the massive screen.

"Roll it!"

The Jumbotron flickered, went dark for half a second, and then Room B-7 filled the screen. Forty feet tall, bright as daylight, impossible to miss. The concrete walls, the equipment racks, the table with straps hanging loose. And standing in the middle of it all, a snow leopard in a lab coat, silver-white fur immaculate, pale blue eyes fixed on something off-camera.

On Cody. Strapped down and helpless.

The audio crackled through the stadium speakers, clear as a bell.

"Every journal that rejected me will be begging to print my name."

Graves's voice, soft and clinical and utterly damning, echoed across forty thousand stunned faces. The footage kept rolling. The sampling kit. The clipboard. The casual discussion of tissue extraction and resonance mapping while Cody lay there unable to move.

Phones came out everywhere. Thousands of tiny screens lighting up, recording, capturing. The evidence playing on an endless loop while the crowd watched in horror.

Cody lowered his arm. The Jumbotron kept playing, but he wasn't watching anymore. He was looking toward the press box, toward the donor suites, searching for a flash of silver-white fur.

He didn't have to search long.

Graves was already coming down. Moving fast through the crowd near the stairs, security trailing behind him, that careful composure cracking with every step. Even from this distance, Cody could see it. The mask slipping.

Good. Let him come. Let everyone see what happened next.

Cody stood at the forty-yard line and waited.

---

Graves reached him in under a minute.

The Dean's fur was disheveled, his suit jacket askew, his carefully maintained composure hanging by threads. Security flanked him on both sides, but they weren't restraining him. Not yet. These were his people. His loyalists. They'd escorted him down, not held him back.

The Jumbotron still played the footage on loop behind them. Forty feet of evidence, impossible to ignore.

"You don't understand what you've done."

The voice was soft, clinical, trying to reclaim control. But Cody could hear the cracks underneath. He could also hear Graves's words echoing back from the stadium speakers, the mic in his hand still live, still broadcasting. Graves either didn't notice or didn't care.

"I understand exactly what I've done."

Graves stepped closer, and Cody held his ground. Around them, the stadium had gone quiet again, everyone watching, everyone recording, everyone waiting to see what happened next.

"That footage is out of context," Graves said, voice still controlled but tight around the edges. "You're confused. Traumatized. I was trying to help you, to understand what you ARE. Do you have any idea what your existence means to science? To this entire world?"

"I know what it meant to you."

Something flickered in those pale blue eyes. The mask slipping, just a little.

"I gave you shelter. I gave you answers. I was going to make you SIGNIFICANT." Graves's claws started to curl, the professional veneer cracking. "And this is how you repay me? By destroying everything I've built? Do you know what you've cost this university? What you've cost ME?"

Cody felt his hands wanting to curl into fists. The old familiar pressure, the one that used to mean fists flying and consequences later. His body wanted to move, to hit, to make Graves feel even a fraction of what he'd felt strapped to that table.

But he stayed planted. Kept his voice steady.

"You strapped me to a table."

"FOR SCIENCE!" The softness was gone now, Graves's voice rising, and the words boomed back from the stadium speakers a half-second later, forty thousand witnesses hearing the Dean of Academic Affairs screaming justifications for human experimentation. A ripple of shock went through the crowd. Someone in the stands yelled something Cody couldn't make out. "For knowledge that would have changed everything! Fifteen years I've waited, FIFTEEN YEARS, and you just... you just..."

"You're scared, aren't you?"

Graves stopped. The words hit him like a slap, cutting through the spiral.

"What?"

"Not of me," Cody said quietly. Every word landing like a hammer. "Of being nobody again. Fifteen years of waiting, and this was supposed to be your legacy. Your name in the journals. Your face at the conferences. But you're not gonna get it. Because I'm not your subject. I'm not your data. I'm just a guy who fell through a hole in the sky."

He let that sit for a beat. Then:

"And I've got folks who actually give a damn about me. What do you have?"

The crack happened all at once.

Graves's face twisted, something ugly and raw surfacing from underneath all those years of careful control. His claws extended fully, his lips pulled back from his teeth, and he lunged. Not calculated, not measured, just pure desperate rage, reaching for Cody like he could somehow take it all back if he could just get his paws around the human's throat.

Cody didn't flinch, didn't move. Just watched him come.

Security grabbed Graves before he got there. Two of them, hauling him back by the arms, but everyone had already seen. Every phone in the stadium had already captured the Dean of Academic Affairs lunging at a student on the forty-yard line. The crowd erupted, gasps and shouts and a growing roar of disbelief. Somewhere in the stands, someone was booing. Then more joined in.

"You're NOTHING!" Graves was shouting now, all pretense abandoned, thrashing against the guards holding him, his words still echoing through every speaker in the stadium. "You hear me? NOTHING! I made you MATTER! Without me you're just another accident! Another piece of DEBRIS!"

The boos were spreading now, rolling down from the upper decks like thunder. Forty thousand people watching a man they'd trusted, a man who'd shaken their hands at fundraisers and smiled at them in hallways, completely fall apart.

Cody didn't respond. Just stood there, hands at his sides, and watched them drag him away.

The mask wasn't slipping anymore. It was gone. Shattered into pieces on the field in front of everyone who'd ever believed in him.

Gem appeared at his elbow, crossing from the tunnel entrance. Benji was jogging across the field from somewhere near the press box, laptop bag bouncing. The crowd noise was shifting now, the boos fading into something else. Someone started clapping. Then more. It spread through the stands in uneven waves, not the thunderous roar of a touchdown but something quieter, more uncertain. Applause for a moment no one had expected to witness.

Cody raised the mic one more time.

"Thanks for listening."

He clicked it off. The applause swelled briefly, then began to settle as forty thousand people tried to process what they'd just witnessed.

"You okay?" Gem asked finally.

Cody thought about it. His hands had stopped shaking. The tension that had been coiled in his shoulders all day had finally let go.

"Yeah," he said. "I think I am."

---

They didn't get to stand there long.

A badger in a university polo pushed through the crowd of staffers and media that had started gathering near the tunnel entrance, a security escort flanking him on both sides. His name tag read CAMPUS OPERATIONS but his expression said something closer to damage control.

"You." He pointed at Cody. "Come with me."

Gem stepped forward, putting herself between them. "He's not going anywhere without us."

The badger looked like he wanted to argue, but something in Gem's eyes made him reconsider. He glanced at the security guards, then back at the three of them, then at the crowd of people still recording on their phones.

"Fine. All of you. Now."

They followed him through the tunnel and into a conference room tucked behind the athletic offices. The walls were lined with team photos and championship banners, and a long table dominated the center of the space. Two more officials were already waiting inside, a deer in a blazer who introduced herself as Associate Dean Morrison and a wolf in campus security uniform who didn't introduce himself at all.

The badger shut the door. "Who are you? What the hell was that out there?"

Cody opened his mouth to answer, but Morrison held up a hoof.

"Wait." She was looking at her phone, scrolling through something. The footage, probably. It was already everywhere. "Before you say anything, I need to understand what we're dealing with here." She looked up at Cody, and her expression had shifted from confusion to something more careful. "What did Dean Graves do to you?"
