Cody told them. The short version, at least. Office 412. The candy bowl. The email threatening his friends. Waking up strapped to a table in a basement room. The things Graves had said while standing over him with a clipboard.

"The email," Gem said, already pulling out her phone. "I still have it. Sent to my campus account." She handed it to Morrison, who read it with the security wolf looking over her shoulder.

*Have the human come to Office 412. Alone. By 5 PM.*

*I'd hate for your academic standing to be affected by any... misunderstandings about unauthorized system access. And I'm sure Mr. Raines would prefer his scholarship review to proceed without complications.*

Morrison's expression hardened as she read. "He put this in writing?"

"Signed it too," Gem said. "Warm regards, Dean Victor Graves."

Nobody interrupted after that. By the end, Morrison's face had gone pale under her fur.

"The footage," she said slowly. "That's all real? That room, that equipment, that's on this campus?"

"Room B-7," Cody said. "Sublevel of the science building."

Morrison and the badger exchanged a look that said entire careers are about to end over this. The security wolf was already on his radio, speaking in low tones.

She stood, straightening her blazer. "We'll need a full statement, but that can wait until tomorrow. Monday morning, my office, nine AM. Bring your friends." She paused at the door. "Tonight, just... watch the game. You've earned that much."

Then she was gone, the badger and the wolf following, and Cody was left standing in the conference room with Gem and Benji.

"Well," Benji said after a moment. "That went better than expected."

Gem shot him a look.

"What? I was ready for handcuffs. This is a win."

They made their way back to the field. The second half was about to start, players jogging back onto the turf, the crowd noise building again as people tried to process what they'd just witnessed while also remembering there was still a football game happening. The Jumbotron had finally stopped playing the footage, replaced by the normal game graphics and sponsor logos.

Cody found a spot on the sideline, just behind the team bench, close enough to see the field but out of the way of the coaches and trainers moving back and forth. Gem stood on one side of him, Benji on the other. They didn't say anything. They didn't need to.

The defense took the field first. Cody scanned the jerseys until he found number 87, Lukas lining up at his position, helmet on, shoulders set. He looked the same as he had in the first half, focused and intense, but something was different. Cody could see it in the way he held himself, a tension that hadn't been there before.

Then Lukas turned.

He wasn't scanning the stands this time, just turning like he knew exactly where to look, and his eyes found Cody on the sideline, ten feet away.

The moment lasted maybe two seconds. Lukas's face was hidden behind his helmet, but Cody could feel the weight of that look. I see you. You made it. Now let me finish this.

Cody nodded, small, just enough to say he understood.

Lukas turned back to the field. And something in his posture changed, a looseness that hadn't been there before, like a weight had lifted off his shoulders.

The whistle blew. The second half started.

The third quarter was a grind. Both teams traded punts, neither offense finding any rhythm, the defenses locked in a war of attrition. Timberwood's lead held at three points, then Ridgewood tied it with a field goal, then Timberwood went back up by four on a short touchdown run. The crowd was on its feet for every play, the earlier chaos fading into the background as the game took over.

Cody watched it all from the sideline, his old instincts firing in ways they hadn't in almost three years. He could read the formations, see the mismatches, predict the plays before they developed. When Ridgewood's quarterback changed the play at the last second, Cody knew it was a short pass before the ball was even snapped. When Timberwood's offense bunched up tight on a crucial third down, he knew they were going to pound it straight ahead.

Football had been his whole life once. Before the incident, before everything fell apart. Standing here, close enough to hear the coaches calling plays and the players shouting adjustments, it felt like reconnecting with a part of himself he'd thought was gone.

The fourth quarter started with Ridgewood driving. They scored on a long march that ate up six minutes of clock, taking the lead 28-24. Timberwood answered with a field goal, cutting it to one. Then Ridgewood got the ball back with four minutes left.

Three minutes left, then two. Ridgewood running the ball on every play, trying to bleed the clock dry.

Cody watched the offensive line, the way one of the blockers was leaning before each snap, telegraphing the direction of the run. They're setting something up, his brain said. Trying to sucker the defense into overcommitting.

On the field, Lukas was reading the same thing. Cody could see it in the way he shifted his weight, refusing to take the bait.

The ball snapped.

The running back took the handoff and followed his blockers toward what looked like an open lane on the right side. But Lukas didn't bite. He cut the other direction, found the real gap, and met the runner before he'd taken two steps. The collision was loud enough to hear from the sideline.

The ball popped loose.

For one frozen moment, everyone just stared at it bouncing on the turf. Then Lukas was there, scooping it up in stride without breaking momentum, and suddenly he wasn't a defender anymore. He was a runner with nothing but open field ahead of him.

Timberwood ball. Forty-two yards from the end zone. And Lukas was already moving.

A defender grabbed at his jersey and missed. Another came in for the tackle and Lukas spun through it, still on his feet, still moving. The forty-yard line, then the thirty, the crowd noise hitting a pitch Cody felt in his teeth, in his bones, in every part of him. The twenty, a defender diving and getting nothing but air. The ten, Cody screaming something he didn't know what, his voice lost in the roar of forty thousand people. The five.

Lukas crossed the goal line and the scoreboard flipped: BADGERS 33, RIDGEWOOD 28. The clock hit zero half a second later.

The stadium exploded.

---

Cody didn't remember pushing through the crowd that flooded onto the field. He didn't remember shoving past reporters and cameras and delirious fans. He just remembered finding Lukas in the middle of the chaos, helmet off, fur matted with sweat, and then they were hugging, Lukas's arms crushing him against that massive chest while people screamed and confetti fell from somewhere and the scoreboard flashed BADGERS WIN over and over.

"You did it," Cody said into Lukas's shoulder. "Holy shit, you actually did it."

Lukas pulled back just enough to look at him. His eyes were wet, and Cody couldn't tell if it was sweat or tears or both. "I saw you on the sideline. When I turned around and you were just there." He shook his head. "I knew. Whatever happened, whatever you did, I knew you were okay."

"I'm okay," Cody said. "We're okay."

Then Thorne was there, lifting both of them off the ground in a bear hug that made Cody's ribs creak. Benji was bouncing around them like a kid on Christmas morning, quills practically vibrating with excitement. And Gem was standing a few feet away, arms crossed, trying to look unimpressed, but the smile tugging at the corner of her mouth gave her away.

The pack. His pack. All of them together on a football field while the world celebrated around them.

Cody let himself feel it, the victory and the relief and the bone-deep exhaustion that came from finally, finally being done with hiding and running and being afraid.

Monday there would be statements and investigations and probably a lot of uncomfortable questions. Monday they'd have to deal with whatever came next. But tonight? Tonight a dean was in custody, a wolf had just run forty-two yards for the win, and Cody was standing on a football field with people who'd risked everything to keep him safe.

Tonight they'd won.

All of it.

---

# Interlude: The Fall

The shredder made a sound like teeth grinding.

Victor Graves fed another stack of papers into the slot and watched fifteen years of research turn into confetti. Subject files. Protocol notes. Observation logs. All of it, gone in seconds, reduced to strips of nothing that piled up in the bin below.

His office door was open. It was always open. That was the Victor Graves brand, the signature move that had defined his tenure as Dean. My door is always open. Come in anytime. I'm here for you. A generation of students and faculty had walked through that door believing they were meeting a mentor, an ally, a friend. None of them had ever understood that the open door wasn't an invitation. It was a trap. A way to keep everyone visible, everyone accessible, everyone where he could see them coming.

Now the door hung open on an empty hallway, and Victor couldn't bring himself to close it.

His phone buzzed on the desk. He glanced at the screen. Chancellor Morrison. The fourth call in ten minutes. He let it ring through to voicemail, the same way he'd let the previous three go. The same way he'd let the calls from Legal go, from the Provost, from Campus Security. They all wanted to talk. They all wanted explanations. They all wanted to figure out how to handle this, how to contain it, how to make it go away.

None of them understood. There was no containing this. The footage was everywhere. Every phone in that stadium had recorded it, uploaded it, shared it before halftime was even over. His face was on every screen in the state by now, probably the country, his voice playing on loop: Every journal that rejected me will be begging to print my name.

The shredder jammed. Victor cursed and yanked at the paper, tearing it, fingers trembling as he tried to clear the teeth. His paws weren't steady anymore. Hadn't been since security had driven him back here from the stadium, since they'd walked him into his own building without a word and left him alone in his office like a prisoner in a holding cell.

He got the shredder running again and fed it another stack. Personnel records. Research grants. The documentation for Room B-7 that he should have destroyed years ago but had kept because he'd thought he might need it someday, might need proof of what he'd accomplished when the world finally recognized his work.

The world was recognizing it now.

His phone buzzed again. This time the screen showed a different name: VICTOR. His son. Victor Jr., who he hadn't spoken to in almost two years, who had stopped returning calls somewhere around the third missed birthday, who had finally given up on a father who was always too busy, always too focused, always chasing something more important than family dinners and graduation ceremonies and all the small moments that added up to a life.

Victor let that call go to voicemail too.

On the wall behind his desk, his diplomas hung in their frames, the gold lettering catching the fluorescent light. PhD in Xenobiology. Advanced Research Fellowship. Distinguished Faculty Award. Fifteen years of credentials, fifteen years of proof that he mattered, that he was somebody, that his name meant something in the halls of academia.

Beside the diplomas, a row of photographs he hadn't looked at in years. His wedding day, both of them young and smiling, before the long hours and the missed anniversaries and the slow erosion of everything that wasn't work. Victor Jr. as a cub, sitting on his father's shoulders at some campus event, his small paws gripping the fur on Victor's head. A family portrait from a decade ago, all three of them posed and stiff, the kind of photo you put on a wall and forget to dust.

The frames were dusty now. He could see it from here, the thin film covering the glass, proof of how long it had been since he'd done anything but glance past them on his way to something more important.

The shredder kept grinding. The bin was almost full.

Footsteps in the hallway. Multiple sets, heavy and deliberate, the kind of footsteps that didn't belong to students or faculty.

Victor looked up.

A Bengal tiger filled the doorway, massive and striped, wearing the uniform of University Oversight. Two more officers flanked him, wolves in tactical gear, their expressions professionally blank. Behind them, two campus security guards that Victor recognized, the same ones who had driven him here from the stadium. They wouldn't meet his eyes.

"Victor Graves." The tiger's voice was flat. "Step away from the desk."

Victor's paw hovered over the shredder's feed slot, one last stack of papers clutched in his grip. Evidence. Proof. Everything they would need to bury him.

"I'm the Dean of Academic Affairs," he said. The words came out wrong, too high, too desperate. "I have rights. I have tenure. You can't just walk into my office and..."

"Victor Graves." The tiger stepped into the room, and suddenly the space felt very small. "You are under arrest by the authority of Timberwood Hollow for kidnapping, unlawful imprisonment, assault, illegal experimentation on a sentient being, conspiracy, abuse of institutional authority, and accessory to the death of Subject TH-2010-003."

The papers slipped from Victor's fingers.

"Turn around. Hands behind your back."

Victor turned. The metal was cold against his wrists, the click of the cuffs loud in the quiet office. One of the wolves gathered the fallen papers, the unshredded evidence, everything Victor hadn't managed to destroy in time.

The tiger glanced at the shredder, still running, the bin overflowing with confetti. "Add destruction of evidence to the list."

"Let's go."

They walked him out of his office, past his nameplate on the door. VICTOR GRAVES, Ph.D. Dean of Academic Affairs. The brass plate he'd polished himself when he'd first been appointed, the title he'd spent his whole career chasing. Down the hallway of Whitmore Hall, the same hallway he'd walked a thousand times, ten thousand times, always with purpose, always with authority, always knowing that everyone who saw him knew exactly who he was and what he meant to this institution.

Students were gathered at the end of the hall. Word traveled fast on a college campus, even faster when the news was this big. They parted as the officers approached, pressing against the walls to make room, and Victor could feel their eyes on him. Phones came up. Screens glowing, cameras pointed, everyone recording.

"Holy shit," someone whispered. "Is that..."

"That's the Dean. That's Dean Graves."

"He's in handcuffs."

Victor kept his eyes forward, but he could hear the whispers spreading, could feel the weight of all those stares, all those lenses, all those witnesses who would share this moment with everyone they knew.

Faculty had gathered too. Colleagues he'd worked with for years, professors who had sat in his meetings and laughed at his jokes and competed for his approval. Dr. Whitmore from Chemistry. Professor Santos from Biology. The department heads he'd mentored, promoted, shaped into allies. They watched him pass in silence, and not one of them looked away.

The lobby of Whitmore Hall was worse.

A television mounted on the wall was tuned to a local news channel, and Victor's face filled the screen. Not a photo, not a headshot, but the footage. The footage from Room B-7, the footage he'd never imagined anyone would see, playing on a loop while a news anchor's voice provided commentary he couldn't quite hear over the blood rushing in his ears.

"Every journal that rejected me will be begging to print my name."

His own words, echoing back at him as he was led through the building in handcuffs.

The front doors of Whitmore Hall loomed ahead. Glass and steel, the same doors he'd walked through on his first day as Dean, the same doors he'd walked through every morning for fifteen years, always in control, always knowing exactly where he stood and what he was building.

The tiger pushed them open.

Outside, the November air hit Victor's face, cold and sharp. More cameras waited on the steps, news crews who had arrived while he was being processed, reporters shouting questions he couldn't parse through the ringing in his head.

The man who had wanted his name in every journal was about to get his wish.

Just not the way he'd planned.

The doors of Whitmore Hall swung shut behind him, and Victor Graves, former Dean of Academic Affairs, was walked down the steps toward a waiting patrol car while the whole world watched.

---

# Chapter 27: The Ledge

The celebration lasted for over an hour.

Cody lost track of how many people hugged him, slapped his back, shouted things he couldn't hear over the noise. Players he'd never met were grabbing his shoulders and yelling "That was INSANE!" Reporters tried to get close but security kept pushing them back, and somewhere in the chaos Coach Hendricks found him and just stared for a long moment before shaking his hand and walking away without a word.

The pack stayed close. Thorne appointed himself unofficial bodyguard, his massive frame clearing a path through the crowd whenever things got too intense. Benji was everywhere at once, bouncing between conversations, showing people something on his laptop, practically glowing with the kind of energy that came from pulling off the impossible. Gem kept to the edges, watching, but every time Cody caught her eye she gave him a small nod that said more than words would have.

And Lukas. Lukas stayed at his side as long as he could, close enough that their shoulders kept brushing, not saying much but always there. Every few minutes someone would pull him away for a photo or a handshake or a shouted congratulation on the game-winning play, and every time he'd drift back to where Cody was standing like it was the only place he wanted to be.

Eventually Coach Hendricks shouted something about showers and film review, and the team started flowing toward the tunnel. Lukas hesitated, looking at Cody.

"Go," Cody said. "I'm not going anywhere."

Thorne caught Lukas's eye and tilted his head toward the tunnel.

Lukas nodded once, then jogged after his teammates, Thorne falling into step beside him, their numbers vanishing into the crowd of green and black.

The pack waited on the field while the team cleaned up. Benji sat cross-legged on the turf, laptop balanced on his knees, muttering about upload speeds and server traffic. Gem stood with her arms crossed, watching the stands empty, but she looked more relaxed than Cody had ever seen her.

"Hell of a day," she said.

"Yeah." Cody sat down on the bench nearby. "Hell of a day."

Twenty minutes later, Lukas and Thorne emerged from the tunnel, both in civilian clothes, fur still damp from the shower. Lukas wore jeans and a flannel, sleeves rolled to the elbows, gym bag slung over his shoulder. Thorne had on sweats and a Timberwood t-shirt stretched tight across his massive chest. They looked different out of their gear. Smaller, somehow. More like the guys Cody had gotten to know than the wolves and bears who'd just won a playoff game.

The crowd had thinned to almost nothing by then. Parents collecting their kids, fans heading for the parking lots, the maintenance crews starting their slow sweep through the stands. The Jumbotron went dark. The scoreboard stayed lit a while longer, BADGERS 33, RIDGEWOOD 28 glowing against the night sky, before someone finally switched it off.

The stadium lights started clicking off section by section, plunging the stands into darkness. A few maintenance crews moved through the aisles picking up trash, their flashlights bobbing like fireflies in the distance.

Cody stood near the tunnel entrance with the pack, all of them tired and wired and not quite ready to leave. Thorne had his arm around Benji's shoulders, the porcupine's energy finally starting to fade into something closer to exhaustion. Gem stood apart, arms crossed, but she hadn't stopped smiling since they'd walked off the field.

"We should head back," she said finally. "It's late. And some of us have statements to give on Monday."

"Buzzkill," Benji muttered, but he was already yawning.

Thorne stretched, his massive frame blocking out what was left of the light. "Food," he said, already heading toward the parking lot.

"Always," Benji said.

Gem glanced at Lukas, then at Cody. Something flickered in her expression, there and gone. "You two coming?"

Lukas shook his head. "We'll catch up. I want to show Cody something first."

Gem's eyes narrowed for just a second, then softened. She nodded once, turned, and started walking. Thorne and Benji followed, their voices fading into the dark.

Then it was just the two of them, standing on the empty field where everything had happened.

Lukas turned to face him. The stadium lights were almost all off now, just a few security floods casting long shadows across the turf. In the dim light, his amber eyes caught what was left of the glow.

"There's a place I go sometimes," he said. "When I need to think. When everything gets too loud." He paused. "I'd like to show you. If you want."

If you want.

Not follow me or come on or any of the other commands Cody had gotten used to over the past week. Not Lukas grabbing his arm and pulling him somewhere safe. Just an invitation, offered with an open hand.

Cody had spent his whole life being led. By coaches, by his father, by circumstances he couldn't control. Following because he didn't know what else to do, because the path was already laid out, because fighting it was harder than going along.

This was different. This was a choice.

"Yeah," he said. "I'd like that."

---

They passed through a gate Lukas unlocked with a key from his pocket, down a concrete corridor beneath the stands, and through an unmarked door that opened onto a narrow stairwell. The metal rungs of a maintenance ladder were cold under Cody's hands as he climbed, the November air biting at his fingers, but he didn't stop until he pulled himself onto a flat concrete roof above the press box.

The view hit him like a fist to the chest.

The stadium stretched out below them, dark and silent, the stands empty, the field a black rectangle under the stars. And above, the sky was thick with more stars than Cody had ever seen back home, where light pollution swallowed everything. No city glow here, no haze, just an endless scatter of light against the dark.

"Damn," he breathed.

Lukas climbed up after him and dropped onto his back, arms folded behind his head. "Found this spot sophomore year. After the knee surgery, when I couldn't sleep. I'd come up here and just look up. Nobody knows about it."

"Until now."

Lukas turned his head, met Cody's eyes in the dark. Something passed between them, quiet and sure.

"Until now."

Cody lay down beside him. The concrete was cold and hard against his back, but he didn't care. From up here, the world felt very far away. The Dean, the footage, the speech, all of it seemed like it belonged to someone else, some other version of him from hours ago.

They lay there in silence for a while, just breathing, just existing.

"Can I tell you something?" Cody said.

Lukas's ear flicked toward him. "Shoot."

Cody stared at the stars. He'd known this was coming. Maybe he'd even wanted it to.

"There was a guy," he said. "Brian. We called him Briar sometimes because of this stupid joke about a rabbit patch that doesn't matter now." He swallowed. "We were together for two years. The kind of thing where you start planning a future without realizing you're doing it."

Lukas didn't move. Just listened.

"He had a motorcycle. Loved that thing more than anything except maybe me." Cody kept his voice flat, easier that way. "One night he went out for a ride. Truck ran a red light. He survived, but the head trauma was bad. When he woke up, he didn't know who I was. Didn't know who he was. Total memory loss."

Lukas's breath caught. "Cody..."

"I stayed. For months. Visited every day, helped with recovery, tried to be there." He laughed, but there was nothing funny in it. "He never remembered. Not me, not us, not any of it. Eventually his family moved him to a facility upstate. I got a letter from his mom thanking me for my help." He paused. "That was it."

The wind cut across the empty stadium below, a low hum through the stands.

"He's still out there somewhere," Cody said. "Living some life that doesn't include me. But the guy I loved? He's gone. Has been for years." He exhaled. "That's one of my ghosts."

Lukas turned his head. "One of them?"

Cody kept his eyes on the sky. "Remember that night on the bench? When I told you about my mom, the pancakes, the lake?" He swallowed. "There's something I didn't tell you. About my dad. About what happened after she died."

Lukas was watching him. Waiting.

"I told you something in him broke. That he started drinking, couldn't look at me without seeing her." Cody's fingers traced the cold concrete. "What I didn't say was where all that grief went. All that anger." He swallowed hard. "He needed somewhere to put it. I was right there."

The wind picked up, sharp against his face.

"He broke one of my ribs when I was fifteen. Told the ER I fell off my bike." Cody shrugged, a small, tight motion. "After that I learned to stay out of his way. Kept my head down, counted the days until I could leave." He finally looked at Lukas. "So yeah. That's why I'm not exactly homesick."

Lukas's eyes were dark, unreadable. When he spoke, his voice was low. "I'm sorry."

"Don't be. It's done." Cody turned back to the stars. "That night, before Gem's text came through, you asked me what I'd tell my mom if I could go back one more time. I was about to answer."

"What were you going to say?"

Cody was quiet for a moment. "That I found people who give a damn." He turned his head, met Lukas's eyes. "But that was before. Before Graves, before that room, before you ran through a wall to get to me." His voice dropped. "Now I'd tell her I found someone worth staying for."

Silence stretched between them. Then Lukas shifted, moved closer until their shoulders touched.

"At the cabin," Lukas said quietly, "I told you that you were pack. That you don't get to trade yourself for us."

Cody turned his head.

"But standin' on that field today, waitin' for you to walk out there..." Lukas's voice roughened. "I realized I was lyin' to myself. Pretendin' it was just about pack. Just about keepin' you safe." He let out a breath. "When I turned around after that play and you were just there, ten feet away, alive, after everything... I knew it was more than that. Has been for a while."

His paw found Cody's hand in the dark, warm and solid.

"I ain't good at this part," Lukas said. "The words. But I need you to know. Whatever this is between us, I'm not runnin' from it anymore."

Cody's heart was doing something strange in his chest. He was tired of pretending he didn't know what it meant.

"I'm not going to lie to you." Cody swallowed. "I'm getting butterflies in my gut. They started in the woods when you found me. Got worse in the tunnels, on that walk, every time you put yourself between me and something dangerous." He paused. "And at the cabin, when I told you all the worst thing I ever did, and you looked at me and said 'that's what you did, it ain't who you are'..." His voice caught. "That's when I knew. Tonight just made it impossible to ignore anymore." He let out a breath. "They come out every time I hear you speak, Lukas. Every single time."

Lukas went still beside him. "Butterflies bite hard," he said, voice low. "Tell me where they sting."

Cody finally turned his head, met those amber eyes. "I'm going to talk plain. I'm falling for you, bud." He swallowed. "The people I've loved have a way of disappearing. One forgot me, one turned into someone I didn't recognize, one I had to bury. So I need to know, and I'm sorry if it sounds like an ultimatum, but with everything that's happened, I need something solid."

A cold gust swept across the roof. Cody's voice stayed steady, even if his hands weren't.

"If you say no, I won't be angry and I won't make it awkward. It'll be like it's been. But if it's yes..." He paused. "I'm willing to take it slow. See where it goes." He turned back to the stars. "Your move."

Silence stretched between them. Cody could hear Lukas's breathing, could feel the warmth radiating off his fur even with the November chill pressing up from below.

Then Lukas shifted. Moved closer until their knees touched.

"Cody." His voice cracked. He cleared his throat, forced it steady. "I'm not good at soft landings. But I feel the same pull. Been fighting it since the woods, and losin' that fight a little more every day. The tunnels. The cabin. That night you told us everything and I realized I'd follow you anywhere." He let out a breath. "By the time I broke down that door in B-7, I wasn't even pretendin' anymore."

Cody's heart slammed against his ribs.

"No ultimatum," Lukas said. "No pressure. Just us, this ledge, these stars." His paw squeezed Cody's hand. "We already did slow, Cody. We already did careful. I think we earned this." He squeezed again. "Yes. I'm right here. I ain't goin' anywhere."

Something broke open in Cody's chest, not painful, just release. He felt a tear slide down his temple before he could stop it.

"Yeah," he managed. "Yeah, we did."

Lukas rolled toward him, pulled him in until Cody's forehead rested against the thick fur of his neck. "Still here," he rasped. "Both of us. After all of it."

Cody held on. Let himself be held. The stars wheeled overhead, indifferent and ancient, but right now they felt like witnesses.

When he finally pulled back, Lukas's thumb brushed the tear from his cheek, fur soft against skin.

"That one's mine," he murmured. "I'll keep it."

Cody laughed, wet and broken. Then he leaned in and kissed him.

He kissed Lukas like he'd been waiting his whole life and didn't want to wait another second. Like every wall he'd ever built had finally come down. Lukas made a sound deep in his chest, something between a growl and a gasp, and pulled Cody closer. Cody's fingers found the thick fur at the back of his neck and held on.

The world went away. The roof, the stadium, the whole damn week. There was just warmth and pressure and the taste of something he'd been too scared to name until right now. Lukas kissed him back like he meant it, like he'd been holding this in just as long, like letting go was the easiest thing he'd ever done.

When they finally broke apart, both of them were breathing hard. Lukas's eyes were bright, his tail thumping against the roof like he couldn't control it anymore and had stopped trying.

"Sealed," Cody whispered against his muzzle.

Lukas pulled back just enough to look at him, amber eyes searching his face like he was memorizing every detail. Then he lifted Cody's hand to his mouth and nipped his fingertip, gentle but deliberate. "Sealed."

For a long moment neither of them moved. Just breathing. Just being. The weight of the word settling between them like something solid, something real, something that couldn't be taken back.

Then Lukas tugged him down and they lay back together, closer this time, Cody's head on Lukas's chest, the wolf's arm wrapped around him like he was never letting go. The steady thump of Lukas's heartbeat against his ear was the best sound Cody had ever heard.

"We should head back," Lukas said eventually. "Security does rounds."

"Yeah."

Neither of them moved.

"Five more minutes," Cody said.

"Five more minutes."

They watched the stars. The empty stadium held them like a secret, and for the first time in a long time, Cody felt like he was exactly where he was supposed to be.

---

# Epilogue: What Quiet Sounds Like

Tuesday morning. Four days since the game, three days since Cody last set foot in an administrative building. Yesterday's statements had taken most of the afternoon, the whole pack retelling everything one by one while a recorder captured every word. Today Morrison had called just Cody back in, and Cody had insisted the others come too.

So here they were, crammed into the Associate Dean's office like they were waiting for a verdict. Benji perched on the arm of a chair, one leg bouncing. Gem stood by the window with her arms crossed, watching the quad below. Thorne took up most of the small couch, and Lukas sat beside Cody in one of the two chairs facing Morrison's desk, close enough that their shoulders touched.

Morrison shuffled papers, adjusted his glasses, cleared his throat. He was a deer, middle-aged, the kind of administrator who probably went into education wanting to help students and ended up buried in paperwork instead. Right now he looked like a guy delivering news he'd rehearsed six times in the mirror.

"First, the update on Dean Graves." Morrison folded his hands on the desk. "He's been formally charged and is currently incarcerated pending trial. Given the evidence, including Mr. Quillfoot's footage and Miss Kindler's documentation, the prosecutor is confident. You may be subpoenaed to testify, Mr. Guiles. We'll provide support through that process if it comes to it."

Cody nodded. He'd expected that part.

"Second." Morrison pulled a folder from his desk drawer and set it between them. "The university's legal counsel has asked me to present you with an offer."

Here it comes, Cody thought.

"In exchange for not pursuing legal action against Timberwood Hollow University..." Morrison opened the folder, turned it so Cody could read. "Full scholarship. Tuition, room and board, meal plan, books, and supplies. All covered for the duration of your undergraduate degree." He paused. "I understand you expressed interest in journalism. We have an excellent program. You'd be enrolled as a freshman starting spring semester, with provisional status until your documentation is fully processed."

The words sat there, black ink on university letterhead. Cody stared at them and felt something sour twist in his gut.

"So let me get this straight." His voice came out flatter than he intended. "Your dean kidnaps me, straps me to a table, and plans to do god knows what to my insides, and in exchange for not suing you into the ground, I get a free ride."

Morrison had the decency to look uncomfortable. "I understand how this might feel."

"Do you?" Cody leaned back in his chair, arms crossed, not bothering to hide the edge in his voice.

"Cody." Gem's voice, quiet from the window. He glanced over. She gave him a look that said take the deal, idiot.

Lukas's knee pressed against his under the desk, steady and grounding.

Cody looked back at the paperwork. Full scholarship, meal plan, books, the whole package. Everything he would have killed for back home, handed to him because a university was terrified of a lawsuit. It was hush money dressed up in academic language, and they all knew it.

But it was also a future. A real one. The kind he'd stopped believing in somewhere between his mother's funeral and his father's fists.

"Where do I sign?" The words came out tired, resigned, but he meant them.

Morrison's shoulders dropped about two inches with relief. He produced a pen, pointed to three separate lines, and Cody signed his name on each one. Cody Guiles. Cody Guiles. Cody Guiles. Like he was making himself real, one signature at a time.

When it was done, Morrison stood and extended his hand. "Welcome to Timberwood, Mr. Guiles. Officially."

Cody shook it. The grip was firm, and unless Cody's read on people had completely failed him, the warmth in Morrison's eyes was genuine. Not everyone at this university was a monster. Just the one who mattered most.

"Now get out of my office," Morrison said, and there was almost a smile tugging at the corner of his mouth. "Go enjoy your Tuesday."

---

The dining hall was loud and warm and smelled like fried food and cheap coffee, which was exactly what Cody needed after that office. They commandeered a table near the back, away from the windows, and within ten minutes Thorne had accumulated enough food to feed a small army.

"So you're official now." Benji was grinning so wide it looked like it hurt. "Cody Guiles, Timberwood University freshman. Got a nice ring to it."

"Sounds fake," Cody said, but he was smiling too.

"Sounds expensive." Gem sat down with a tray that held a salad and a cup of tea. "For them, I mean. Full ride? They must be terrified of what you could do to their reputation."

"Good," Lukas said. "They should be."

Thorne grunted in agreement, which for Thorne was practically a speech.

Benji launched into a retelling of the Jumbotron hack that Cody had now heard four times, but somehow it got more elaborate with each version. This time there were "military-grade firewalls" and "a three-second window before the whole system crashed" and Cody was pretty sure none of that was true, but Benji's quills were practically vibrating with pride so nobody corrected him.

Gem rolled her eyes but didn't interrupt. Thorne ate. Lukas's tail found its way around Cody's waist under the table, warm and solid, and stayed there.

"Oh!" Benji pointed his fork at Lukas. "Pay up, by the way."

Lukas raised an eyebrow.

"The bet. From Saturday." Benji turned to Cody. "How long before you stopped looking over your shoulder every five seconds. I gave you a month. Lukas said a week." He counted on his fingers. "Six days since you got here. He wins."

"You bet on me?" Cody's eyebrows shot up.

"We bet on your recovery," Benji corrected. "Very supportive. Very wholesome."

"I hate both of you." But Cody was fighting a grin, and losing badly.

"You love us," Benji said. "Also Lukas won, so technically he's buying lunch, which means I'm ordering dessert. Thorne, you want pie? I want pie. We're getting pie."

Thorne was already standing, headed for the dessert line. Benji scrambled after him.

Gem watched them go, then turned back to Cody and Lukas with something that might have been a smile. "You two are disgusting, by the way."

"We're not doing anything," Cody said.

"His tail has been around your waist for ten minutes." Gem gestured with her tea cup at the furry evidence in question.

Lukas didn't move it. "And?"

Gem shook her head, but she was definitely smiling now. "I'm happy for you. Both of you." She stood, picked up her tea. "I'm going to go make sure Benji doesn't order the entire dessert case. Try not to be too disgusting while I'm gone."

Then it was just the two of them, surrounded by the noise of the dining hall, Lukas's tail still warm against Cody's hip.

"So," Lukas said. "How's it feel? Being official."

Cody thought about it. "Weird. Good weird." He looked down at his tray, at the dining hall food that was now technically his dining hall food, at the table where his pack was sitting, at the wolf beside him who'd broken down a door to find him. "Like maybe I'm actually allowed to be here now."

Lukas's paw found his under the table. Squeezed once. "You were always allowed."

---

After lunch they walked. No destination, no hurry, just Cody and Lukas crossing campus in the early afternoon light. Lukas's tail had migrated from Cody's waist to a loose curl around his hip, and their shoulders bumped with every few steps. A few students glanced their way. A raccoon girl waved. Someone from the football team shouted something that was probably congratulations but might have been an innuendo. Lukas flipped him off without breaking stride.

It was halfway across the quad that Cody noticed it.

His brain was quiet.

Not scanning the crowd for threats. Not mapping exits or cataloging faces or running the constant background calculation of where danger might come from and how fast he could get away from it. For the first time in as long as he could remember, his head was just... still.

They rounded the corner by the old oak tree and there it was.

The science building stood gray and silent against the afternoon sky. Yellow tape crossed the main entrance in a bright X. A security officer sat on the steps, scrolling through his phone, looking like he'd rather be anywhere else. And on the double doors, in black letters that caught the light:

**CLOSED**

Cody stopped walking.

Lukas's tail tightened around his hip. "You okay?"

Cody stared at the sign. He thought about fluorescent lights and cold metal and the sound of his own breathing echoing off tile walls. He thought about straps and needles and a snow leopard's patient smile. He thought about all the nights he might have spent in that building if things had gone differently, if his pack hadn't come for him, if Lukas hadn't broken down that door.

Then he looked at the CLOSED sign again. At the bored security guard. At the yellow tape that meant nobody was getting in, that the man who'd wanted to dissect him was sitting in a cell somewhere, that it was over.

"Yeah." He let out a slow breath, felt something loosen in his chest. "I really am."

Lukas bumped his shoulder. "Come on. Benji's buying dinner too."

"He lost the bet. You won." Cody poked Lukas in the ribs.

"And I'm makin' him pay for it all week." Lukas's tail gave a smug little wag.

Cody laughed, and it felt easy, felt real in a way laughing hadn't felt in years. He let Lukas pull him away from the science building, away from the yellow tape and the locked doors and everything they represented.

They walked through campus in the afternoon light, a wolf's tail around his waist and nowhere left to hide.

He didn't need to anymore.
